# Volume 2 — Jazz

*Top 10 Albums of All Time, by Sub-Genre*

---

## On the Volume

Jazz gets its own volume. The first volume in this project covered four root traditions — Blues, Folk, Country, Gospel — and treated them as the river system from which essentially everything in twentieth-century American popular music flows. Jazz is part of that watershed. It is also large enough, internally subdivided enough, and densely enough canonized that putting it inside Volume 1 as a fifth section would have produced a section roughly the size of the other four combined and still given each of its sub-genres half the space they deserve. So jazz becomes Volume 2 on its own. Seventeen sub-genre files, from Dixieland and New Orleans through to Nu Jazz and the contemporary London / Los Angeles spiritual-jazz revival, each adjudicated through the same Scaruffi-versus-Fantano debate process the first volume established and each closing with a coin-flip ledger and a long honorable-mentions tail.

The volume begins where Volume 1 ended. The argument of *The Roots* was that blues, folk, country, and gospel are the deepest layer of the American popular system — the river system — and that everything downstream borrows from one or more of those headwaters. Jazz is one of the first things downstream. The form coalesces in turn-of-the-century New Orleans out of a specific syncretic moment: the city's brass-band tradition (military and Creole funeral bands, the Eureka and Olympia and Excelsior and Onward bands), the blues coming up from the Delta and the Gulf Coast, ragtime's syncopated piano vocabulary moving south out of Sedalia and St. Louis, the Black-church congregational singing that gave the music its emotional register, the spirituals tradition transmitted through Reconstruction-era HBCUs and itinerant choirs, and — substantially less acknowledged in standard histories — the Cuban *contradanza* and *habanera* rhythms moving north through the Gulf trade routes (what Jelly Roll Morton called the "Spanish tinge"). The form that emerged was new, but every component of it was already in the volume the reader has just finished. Jazz is the first downstream tradition that refines the roots material into something the roots themselves did not contain: a music for instrumental virtuosos, organized around improvisation as the central artistic act, on a song-form architecture inherited from the popular-song industry but used as scaffolding for soloistic invention rather than as the song itself.

That refinement is what justifies the volume's length. Within sixty years of Buddy Bolden — the cornetist whose New Orleans bands in the late 1890s are the first documented jazz performers, even though no recording of him survives — the form had run through Dixieland, classic jazz, swing, the small-group bop revolution, cool jazz, hard bop, modal jazz, free jazz, soul jazz, Latin jazz, spiritual jazz, fusion, third stream, ECM chamber jazz, the loft scene, smooth jazz, and the contemporary nu-jazz / future-jazz hybrids. Each of those is a coherent tradition with its own canon, its own apex records, its own internal disputes about what counts and what doesn't. The 17-sub-genre count in this volume is not arbitrary; it reflects the actual taxonomic shape of how serious jazz listeners and serious jazz critics talk about the music. A canon project that collapsed even two of these sub-genres into each other (modal and hard bop, say, or loft and free) would mis-describe the form in ways the form's actual practitioners and historians would refuse.

## Why Jazz Branches the Way It Does

The structural fact about jazz that organizes this volume is that the music branches early and keeps branching. Almost every other tradition in this project has a dominant linear narrative — blues runs from rural pre-war through Chicago electric and out into rock and roll, country runs from the Carter Family through honky-tonk and Bakersfield and outlaw and into modern Americana, gospel runs from spirituals through Dorsey's Chicago axis and out into contemporary urban gospel — and the variants and side-branches can be hung off the main trunk. Jazz does not have a single trunk. The bebop revolution of the mid-1940s splits the form into at least four near-immediate descendants — hard bop (East Coast, Black, blues-and-gospel-saturated), cool jazz (West Coast and Birth-of-the-Cool New York, white-presenting, classically-tinged), modal jazz (Miles, Coltrane, Russell's Lydian Chromatic theoretical framework), and free jazz (Ornette, Cecil Taylor, the Coltrane *Ascension* lineage) — and each of those has its own subsequent branches. By 1970 the form has fusion, soul jazz, spiritual jazz, third stream, ECM's European chamber wing, the loft scene's post-free continuation, and a growing Latin-jazz parallel that had been running since Mario Bauzá's Machito band in the 1940s but only fully crystallizes commercially with the Fania-era Latin-jazz crossover. The branching is not a sign of confusion in the form; it is the form's defining feature. Jazz is a music that, having figured out that the central artistic act is improvisation over harmonic and rhythmic scaffolding, then asked — across every decade — what kind of scaffolding produces what kind of improvisation, and split into schools according to the answers different musicians gave.

This is the kind of taxonomic tension the whole project negotiates, and jazz is where it is most acute. The seventeen sub-genres in this volume are neither perfectly distinct nor perfectly nested. Modal jazz and hard bop overlap in the early-60s Miles Davis quintet sessions; soul jazz and hard bop overlap on most of the Blue Note Jimmy Smith / Lou Donaldson / Stanley Turrentine catalog; spiritual jazz and free jazz overlap on most of the post-1965 Coltrane material and on Pharoah Sanders' Impulse! sides; third stream and modal jazz overlap on the George Russell records; ECM and chamber jazz overlap on most of Manfred Eicher's roster from the early 70s forward; nu jazz and acid jazz and broken beat overlap with the entire late-90s European electronic-jazz hybridization. The cross-listing convention from Volume 1 (an album can anchor in one list and cross-reference in one other) carries over here and gets a heavier workout than it did in the first volume; the overlap is structural to jazz, not incidental.

## Methodology Recap, with a Heavy Caveat

The debate process is the same as Volume 1: independent top-15s from each critic, consensus placements where they overlap, argument over the contested slots, coin flips when the argument cannot move either critic. Coin-flip resolutions are recorded explicitly in each file. Honorable mentions follow. Cross-listings are flagged.

The caveat is large enough that it must be named at the front of the volume rather than buried in a methodology footnote. **The Scaruffi/Fantano matchup is structurally asymmetric on jazz to a degree it was not on the roots traditions.** Scaruffi has a comprehensive jazz canon. He has written long-form essays on every major jazz figure from King Oliver to Vijay Iyer, his ratings system covers the entire recorded history of the music, and his historiographical positions are stable, well-documented, and represented in his publications across decades. He treats jazz as one of the four or five great twentieth-century artistic traditions (alongside the European avant-garde, the American avant-rock canon, classical modernism, and the international art-cinema canon) and writes about it accordingly. Fantano, by contrast, has a real but heavily time-bounded jazz catalog. His on-record jazz reviews are concentrated in the post-2010 spiritual-jazz wave (Kamasi Washington's *The Epic*, the Floating Points / Pharoah Sanders / London Symphony Orchestra *Promises*, Christian Scott aTunde Adjuah, the Robert Glasper Experiment records, the contemporary London scene around Shabaka Hutchings and Sons of Kemet and Nubya Garcia and Moses Sumney-adjacent material, and the more recent Chicago / International Anthem axis around Makaya McCraven, Jeff Parker, Damon Locks, and Angel Bat Dawid). His pre-1990 jazz coverage is sparse — passing references to Coltrane and Miles and Mingus and Monk as obvious giants, occasional reaction-video material on classics like *Kind of Blue*, but nothing that would count as a granular ranking of the early-60s Blue Note hard-bop catalog or the late-50s cool-jazz axis or the entire pre-bop era.

What this means in practice: the older sub-genres in this volume lean Scaruffi-first, with Fantano positions extrapolated from his stated criteria (sonic singularity, harmonic adventurousness, raw expression, a clear preference for Black American vernacular forms, a particular ear for production density and rhythmic complexity, a tolerance for length and intensity in the avant-garde wing) and from his contemporary reviews of records that draw on the older traditions. The extrapolation is flagged the same way it was in Volume 1, with the `(EXTRAPOLATED, FLAGGED)` convention. The newer sub-genres — Nu Jazz, contemporary Avant-Garde / Loft Jazz, the post-2010 portion of Spiritual Jazz and Jazz Fusion — have direct Fantano coverage and the placements there are arrived at the same way the bulk of Volume 1's contemporary entries were. Readers should weight the older placements with the appropriate skepticism. The volume is honest about which positions are direct and which are constructed.

There is a related point worth making. Jazz is the tradition where Scaruffi's voice is most likely to dominate by default not just because of Fantano's coverage gap but because Scaruffi has done the work — the work of ranking, comparing, contextualizing, and writing his way through every era of the form — that almost no other single critic in the English- or Italian-language criticism has done at the same scale. There is a real sense in which the jazz volume is, more than the roots volume was, a Scaruffi canon with Fantano positions argued in where his coverage permits. The editor has tried to push back on Scaruffi's most contrarian positions (his hostility to the cool-jazz wing, his suspicion of fusion's commercial moment, his low rating of certain hard-bop figures) where Fantano's general criteria would plausibly produce a different placement, but the volume is unavoidably Scaruffi-weighted in a way the roots volume was not. Reading it as such — as a strong-canonical jazz canon with a working-critic's contemporary corrective — is the intended frame.

## Reading Instructions

The volume is organized in three parts rather than the four of Volume 1. Jazz is one tradition with internal branches, not four parallel root traditions, and a three-part structure tracks the music's actual development better than a sub-genre-by-sub-genre serial would.

**Part One — Foundations** covers the pre-1965 jazz canon: Dixieland and New Orleans Jazz, Swing and Big Band, Bebop, Cool Jazz, and Hard Bop. This is the linear-history portion of the volume, the arc from collective improvisation in early-twentieth-century New Orleans through the swing era's commercial dominance, the bebop revolution that split the form open, and the immediate post-bop responses (cool's reaction, hard bop's synthesis). Five sub-genres.

**Part Two — Branches** covers the mid-60s through 70s explosion outward: Modal Jazz, Free Jazz, Soul Jazz, Latin Jazz, and Spiritual Jazz. This is where the form stops having a single forward direction and starts branching. Modal pivots to scales, free destroys harmonic hierarchy, soul jazz fuses gospel groove into hard bop, Latin jazz crystallizes the long-running Afro-Cuban parallel, and spiritual jazz turns the form mystically and politically outward. Five sub-genres.

**Part Three — Adjacent and Modern** covers the records that don't fit the linear bop-to-free narrative: Vocal Jazz / Standards, Jazz Fusion, Avant-garde / Loft Jazz, Third Stream, ECM / Chamber Jazz, Smooth Jazz, and Nu Jazz / Future Jazz. The vocalists carrying the standards tradition through the bop revolution and after, fusion's electric collision with rock, the loft scene's post-free continuation in 70s New York, third stream's classical reach, ECM's European chamber aesthetic, smooth jazz's much-maligned commercial branch, and the contemporary nu-jazz / future-jazz / London-revival material. Seven sub-genres.

Each part has its own short introduction. Each sub-genre file is self-contained and can be read on its own. The recommended reading order is the order the volume is bound in — Foundations first, Branches second, Modern third — because the dependencies run that direction (you cannot read the modal-jazz file without the bebop and hard-bop context behind it, you cannot read the loft-jazz file without the free-jazz context, you cannot read the nu-jazz file without all of the above). Reading any individual file in isolation works, but reading the volume in order is what the structure rewards.

A standing scope note. The "album" as a unit of analysis fits jazz unevenly. The pre-bop and bop canons are heavily compilation-based — Charlie Parker's essential work was issued on 78 RPM sides under the AFM recording-ban era, and the canonical "albums" are reissue compilations (the *Complete Savoy and Dial*, the *Verve Master Takes*, the various Blue Note Mosaic boxes). The lists treat the canonical reissue as the artifact under evaluation in those cases, the same way Volume 1 did with the pre-war blues compilations. This is standard practice in jazz scholarship and in both critics' published work. From the late-50s LP era forward, the album-as-conceived-unit is the primary unit and the lists treat it as such. The convention is flagged where it matters.

## Part One — Foundations

Five sub-genres, roughly sixty years. The arc this part covers runs from the first recorded jazz — the Original Dixieland Jass Band's "Livery Stable Blues" cut for Victor in February 1917, a recording whose musical merit is dwarfed by its historical position as the first commercially issued jazz record — through the pre-1965 hard-bop catalog of Blue Note, Prestige, Riverside, and Columbia. Inside that arc the form does almost everything it will ever do at the level of small-group acoustic jazz: it invents collective improvisation in New Orleans, scales it up to the swing-band industrial format in the 1930s, breaks the swing apparatus apart into the small-combo bebop revolution of the mid-1940s, generates a cool-jazz reaction along the West Coast and at certain New York studios that wants the harmonic adventurousness of bop without its emotional intensity, and then synthesizes the whole sequence into hard bop — the post-1954 East Coast Black-jazz mainstream that re-injects blues, gospel, and rhythm-and-blues feel back into bop's harmonic framework and produces what may be, on aggregate, the deepest stretch of recorded music the form has ever generated.

**Dixieland / New Orleans Jazz** is the foundation. The form's documented history begins in turn-of-the-century New Orleans — Buddy Bolden's bands in the 1890s and 1900s, undocumented on record but extensively memorialized in interviews; the post-Bolden generation of King Oliver, Kid Ory, Bunk Johnson, Sidney Bechet, Jelly Roll Morton, and (transitionally) Louis Armstrong, who left New Orleans for Chicago in 1922 to join Oliver's Creole Jazz Band and brought the music's center of gravity north with him. The 1923 Gennett sessions by King Oliver's Creole Jazz Band — the first substantial recordings of authentic New Orleans-tradition Black musicians — are the canonical pre-LP document. The Hot Five and Hot Seven Armstrong sessions of 1925-1928 are where the form crosses over from collective polyphonic improvisation into individual-soloist-driven jazz, a structural shift that defines almost everything that follows. Jelly Roll Morton's Red Hot Peppers sides for Victor in 1926-1928 are the most fully composed and arranged of the canon — Morton's claim to have "invented jazz" is extravagant but the Red Hot Peppers material is the single best demonstration of how the New Orleans collective-improv style could be organized into actual compositions. The Dixieland-revival material from the 1940s onward (the New Orleans Revival proper, plus the Lu Watters / Yerba Buena Jazz Band wing in the Bay Area) gets a smaller share of the file's attention; the canonical pre-1929 recordings are where the artistic peaks are.

**Swing / Big Band** is the form's first commercial dominance. From roughly 1935 (the Benny Goodman Carnegie Hall date and the broader "Swing Era" inauguration) through 1945 (the wartime travel restrictions and AFM recording bans that broke the touring big-band economy), big-band swing was American popular music — not a niche or a subculture, the actual mainstream, the music that played on every radio and at every dance hall and that put Goodman and Glenn Miller and Tommy Dorsey on the cover of magazines. The artistic peaks of the era live in the Black bands the popular history under-emphasizes: Duke Ellington's Cotton Club orchestra and his subsequent 1940-1942 "Blanton-Webster" band (with Jimmy Blanton on bass and Ben Webster on tenor), which produced "Take the A Train," "Cotton Tail," "Ko-Ko," "Concerto for Cootie," and the deepest stretch of orchestral jazz composition the form has ever generated; Count Basie's Kansas City band with Lester Young, Buck Clayton, Herschel Evans, Walter Page, and Jo Jones, which invented the riff-driven blues-saturated swing that would become the rhythmic foundation of bebop; Jimmie Lunceford's organizational rigor and his arranger Sy Oliver's compositional sophistication; Chick Webb's Savoy Ballroom band and the early Ella Fitzgerald sides; Earl Hines's Grand Terrace orchestra, which incubated the early bop generation (Parker, Gillespie, and Sarah Vaughan all passed through). The Ellington material specifically is the part of the file where the rankings are hardest to construct because the Ellington discography is enormous and the canonical "albums" — *The Blanton-Webster Band* box on Bluebird, the *Far East Suite*, *Money Jungle*, the *Sacred Concerts* — are scattered across formats and decades. Both critics rank Ellington as one of the two or three most important figures in twentieth-century jazz; the question is which Ellington records to canonize, not whether to.

**Bebop** is the revolution. The form coalesces in the early-to-mid 1940s in the after-hours jam sessions at Minton's Playhouse in Harlem and Monroe's Uptown House in Sugar Hill — Charlie Parker, Dizzy Gillespie, Thelonious Monk, Kenny Clarke, Charlie Christian, Bud Powell, and the small surrounding group that re-coded what jazz improvisation could do harmonically and rhythmically. The technical content of the revolution is well-documented: the systematic use of upper-structure chord extensions (9ths, 11ths, 13ths) over the standard popular-song changes, the substitution of tritone and altered-dominant harmony for the simpler ii-V-I cadences swing-era jazz had used, the introduction of chromatic passing tones at velocities the previous generation could not play, the relocation of the rhythmic pulse from the bass drum (Webb-era big-band time) to the ride cymbal (Clarke's innovation, then Roach's refinement), and the shift from dance-music tempos to bop's much faster tempos that effectively made the music undanceable and pushed it into the listening-club format. The cultural content of the revolution is equally well-documented and harder to summarize: bebop was the moment Black jazz musicians decided their music was art rather than entertainment, refused the swing era's racial-economic structure (white bandleaders making the money on Black-musical innovations), and built a small-club performance culture that demanded close listening rather than dancing. The Parker recordings (the Savoy/Dial sessions of 1944-1948, the Verve sessions of 1949-1954) are the canonical text. The Powell, Monk, Gillespie, Navarro, and Dexter Gordon material rounds out the file's top ten.

**Cool Jazz** is the immediate reaction. By 1949 — five years into the bop revolution — a small group of mostly West Coast and Birth-of-the-Cool-axis musicians had begun producing a music that took bop's harmonic vocabulary but reduced its emotional intensity, slowed its tempos, foregrounded its compositional and arrangement work, and drew on classical-music orchestration in ways bop had not. Miles Davis's *Birth of the Cool* sessions (1949-1950, released as an LP in 1957) with Gerry Mulligan, Lee Konitz, Gil Evans, John Lewis, and Max Roach are the foundational document. The subsequent West Coast wing — Mulligan's pianoless quartet with Chet Baker, the Modern Jazz Quartet's classically-tinged chamber jazz, Dave Brubeck's quartet with Paul Desmond, Stan Getz's Lighthouse Café records and his subsequent bossa-nova crossover with João Gilberto, Lennie Tristano's New York-based but cool-aligned harmonic-experimental wing — produces the bulk of the file. The cool jazz file is where the racial politics of postwar jazz get most uncomfortable: the form is whiter, more academically oriented, and more commercially successful in its day than the contemporaneous hard-bop wing was, and a substantial historiographic argument (which Scaruffi partially endorses, which Fantano-extrapolated does not) treats the cool wing as a derivative refinement of bop's innovations by a whiter musician class that benefited from the racial economics. The file works through the argument explicitly rather than around it.

**Hard Bop** is the synthesis. From roughly 1954 (the Art Blakey / Horace Silver Jazz Messengers' formation, the Clifford Brown / Max Roach quintet's first sides) forward through the late 1960s, the East Coast Black jazz mainstream re-injects blues, gospel, and rhythm-and-blues feel into bop's harmonic framework and produces what is, by aggregate, the densest stretch of recorded music the form has ever generated. The Blue Note catalog under Alfred Lion and Francis Wolff is the central document — the Horace Silver records, the Art Blakey Jazz Messengers' decades-long succession of bands, the Lee Morgan / Wayne Shorter / Donald Byrd / Hank Mobley / Freddie Hubbard / Booker Little / Joe Henderson / Andrew Hill / Bobby Hutcherson catalog, the Sonny Clark sessions, the Jackie McLean records that bridge into modal and free territory. Prestige, Riverside, and Columbia round out the major-label hard-bop infrastructure. The form's emotional center is the gospel-and-blues groove (the "funky" or "soulful" hard-bop sub-current that produced "The Sermon," "Moanin'," "Song for My Father," and the entire soul-jazz wing that gets its own file later in the volume), but the form's compositional ceiling is much higher than the funky-hard-bop reputation suggests — the Wayne Shorter compositions of the mid-60s Blue Notes (*Speak No Evil*, *Adam's Apple*, *Schizophrenia*, *Etcetera*) are some of the most harmonically sophisticated small-group jazz writing in the recorded canon. Hard bop is also where the form starts to feel, in retrospect, like a settled mainstream — by 1965 the Blue Note hard-bop sound was the default setting of jazz, and the avant-garde, modal, and free responses that follow in Part Two of this volume are partly responses to that very settledness.

Five sub-genres. The arc they describe is the form's classical period — the sixty years during which jazz was both the central American art-music and (through swing's commercial dominance) the central American popular music. Reading them in order is the right way through; the dependencies run forward (you cannot read the bebop file without the swing context, you cannot read the hard-bop file without the bebop context) and the cross-references are dense.

---

### Dixieland / New Orleans Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

This is the most lopsided pairing in the entire jazz volume. Scaruffi has written the canonical European-critic history of early jazz; he treats Louis Armstrong's Hot Fives and Sevens as the literal big bang of recorded improvisation, ranks Jelly Roll Morton among the first true composer-arrangers in the music, and gives Sidney Bechet and King Oliver substantial individual entries on scaruffi.com. He is dismissive of most of the white Chicago and revival scenes — Bix Beiderbecke gets respectful but cooler treatment, the ODJB is acknowledged historically rather than musically, and the Preservation Hall / Marsalis revival lineage gets virtually nothing. Fantano, by contrast, has functionally zero published commentary on pre-bebop jazz. His "best jazz albums" lists begin with *Kind of Blue* and *A Love Supreme*; everything before 1955 is a black hole. Every Fantano position in this debate is **EXTRAPOLATED** from his stated values: rawness over polish, Black originators over white revivalists, primary documents over later restatements, and a strong allergy to museum-piece reverence.

That extrapolation actually narrows the gap. Fantano-by-extrapolation would converge with Scaruffi on the top tier — Armstrong, Morton, Oliver, Bechet — because those are exactly the records a hip-hop-trained ear would still hear as "the source." The fights are at the edges. Scaruffi favors Beiderbecke as a genuine harmonic innovator (the "white Armstrong" framing he both uses and complicates); Fantano-extrapolated would be wary of a romanticized white cornetist eclipsing Black contemporaries. Scaruffi treats the New Orleans Revival (Bunk Johnson, George Lewis) as historically interesting but artistically minor; Fantano-extrapolated would likely agree but for opposite reasons — not "not innovative enough" but "too tourist." Both would push the ODJB down on grounds of being first-to-record rather than first-to-create. Wynton Marsalis's neoclassical New Orleans projects are the one place Scaruffi is openly hostile and Fantano-extrapolated would be skeptical-to-neutral, which means Wynton lands in honorable mentions despite his real importance to the tradition's survival.

Coin flips were needed for three slots: Beiderbecke vs Bechet ordering inside the top five, the Eddie Condon Chicago slot vs a second Bechet compilation, and whether Kid Ory's *Creole Jazz Band 1954-1956* edges Preservation Hall. The pre-1940 compilation problem dominates the format: nine of these ten "albums" are posthumous compilations of 78s, and the canonical issues (Columbia Legacy, JSP, Mosaic, RCA Bluebird) are cited because the original 10-inch shellac doesn't translate to a "best albums" list in any honest way.

A meta-note on Fantano extrapolation: the methodology here is to take the documented Fantano value system — preference for primary sources over derivatives, suspicion of canon as institutional consensus, willingness to reward emotional directness over technical sophistication, and an explicit anti-revivalist instinct that comes from his hardcore-punk background — and apply those values to records he has demonstrably never reviewed. This is not the same as claiming Fantano would actually rank these records this way; it's a structured guess. Where the guess is especially uncertain (the Bix Beiderbecke slot, the post-1929 Armstrong slot), I have **FLAGGED** it explicitly and resolved by coin flip rather than by pretending to know.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Louis Armstrong & His Hot Five / Hot Seven — *The Complete Hot Five and Hot Seven Recordings* (Columbia/Legacy, 2000; recorded 1925-1928)

Both critics — to the extent Fantano can be said to hold a position here — agree this is the foundational document of recorded jazz, and arguably of recorded American music. Scaruffi's site ranks the Hot Fives and Sevens above almost anything else in the prewar catalog; he explicitly credits Armstrong with inventing the soloist as the center of jazz, displacing the New Orleans collective polyphony that produced him. The 4-CD Columbia/Legacy box (or the equivalent JSP set) collects every session from November 1925 through December 1928, moving from the first Hot Five sides ("Heebie Jeebies," "Cornet Chop Suey," "Muskrat Ramble") through the Hot Seven expansion ("Potato Head Blues," "Wild Man Blues," "Alligator Crawl") to the late masterpieces with Earl Hines on piano ("West End Blues," "Weather Bird," "Tight Like This").

The "West End Blues" cadenza alone — twelve seconds of unaccompanied trumpet that opens the 1928 recording — is the moment a folk music becomes an art music. Gunther Schuller's analysis in *Early Jazz* describes those twelve seconds as the line that separates ragtime-derived ensemble music from jazz-as-art, and that judgment has held up for sixty years. Armstrong's phrasing extends and contracts the beat in ways no recorded improviser had attempted; his vocal on "Heebie Jeebies" introduces scat singing to the world (the apocryphal story is that he dropped the lyric sheet during the take and improvised syllables; the truer story is that scat already existed and Armstrong simply made the first commercial recording of it); the Hines duet on "Weather Bird" is the first piano-trumpet conversation in recorded jazz, with Hines's trumpet-style right hand answering Armstrong measure for measure. Scaruffi treats this as the literal origin point of jazz as composer-music-for-improvisers. Fantano-extrapolated would defer to historical consensus and the obvious vitality of the playing; nothing in his stated taste would push him to dissent. The only debate is which compilation to cite, and Columbia/Legacy's box wins on completeness, sound restoration by Phil Schaap (controversial in audiophile circles, but standard), and the inclusion of alternate takes that show Armstrong revising his own breakthroughs in real time. Unanimous #1.

##### 2. Jelly Roll Morton & His Red Hot Peppers — *The Complete Victor Recordings* (RCA Bluebird, 1997; recorded 1926-1930)

Scaruffi rates Morton as the first jazz composer in any meaningful sense — the first musician to write down the music as deliberate orchestration rather than transcribed head arrangement. The Red Hot Peppers sessions, recorded in Chicago between September 1926 and 1930, are the proof. "Black Bottom Stomp," "Grandpa's Spells," "The Chant," "Dead Man Blues," "Jungle Blues," "Smoke-House Blues" — these are tightly composed three-minute structures that braid solo and ensemble, written breaks and improvised fills, into something neither King Oliver's collective polyphony nor Armstrong's soloist-centric model could produce on its own. Morton is the bridge between New Orleans group music and the swing-era arranger.

The 5-CD Bluebird box collects every Red Hot Peppers Victor session plus the solo piano sides and the New Orleans Rhythm Kings dates. Sound is variable — these are 1926-1930 acoustic and early electric recordings — but RCA's restoration is the standard reference. The personnel rotated but consistently included Omer Simeon or Johnny Dodds on clarinet, Kid Ory or Geechie Fields on trombone, George Mitchell on cornet, and Andrew Hilaire or Baby Dodds on drums; the rhythm sections are looser than Oliver's and tighter than the contemporaneous Fletcher Henderson groups, which is exactly the point — Morton was triangulating between collective improvisation and arranged ensemble in a way no one else attempted in the period. Scaruffi gives Morton a long entry on his site treating him as a composer first and a pianist second, which is the right frame; Morton's claim to have "invented jazz in 1902" is self-aggrandizing nonsense, but his claim to have first formalized it is defensible. Fantano-extrapolated would respond to the discipline and structural intelligence of the writing — Morton is the prewar figure whose music most resembles what a contemporary listener thinks of as "composition" rather than "recorded performance." Both critics land him at #2 without much argument, though Scaruffi might privately argue for #1 if Armstrong didn't exist.

##### 3. King Oliver's Creole Jazz Band — *Off the Record: The Complete 1923 Jazz Band Recordings* (Archeophone, 2006)

Before Armstrong was Armstrong, he was the second cornet in King Oliver's Creole Jazz Band. The 1923 sessions for Gennett, OKeh, Columbia, and Paramount — collected on the Archeophone double-CD with the most aggressive sonic restoration anyone has ever attempted on this material — are the purest surviving document of New Orleans collective improvisation. Three horns (Oliver and Armstrong on cornets, Honore Dutrey on trombone, Johnny Dodds on clarinet) weave continuous polyphonic lines over Lil Hardin's piano, Bill Johnson or Bud Scott on banjo, and Baby Dodds's drums. There are no real solos in the post-Armstrong sense; the music is conversation rather than declamation.

Scaruffi treats Oliver as the last great exponent of the pre-soloist New Orleans style and Armstrong's apprenticeship in this band as the moment the soloist was about to be invented. "Dipper Mouth Blues," "Snake Rag," "Mabel's Dream," "Chimes Blues" (which contains Armstrong's first recorded solo, sixteen tentative bars), and "Riverside Blues" are the canonical sides. The Archeophone restoration is the audiophile choice; Off the Record uses copies of the original metal masters where they survive and superior shellac transfers where they don't, which makes the difference between hearing the music and reading about it. Fantano-extrapolated has no horse in this race but would respond to the obvious primacy of the document — this is the source code. Both critics agree on #3, though Scaruffi might rank Oliver above Morton on purely historical grounds and is talked out of it by the compositional weight of the Red Hot Peppers.

##### 4. Sidney Bechet — *The Best of Sidney Bechet* (Blue Note, 1994; recorded 1939-1953)

Bechet is the third figure in the New Orleans pantheon and the only one whose virtuosity rivals Armstrong's. He recorded with the Original Dixieland Jazz Band's contemporaries, then with Clarence Williams in the 1920s, then disappeared into European obscurity, then re-emerged in the late 1930s and recorded the Blue Note sessions that constitute his definitive statement. The Blue Note compilation collects "Summertime" (1939, his commercial breakthrough), "Blue Horizon" (1944, six minutes of slow blues clarinet that is among the most emotionally direct performances in jazz), "Dear Old Southland," "Royal Garden Blues," and the soprano saxophone features that established that horn as a serious jazz instrument decades before Coltrane.

Scaruffi gives Bechet a substantial entry treating him as Armstrong's only true peer in the pre-swing era — a soloist of comparable invention but with a vibrato-heavy, vocally inflected approach that pointed toward a different jazz future than the one Armstrong actually produced. Bechet's later French recordings (1949-1959) made him a national celebrity in France; his "Petite Fleur" was a European hit. Fantano-extrapolated would respond to "Blue Horizon" specifically — the kind of slow, exposed, emotionally unguarded performance that translates across genre boundaries to anyone with ears. The Blue Note compilation is preferred over the Mosaic complete-sessions box for accessibility; the Mosaic set is the completist choice but the Blue Note "Best of" is what a person should actually own. #4 unanimous.

##### 5. Bix Beiderbecke — *Bix Beiderbecke, Vol. 1: Singin' the Blues* (Columbia/Legacy, 1990; recorded 1927)

The first real disagreement. Scaruffi rates Beiderbecke as a genuine harmonic innovator — the cornetist who heard Debussy and Ravel and tried to import their chromaticism into jazz, decades before bebop made that move standard. The 1927 Frankie Trumbauer sessions ("Singin' the Blues," "I'm Coming Virginia," "Way Down Yonder in New Orleans") feature Beiderbecke solos that move horizontally across changes in ways Armstrong did not, with a cool, bell-clear tone that became the template for every white cornetist for the next thirty years. "Singin' the Blues" in particular is one of the most-imitated solos in jazz history; Lester Young learned it note-for-note.

Fantano-extrapolated is wary. The Beiderbecke myth — tragic white prodigy dead at 28, romanticized by Dorothy Baker and Hollywood — is exactly the kind of revisionist canon-building that distorts who actually invented what. A coin flip was needed: should Beiderbecke be in the top five at all, or should a second Bechet compilation take this slot? The flip went to Bix on the grounds that his harmonic language genuinely did influence the music's evolution in ways that pure Black-originator Bechet appreciation can obscure. The Columbia/Legacy volume covers the 1927 Trumbauer and Beiderbecke-led sessions; the later Whiteman orchestra material (where Bix is buried in a commercial dance band) is correctly omitted. Scaruffi at #4-5, Fantano-extrapolated at #7-8, settle at #5.

##### 6. Louis Armstrong — *The Complete RCA Victor Recordings* (RCA, 2001; recorded 1932-1933, 1946-1947)

The second Armstrong entry — and the one Scaruffi would not include but Fantano-extrapolated argues for. After the Hot Sevens, Armstrong moved to larger ensembles and pop material; the early 1930s RCA Victor sides ("Stardust," "Body and Soul," "Lazy River," "Sweethearts on Parade," "Star Dust" in two takes) show him reinventing himself as the first great jazz vocalist and pop interpreter while still playing trumpet at the highest level. The 1946-1947 Esquire All-Star sessions and the All Stars material round out the box.

Scaruffi treats post-1929 Armstrong as a commercial decline — the great soloist becoming an entertainer, the music becoming pop. Fantano-extrapolated, working from values shaped by hip-hop's omnivorous treatment of "high" and "low" material, would reject that frame entirely; the 1932 "Stardust" is one of the great vocal performances of the century, and the trumpet work on the early 30s sides is more harmonically sophisticated than the Hot Sevens, just less raw. The argument is real and the coin flip was needed; the resolution is to include this set at #6 over Scaruffi's objection on the grounds that any Dixieland list that pretends Armstrong stopped mattering in 1928 is lying. The RCA box is preferred to the Decca material because the playing is hotter and the arrangements less syrupy; the Deccas appear in honorable mentions.

##### 7. Original Dixieland Jass Band — *The 75th Anniversary* (RCA Bluebird, 1992; recorded 1917-1921)

The historically necessary inclusion that neither critic loves. The ODJB cut "Livery Stable Blues" / "Dixie Jass Band One Step" in February 1917 — the first jazz records ever commercially released, by a white New Orleans band led by cornetist Nick LaRocca that frankly stole most of its book from the Black bands LaRocca had heard in the city. The music itself is novelty-heavy: barnyard imitations, two-beat polkas, white minstrel-show energy. But the document matters; the ODJB sides are why jazz spread to Chicago, New York, and London in 1917-1920 instead of 1925, and they're the first evidence of what New Orleans ensemble playing sounded like to anyone who wasn't physically in New Orleans.

Scaruffi treats the ODJB as a historical footnote — first to record is not first to create — and would rank them outside the top ten on artistic grounds. Fantano-extrapolated would be even harsher on the white-priority issue and the minstrel residue. But both would concede that any "top 10 Dixieland albums" list that omits the literal first jazz records is intellectually dishonest. The RCA Bluebird 75th Anniversary collection is the standard issue, with reasonable transfers and the full early Victor and Aeolian Vocalion catalog. #7 by historical necessity, with both critics noting that the next three slots are all artistically superior.

##### 8. Kid Ory's Creole Jazz Band — *Kid Ory's Creole Jazz Band 1954-1956* (Good Time Jazz / OJC, reissued 1991)

The Dixieland revival's best record. Edward "Kid" Ory was the original trombonist on Armstrong's Hot Five sessions and one of the architects of the New Orleans tailgate trombone style; after a decade running a chicken farm in California, he returned to music in the 1940s and led a band through the 1950s that included veterans (Alvin Alcorn or Teddy Buckner on trumpet, George Probert on clarinet) playing the New Orleans book with the relaxed authority of musicians who had lived inside it. The Good Time Jazz sessions from 1954-1956 are the definitive document — better recorded than the prewar originals, played by surviving originators rather than revivalists, and free of the museum atmosphere that infects most postwar New Orleans recordings.

Scaruffi is generally hostile to the New Orleans Revival but acknowledges Ory as a genuine link to the original tradition rather than an antiquarian. Fantano-extrapolated would respond to the looseness of the playing — these are not careful reconstructions but working-band performances by people for whom this was still living music. The repertoire ("Muskrat Ramble," which Ory wrote, "Tiger Rag," "South," "Maryland My Maryland," "Original Dixieland One-Step") is canon. Coin flip was needed against Preservation Hall for this slot; Ory wins because he is a primary source and Preservation Hall, however valuable, is institutional preservation rather than living tradition. #8 with mild disagreement on placement.

##### 9. Bunk Johnson & George Lewis — *Bunk Johnson and His New Orleans Band* (American Music / Smithsonian Folkways, recorded 1942-1945)

The New Orleans Revival's founding document. Willie "Bunk" Johnson was a pre-1920 New Orleans trumpeter who'd dropped out of music for two decades; he was rediscovered in the early 1940s by collector-evangelists Bill Russell and Gene Williams, fitted with new teeth, and recorded with George Lewis on clarinet, Jim Robinson on trombone, and a rhythm section that played the New Orleans book as it had sounded before the music left the city. The American Music sessions (later issued by Smithsonian Folkways) are unvarnished: out-of-tune horns, ragged ensembles, and an authority of phrasing that no later revival ever matched.

Scaruffi treats the Bunk Johnson revival as a curiosity rather than a major contribution to the music's evolution — the playing is technically rougher than the prewar originals, and the project's premise (that Johnson represented a "purer" pre-Armstrong New Orleans) is partly Russell's romantic fiction. Fantano-extrapolated would be split: the documentary value is real and the performances have a punk-rock unguardedness that contemporary ears can hear, but the museum framing is exactly what Fantano dislikes about jazz reverence culture. The compromise is to include the record at #9 as the best example of revival-as-anthropology, with the caveat that George Lewis's later solo and small-group records (on Blue Note and Delmark) are arguably more musically rewarding. The Smithsonian Folkways issue is the canonical reference.

##### 10. Sidney Bechet & Muggsy Spanier — *The Big Four* (RCA, recorded 1940; various reissues)

The last slot was contested between this Bechet-Spanier quartet, the Eddie Condon Chicago material, and Preservation Hall. The Big Four sessions — Bechet on soprano and clarinet, Muggsy Spanier on cornet, Carmen Mastren on guitar, Wellman Braud on bass, with no piano and no drums — are an unusual format that strips New Orleans music to its melodic essentials. "Squeeze Me," "China Boy," "Sweet Sue," and "That's a Plenty" are played with a chamber-jazz intimacy that anticipates the small-group cool jazz of a decade later.

Scaruffi rates the Big Four sessions highly as evidence of Bechet's adaptability and as a bridge between New Orleans polyphony and the chamber-jazz formats that would emerge with Lennie Tristano and Jimmy Giuffre. Spanier is the underrated figure here — a white Chicago cornetist who'd absorbed Armstrong without imitating him, and who matches Bechet's intensity without trying to overpower it. The recording quality on the original 1940 RCA sides is the best of any prewar Bechet; you can hear the breath and the reed in a way the OKeh and Vocalion sides never permitted, and the absence of rhythm section means the ear can follow the contrapuntal lines the two horns are weaving without the clutter of stride piano or banjo accompaniment. Fantano-extrapolated would respond to the format — the absence of drums and piano forces the horns to carry the music's rhythm and harmony simultaneously, which is the kind of constraint-driven creativity contemporary listeners value. The coin flip went to the Big Four over Eddie Condon's *Dixieland Jam* (Columbia, 1957) on the grounds that Condon's Chicago crew is a parallel tradition rather than a New Orleans one strictly speaking, and over Preservation Hall on the grounds that institutional preservation is not the same thing as a recording session. The Big Four has been reissued in various RCA and JSP packages; the Mosaic Bechet box is the audiophile reference but any reasonable RCA compilation will do. #10.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three positions required coin flips because no critical consensus could be honestly extrapolated. **Slot 5 (Beiderbecke vs second Bechet):** flipped to Beiderbecke because his harmonic influence on what jazz became is too large to relegate to honorable mentions, even granting Fantano-extrapolated's discomfort with the white-prodigy myth. The losing alternative was a second Bechet compilation focused on the European 1949-1959 sides, which would have given Bechet two of the top five slots; that would have been more historically honest in some ways but would have crowded out a genuinely distinct musical voice. **Slot 6 (later Armstrong vs second Morton):** flipped to the RCA Victor Armstrong because Fantano-extrapolated's pop-omnivore instincts refuse to accept the Scaruffi premise that Armstrong declined after 1929; the Morton Library of Congress sessions appear in honorable mentions instead. The Library of Congress recordings are arguably the more important historical artifact, but they are interviews-with-piano-demonstrations rather than studio music in any conventional sense, and including them would have stretched the "album" format past breaking. **Slot 8 (Kid Ory vs Preservation Hall):** flipped to Ory because primary-source revivalists outrank institutional preservation, with the loser appearing in honorable mentions. The argument for Preservation Hall was that its 1963 Atlantic recording captures musicians who were closer in age to the original generation than Ory's California sidemen were; the argument against was that the Preservation Hall project was always partly tourism, and Ory's records are working-band documents rather than ceremonial ones.

A fourth flip was nearly required for slot 10 between the Bechet-Spanier Big Four and Eddie Condon's Chicago Dixieland; Condon lost on the genre-purity argument that Chicago Dixieland is a separate tradition deserving its own list rather than a slot on a New Orleans list. A fifth near-flip involved whether to include any Wynton Marsalis at all in the top ten; the answer was no, on grounds that even *The Majesty of the Blues* is a contemporary-jazz record about New Orleans rather than a New Orleans record, and the brief was for the tradition itself rather than for tributes to it.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Eddie Condon — *Dixieland Jam*** (Columbia, 1957). The Chicago Dixieland school's best single record; Condon's rhythm guitar anchoring trumpet (Wild Bill Davison), trombone (Cutty Cutshall), and clarinet (Edmond Hall) through standards played with after-hours looseness.
- **Preservation Hall Jazz Band — *New Orleans, Vol. I*** (Atlantic, 1963). The institutional document. Slow, ceremonial, important to the music's survival in its native city; not the strongest record on this list but the most loved.
- **Wynton Marsalis — *The Majesty of the Blues*** (Columbia, 1989). Marsalis's most defensible New Orleans project, with Reverend Jeremiah Wright's sermon "Premature Autopsies" framing Wynton's own playing in genuine historical conversation rather than pure neoclassical recital.
- **Jelly Roll Morton — *The Library of Congress Recordings*** (Rounder, 8 CDs, 2005). Alan Lomax's 1938 interviews — Morton talking, demonstrating, and playing solo for hours. Less an album than a primary historical document; essential for anyone who cares about the music's origin.
- **Louis Armstrong — *The Complete Decca Studio Master Takes 1935-1939*** (Definitive). The post-RCA, pre-All Stars Decca material; sweeter and more pop-oriented than the RCA box but with consistently strong trumpet playing.
- **Sidney Bechet — *The Complete Blue Note Recordings*** (Mosaic, 1990, OOP). The completist's choice for Bechet; the Blue Note "Best of" in slot 4 is what most listeners should own, but the Mosaic box is the reference document.
- **George Lewis — *George Lewis with Kid Shots*** (American Music, 1944). Lewis's clarinet in a more focused small-group setting than the Bunk Johnson sessions; the better musical document of the revival even if Bunk is the more historically important one.
- **Original Memphis Five / New Orleans Rhythm Kings — various compilations**. The white New Orleans-influenced bands of the late 1910s and early 1920s; collectively they're the missing link between the ODJB and the Chicago school, but no single compilation makes the top ten on its own.
- **Johnny Dodds — *Blue Clarinet Stomp*** (RCA Bluebird). The Hot Five clarinetist's own sessions; an essential supplement to the Armstrong material rather than a standalone top-ten record.
- **Bix Beiderbecke — *Bix Restored, Vols. 1-5*** (Sunbeam). The completist alternative to the Columbia/Legacy *Singin' the Blues* compilation; collector-grade transfers covering every surviving Beiderbecke side, including the Wolverine Orchestra material that predates the Trumbauer sessions and the Whiteman dance-band sides where Bix is buried but audible.
- **Various Artists — *The Sound of New Orleans 1917-1947*** (Columbia, 3 CDs). The best single-purchase overview of the city's recorded output across three decades; useful as orientation before committing to any of the above single-artist boxes.

---

### Swing / Big Band
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Two critics walk into the swing era and only one of them actually lives there. Piero Scaruffi treats Duke Ellington as one of the half-dozen great American composers of the twentieth century — full stop, no jazz qualifier needed — and gives Basie, Lunceford, and Fletcher Henderson serious structural respect as orchestrators and bandleaders. Fantano, by contrast, has effectively zero on-record commentary about pre-bop big band jazz. His Best Ever Albums of the 1950s ventures barely touch swing, and his pre-1950 commentary is essentially nonexistent. **EXTRAPOLATION FLAG**: every Fantano position below is inferred from his stated values — formal ambition, rhythmic risk, Black avant-garde lineage, suspicion of nostalgia, preference for composers over personality bands — applied to swing-era output. Treat his "votes" as a reasonable projection, not a quote.

The debate broke into four real arguments. **First**: how much Ellington is too much Ellington? Scaruffi wanted four Ellington records in the top ten, including two late-period suites; projected-Fantano pushed back, arguing canon-stuffing one bandleader misrepresents the era's plurality. We compromised at three Ellington-led entries (one of which, *Money Jungle*, is small-group). **Second**: Glenn Miller vs. Artie Shaw. Scaruffi loathes Miller as a sentimental bandleader who flattened swing into pop; projected-Fantano agrees — both wanted Miller out, Shaw in. Easy cut. **Third**: Stan Kenton — Scaruffi grudgingly respects the harmonic ambition of *City of Glass*; projected-Fantano (who tolerates pretension when it actually pushes form) agrees Kenton's avant-side belongs over his pop-side. **Fourth**: Basie's count. Scaruffi pushed for both the 1930s Decca-era Basie and the 1950s Atomic-era Basie; projected-Fantano argued the Atomic band is the more compositionally interesting unit (Neal Hefti's charts as proto-arranger-as-auteur). We held one Basie, the late one, and put the early Basie in honorables.

Three coin-flips were needed: Lunceford vs. Henderson for the orchestrator slot, Chick Webb vs. Cab Calloway for the showman slot, and Woody Herman's First Herd vs. Boyd Raeburn for the avant-curio slot. Resolutions noted below.

A fifth argument that didn't reach the cutting-room floor but shaped the framing throughout: how much weight should "compilation" entries get in a top-ten list when most of swing-era output predates the LP as a coherent artistic unit? The 78 rpm single was the working format from roughly 1925 through 1948, and the canonical jazz LPs from the swing-era catalog are almost entirely posthumous compilations curated decades after the fact. Scaruffi treats this as a non-issue — a great body of work is great regardless of its packaging — while projected-Fantano (whose Anthony Fantano persona is built around the album as artistic statement) is in principle more skeptical. We resolved this in favor of Scaruffi's framing, with the note that several entries are explicitly compilations and one (*Ellington at Newport*) is a live album that became canon partly because it happened to land at the moment the LP became the dominant jazz format. The honorable mentions tilt slightly more toward "great compilations of working bands" to balance.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Duke Ellington & His Orchestra — *Ellington at Newport* (1956)

Both critics, in their actual and projected forms, agreed this had to anchor the list, though for different reasons. Scaruffi places it in the Ellington pantheon as the moment a fading bandleader publicly reasserted himself as the most important composer in jazz; the seven-minute Paul Gonsalves solo on "Diminuendo and Crescendo in Blue" is the canonical example of swing-era big band logic surviving and thriving into the bebop and hard-bop era. The album re-bridged Ellington's 1930s book and his late-suite ambitions, and it did so in front of a Newport crowd that, on tape, sounds like it is being physically lifted out of its chairs.

Projected-Fantano's read centers on rhythmic risk: a 1956 audience expected polite revival; what they got was a band committing to a structure that dares the soloist to keep playing through twenty-seven blues choruses without losing the form. That is a more demanding architectural feat than most of what bebop's supposed avant-garde was attempting in the same year. The 1999 Columbia/Legacy reissue with the restored sound (and with the studio-overdub corrections undone) is the canonical version, and it matters — the originally released album was a partial fabrication, and the truth-restored edition is the one that documents what actually happened on stage.

Where it sits in the swing argument: this is the record that proves big band wasn't a museum piece in the Coltrane decade. Ellington is composing, the band is reading the chart, and Gonsalves is improvising inside the chart. All three things at once. The list begins here because everything below either descends from this or reacts against it. The supporting personnel matters too — Johnny Hodges back in the band after his late-40s departure, Harry Carney still anchoring the baritone chair he held for forty-plus years, Ray Nance and Cat Anderson on trumpets, Sam Woodyard pushing the rhythm section harder than the late-Sonny-Greer band had ever been pushed. The orchestra Ellington brought to Newport was a different unit from the Blanton-Webster band but it could play with comparable authority, and the Newport date is the night that became indisputable.

##### 2. Duke Ellington — *The Far East Suite* (1967)

Scaruffi's #1 if it were up to him alone — he calls the late suites Ellington's most important compositional work, and *Far East Suite* the most coherent of them. Built from impressions of a 1963 State Department tour through the Middle East and South Asia, the suite is collaborative with Billy Strayhorn (his last major project before his death) and treats the orchestra as a chamber ensemble that happens to contain a jazz rhythm section. "Isfahan" is the most famous movement and one of the half-dozen most beautiful things Ellington ever wrote; "Ad Lib on Nippon" is the most formally adventurous, with Jimmy Hamilton's clarinet writing extended cadenzas over a quasi-Japanese pentatonic frame.

Projected-Fantano's pushback: this is where Ellington stops sounding like a swing-era bandleader and starts sounding like a third-stream composer working with a jazz vocabulary. That's a feature, not a bug, but it complicates the "swing/big band" framing. We kept it because the personnel, the section voicings, and the rhythmic feel are direct descendants of the 1940s Blanton-Webster band, even when the harmonic vocabulary has moved into territory Stravinsky and Debussy might recognize.

The album rewards repeat listening more than almost any other entry on this list. The first pass sounds pretty; the third pass reveals how ruthlessly Ellington and Strayhorn voice-lead through the inner sections of the orchestra. There is no swing-era record that is more compositionally dense, and very few jazz records of any era that match it. The 1995 Bluebird/RCA reissue with the bonus alternate takes is the version to seek out — the alternates on "Mount Harissa" and "Blue Pepper" reveal the band working out section balances that the master takes resolve into apparent inevitability. Strayhorn's contribution is impossible to extract cleanly from Ellington's because the two of them had been writing in close enough collaboration for thirty years that their hands are indistinguishable on most pages of the manuscript, but "Isfahan" is a Strayhorn composition through and through, and it sits in the same body of work as "Lush Life" and "Chelsea Bridge" — the most harmonically refined ballad writing in the jazz tradition.

##### 3. Count Basie & His Orchestra — *The Atomic Mr. Basie* (1958)

The late Basie band, with Neal Hefti as principal arranger, distilled swing-era big band into something colder, sharper, and more architecturally precise than anything from the original Kansas City unit. "Splanky," "Whirly-Bird," and "Lil' Darlin'" are essentially three different theses on what a 1958 big band could be: a riff machine, a brass-heavy bop chart, and a slow ballad that requires the band to play quieter than any swing-era band had ever needed to. Hefti's voicings — close, clipped, with the saxes treated as a single sustained pad — are the template for nearly every studio big band that followed, from Quincy Jones's small-group writing through the Thad Jones/Mel Lewis Orchestra.

Scaruffi gives this Basie the edge over the 1930s band because the compositions are more sophisticated and the ensemble execution is more disciplined. Projected-Fantano agrees on different grounds: the Hefti charts are the swing-era equivalent of producer-driven records, where the arranger functions as auteur and the bandleader functions as performer of the arranger's vision. That model — composer/arranger as primary creative force, bandleader as channel — is one Fantano routinely defends in other contexts, and it makes the Atomic band a more interesting specimen than the looser Decca Basie.

The Roulette stereo recording is also an artifact: this is one of the first big band records made for hi-fi as a primary delivery medium, and the spatial imaging shapes the writing. The album sounds like the room. Soloists on the date include Thad Jones (trumpet), Frank Foster and Frank Wess (tenors), and Sonny Payne (drums) — a generation younger than the original Basie sidemen, and a generation more bop-literate. The synthesis is what makes the record work: Hefti is writing for a band whose members can play bop changes inside swing time, and the result has a poise that neither pure-swing nor pure-bop ensembles ever quite achieved.

##### 4. Duke Ellington / Charles Mingus / Max Roach — *Money Jungle* (1962)

Borderline case for a big band list — it is a piano trio — but it was the consensus argument-winner for the slot of "small-group Ellington record that proves the composer was bigger than the orchestra." Ellington at sixty-three sits down with the two most aggressive rhythm-section players of the post-bop generation, and the result is the most physically violent record he ever made. Mingus reportedly tried to walk out of the session. Roach is playing as hard as he plays anywhere on tape. Ellington is, somehow, more rhythmically extreme than either of them.

Scaruffi loves this record because it shows Ellington could have been a free-jazz composer if he had wanted to be. Projected-Fantano loves it because it scans as a 1962 hardcore record played by people in their fifties and forties: dense, percussive, melodically jagged, refusing to settle into groove. "Wig Wise" and "Money Jungle" itself are in the avant tradition more than the swing one, but the harmonic language is unmistakably Ellington — augmented fifths, half-diminished resolutions, the late-period habit of leaving chords unresolved for entire phrases.

It is on this list because Ellington's swing-era thinking is fully audible in a non-orchestra context, and that data point matters: the great composer was the composer, regardless of the ensemble size. The session is also a useful corrective to any reading of Ellington as a man-of-his-era figure who got left behind by bop and post-bop. Confronted with Mingus and Roach — both of whom had openly criticized older players for not keeping up — Ellington outplayed them, in their own rhythmic vocabulary, on their preferred terms, in three hours in a New York studio.

##### 5. Count Basie & His Orchestra — *The Original American Decca Recordings* (recorded 1937–39, comp. 1992)

The Kansas City Basie band as it actually existed when Lester Young was the lead tenor, Jo Jones was the rhythmic engine, and the whole unit operated on the head-arrangement, riff-and-soloist principle that defined Southwest swing as a regional style distinct from the New York bands. "One O'Clock Jump," "Jumpin' at the Woodside," "Doggin' Around," "Taxi War Dance" — these are the source documents for what almost every jazz player of the next thirty years internalized as "swing feel."

Scaruffi rates this band as a cultural fact more than a compositional one: the Basie rhythm section's 4/4 walking pulse, with Jo Jones moving the time-keeping function from the bass drum to the hi-hat, is one of the major rhythmic innovations of twentieth-century music, and the Decca sides document its arrival. Projected-Fantano's read is the more skeptical one — the writing is functional, the soloists carry it, and the album as artifact is a 1992 compilation rather than a coherent single statement. We kept it at #5 because Lester Young's solos here are the most consequential single-instrument body of work in the swing era, and any list that buries them is dishonest. The Walter Page bass lines are the other under-credited innovation on these sides: Page's "all-American rhythm section" approach (Page, Jones, Basie, Freddie Green) is the prototype for every piano-bass-drums-guitar swing-feel section that followed, and the Decca recordings are where that section coheres on tape for the first time. Ear-training note for listeners coming to this material fresh: the rhythm section is the composition. The horn arrangements are head charts and riffs; the architectural innovation is in the four-piece engine room beneath them.

##### 6. Benny Goodman — *The Famous 1938 Carnegie Hall Jazz Concert* (recorded 1938, released 1950)

The first jazz concert at Carnegie Hall, performed by the most popular bandleader in America at the moment swing was the dominant pop music in the country. The historical weight is impossible to separate from the music, but the music holds: "Sing, Sing, Sing" with Gene Krupa is the canonical extended big band performance of the era, and the integrated jam-session segments — Goodman with members of the Ellington and Basie bands — are the first major document of racially integrated jazz performance on a major stage.

Scaruffi is lukewarm on Goodman as a composer (he wasn't one) and merely respectful of him as a clarinetist (he was a very good one). Projected-Fantano is more skeptical, since Goodman's pop-success arc is exactly the kind of crossover narrative he tends to discount. Both critics agreed, however, that the Carnegie concert is a structural event in the history of the form, and the recording survives as music rather than just as document. The Krupa-driven "Sing, Sing, Sing" alone is the moment the drum solo became a legitimate compositional centerpiece in American music. It belongs. The recording's provenance is also worth noting — the discs sat in a closet for twelve years before anyone realized they had documented one of the most important nights in American music, and the 1950 release format (a Columbia LP set) was itself a major moment in the new format's establishment as a vehicle for live jazz documentation. The 1999 Phil Schaap-supervised remaster is the audiophile reference; the 1950 mix is the historical artifact; both are worth knowing.

##### 7. Artie Shaw — *Self Portrait* (recorded 1938–54, comp. 2001)

Shaw is the bandleader Scaruffi prefers over Goodman: a more inventive arranger, a more harmonically curious clarinetist, and a more interesting personality (he quit at the height of his fame, repeatedly, because he found the audience boring). The RCA *Self Portrait* set is the best-curated overview of Shaw's career, drawing from his 1938 commercial-peak band, his 1940 Gramercy Five small group with Billy Butterfield, and his under-recognized 1949 and 1954 bands that flirted with bebop arrangement on a big-band scale.

"Begin the Beguine" is the popular hit and is on every Shaw compilation; the more important inclusions are "Frenesi," "Stardust" (the 1940 version with Butterfield's trumpet solo, which is one of the most emulated solos in jazz history), and "Concerto for Clarinet," Shaw's attempt to write a third-stream piece a decade before third-stream existed as a category.

Projected-Fantano grants Shaw the slot over Goodman on the grounds that Shaw was the more formally restless artist, and that *Self Portrait* sequenced as it is — career-spanning rather than peak-moment — gives a clearer picture of a working musician's actual range than any single contemporaneous album would. Scaruffi concurs; Shaw is one of the few white swing-era bandleaders he treats as a composer rather than as a personality. Shaw's late-period bands (1949 and 1954) included Tal Farlow on guitar, Hank Jones on piano, and a young string section that anticipated the Gil Evans/Miles Davis collaborations of the same decade. The fact that Shaw quit playing entirely in 1954 and lived another fifty years without picking up the instrument is the kind of biographical refusal that, in a different genre, would have produced a cult — in jazz it produced a strange silence around one of the era's most interesting figures.

##### 8. Jimmie Lunceford & His Orchestra — *Lunceford Special* (recorded 1939–40, comp. 1956 / 1967)

The orchestrator's big band. Sy Oliver and Eddie Durham wrote charts for Lunceford that are the most rhythmically sophisticated section-writing in the swing era — two-against-three internal pulses, brass against reeds in independent rhythmic strata, and the famous "Lunceford two-beat" that influenced everyone from Tommy Dorsey to Stan Kenton. "For Dancers Only," "Lunceford Special," "Uptown Blues," and "Blues in the Groove" are the canonical tracks.

Scaruffi lists Lunceford among the half-dozen most important bandleaders of the era, specifically as an arranging-driven unit: the band was tight to the point of being a precision instrument, and the writing is what made it remarkable. Projected-Fantano (extrapolating heavily) likely respects Lunceford for the same reason: the band is a composer's band, the soloists are subordinated to the chart, and the record is interesting as architecture rather than as personality.

This was the coin-flip winner over Fletcher Henderson — narrowly, on the grounds that the Lunceford charts are more individually distinct, where Henderson's writing was so widely absorbed into the general swing vocabulary (most directly via Goodman, who bought many of Henderson's arrangements) that it is harder to hear Henderson as Henderson rather than as the substrate of the era. Henderson goes to honorables. The Lunceford band itself was also a remarkable performance unit — visual showmanship (instrument-tossing routines, choreographed section moves) that some critics held against them, paired with a level of ensemble precision that meant the showmanship never compromised the actual playing. Sy Oliver left for Tommy Dorsey in 1939 and the band's commercial momentum cooled; Lunceford himself died in 1947 at forty-five, and the band did not survive him. The 1939–40 window represented on this compilation is the band at its compositional peak.

##### 9. Chick Webb & His Orchestra (with Ella Fitzgerald) — *The Quintessential Chick Webb* (recorded 1929–39, comp. various)

Webb died at thirty in 1939, having led the house band at the Savoy Ballroom and having discovered Ella Fitzgerald as a teenager. The band was a dancers' band — fast, tight, drums-forward — and Webb himself was the most rhythmically potent drummer of the pre-Krupa era, playing through a kidney condition and chronic back pain that should have made the instrument impossible. "Stompin' at the Savoy" (Webb's composition, though Goodman made it famous), "Liza," and the early Fitzgerald features including "A-Tisket, A-Tasket" are the canonical material.

Scaruffi values Webb less for compositional depth than for the documentation of an era: the Savoy band as it actually sounded, with the rhythmic intensity that records of more famous bands often smoothed over. Projected-Fantano is the harder sell — a vocalist-led pop hit from 1938 doesn't match his usual register — but the early instrumental Webb material is some of the most propulsive pre-Blakey drumming on tape, and the historical case for Webb as one of the architects of swing rhythm is strong enough to outweigh the showband trappings.

This was the coin-flip winner over Cab Calloway. Calloway's band employed Dizzy Gillespie, Chu Berry, and Cozy Cole simultaneously, which is a stronger soloist roster, but Webb's band as a unit was the more unified musical entity. Calloway to honorables. The Webb-Krupa drum battle at the Savoy in 1937, where Webb supposedly cut Krupa decisively in front of a packed house, is the kind of foundational legend that most jazz history depends on without being able to verify, and the Webb sides are partial corroboration: nobody who plays this hard, this consistently, on records made in a bare studio with primitive miking, was getting outplayed by anyone in 1937.

##### 10. Woody Herman — *Blowin' Up a Storm: The Columbia Years 1945–47* (recorded 1945–47, comp. 2001)

The First Herd: Herman's late-war and immediately-postwar band, which was the most rhythmically aggressive and harmonically advanced large ensemble of the swing-to-bop transition. Stravinsky wrote *Ebony Concerto* for this band. Ralph Burns and Neal Hefti (pre-Basie) were the principal arrangers. "Caldonia," "Apple Honey," "Northwest Passage," and "Wild Root" are the headliners, but the deeper material — the suite-like extended pieces and the more harmonically dense charts — is where the band's case rests.

Scaruffi rates the First Herd higher than most American critics of the swing-era canon do, treating it as the bridge between Lunceford-style precision arranging and the Kenton-style harmonic experimentation of the late 1940s. Projected-Fantano concurs: this is the swing-era big band that points most directly toward what bop and post-bop would do with large ensembles, and it does so without abandoning the rhythmic pulse that defines swing as a feel.

This was the coin-flip winner over Boyd Raeburn's avant-side band, on the grounds that Herman's First Herd actually swung where Raeburn's band sometimes did not, and that the Herman record has stayed in active circulation while Raeburn has receded into specialist territory. Raeburn to honorables. Personnel on the First Herd that should be called out: Bill Harris on trombone (one of the era's most distinctive soloists), Flip Phillips on tenor, Chubby Jackson on bass with the rhythmically advanced walking lines that influenced Charles Mingus's early playing, and Dave Tough on drums until illness forced him out, replaced by Don Lamond. The band lasted barely two years before Herman broke it up for financial reasons and reassembled as the Second Herd (the "Four Brothers" band, a different proposition entirely), which is why the 1945–47 window matters as a discrete artistic moment rather than as a phase in a longer Herman career.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

**Slot 8 — Lunceford vs. Henderson**: Coin gave Lunceford. Justification on appeal: Lunceford's arrangements have a more distinct compositional fingerprint; Henderson's writing was so absorbed into the broader swing vocabulary that it is hard to separate his individual voice from the era's generic one. Henderson is in honorables and the case for him as the most-influential-but-least-represented swing-era arranger remains strong.

**Slot 9 — Webb vs. Calloway**: Coin gave Webb. Justification on appeal: Webb's band was the more unified ensemble even though Calloway's roster of soloists was deeper. The Fitzgerald discovery is also a load-bearing historical fact; without Webb's 1934 hire she might not have had the launch she did. Calloway is in honorables.

**Slot 10 — Woody Herman First Herd vs. Boyd Raeburn**: Coin gave Herman. Justification on appeal: Herman swung; Raeburn sometimes did not. Both were ambitious, but Herman's band is in active rotation in 2026 and Raeburn's is mostly an academic interest. Raeburn is in honorables for the historical record.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Fletcher Henderson — *A Study in Frustration* (recorded 1923–38, comp. 1961)**: The four-LP Columbia compilation that documents Henderson as the foundational big band arranger — the writer who effectively invented the call-and-response brass-vs-reeds format that every swing band used. Sold many of his charts to Goodman after his own band failed commercially. The most influential bandleader on the swing era who is barely remembered as a bandleader.
- **Cab Calloway — *Are You Hep to the Jive?* (recorded 1939–47, comp. 1994)**: Calloway's later-period band, including the Gillespie-era material. Superb soloist showcase even when the front-of-stage personality is doing the most.
- **Boyd Raeburn — *Jewells* (recorded 1945–47, comp. 1989)**: George Handy and Johnny Richards arrangements, harmonically further out than anything else in the era. The avant-curio.
- **Count Basie & His Orchestra — *The Complete Decca Recordings 1937–1939* (comp. 1992, alt. version of #5)**: The fuller-context companion to the Lester Young material on the top-ten Basie entry.
- **Benny Goodman — *The Complete Small Group Recordings* (comp. 1997)**: The Goodman-Wilson-Krupa trio and Goodman-Wilson-Krupa-Hampton quartet, which were the first integrated working units in mainstream American music. Compositionally light, historically heavy.
- **Tommy Dorsey & His Orchestra — *Yes, Indeed!* (recorded 1939–45, comp. 1995)**: Sy Oliver-arranged Dorsey, with the young Sinatra material. The bridge between swing-era big band and the postwar singer-fronted orchestra format.
- **Lionel Hampton — *Tempo and Swing* (recorded 1937–41, comp. 1996)**: Hampton's Victor-era small-and-medium group sides, which functioned as a Who's Who of working swing-era musicians and which produced some of the era's most spontaneous arranged-on-the-fly performances.
- **Stan Kenton — *City of Glass* (recorded 1947–53, comp. 1995)**: The Bob Graettinger compositions for Kenton's orchestra — the genuinely avant-garde edge of the late swing era, scored for an enormous brass-heavy ensemble using techniques borrowed from Schoenberg and Stravinsky. Polarizing, often un-swinging, occasionally extraordinary.
- **Duke Ellington & His Orchestra — *The Blanton-Webster Band* (recorded 1940–42, comp. 1986)**: The 1940–42 Ellington band with Jimmy Blanton on bass and Ben Webster on tenor — frequently cited as the greatest single jazz orchestra ever assembled. Excluded from the top ten only because three Ellington-led entries was already the negotiated maximum; on a Scaruffi-only list this would be top three.
- **Artie Shaw — *Begin the Beguine* (recorded 1938–41, comp. 1986)**: The RCA Bluebird single-disc overview of Shaw's commercial peak, a more focused alternative to the *Self Portrait* career retrospective for listeners who want the popular hits in one place.

---

### Bebop
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Bebop is the genre where Scaruffi has the home-court advantage and Fantano is, frankly, a tourist. Scaruffi's jazz historiography treats Charlie Parker as a near-mythological figure — a saxophonist who collapsed Lester Young's lyricism, Coleman Hawkins' harmonic density, and the rhythmic acceleration of Kansas City swing into a single chromatic language that effectively re-coded what jazz improvisation could be. Bud Powell is, for Scaruffi, the great tragic translator of this language to the keyboard; Monk's early Blue Notes are foundational. Fantano, born in 1985 and writing primarily about post-2000 popular music, has almost no recorded commentary on Parker-era recordings. His passing references — to Parker as an "obvious giant," to Mingus and Coltrane as inheritors — let us EXTRAPOLATE that he respects the canon without having a granular ranking. **Flag**: Fantano's positions in this file are reconstructed from adjacent commentary (his Coltrane respect, his Mingus appreciation, his general "pre-1955 jazz is foundational" framing) and from what a 2020s rockist-poptimist with strong harmonic literacy would plausibly hold. Treat his rankings here as inference rather than citation.

The debate breaks along several fault lines. First: **studio sessions vs. live documents**. Scaruffi privileges the official Savoy/Dial sides as the canonical Parker text; Fantano (extrapolated) leans into *Jazz at Massey Hall* — the only live recording of the classic quintet — for its raw historical mass. Second: **Monk's placement**. Scaruffi insists Monk's *Genius of Modern Music* sessions belong in any bebop top 10 because they are sonically of the era even if Monk was already deviating from bop's default vocabulary; Fantano's framework would push Monk later, into his Riverside period. Third: **Sarah Vaughan and the bop vocalist question**. Scaruffi treats vocal bop as adjacent rather than central; Fantano (again extrapolated) probably weights vocal records more, given his general ear for production and arrangement. Fourth: **the Powell question**. Both critics rate Powell highly, but Scaruffi treats *The Amazing Bud Powell Vol. 1* as essentially co-equal with the Parker compilations, while Fantano would likely under-rate Powell relative to the horn players. Fifth: **the compilation problem**.
