The form coalesces around three figures working partly in parallel: Ornette Coleman in Los Angeles and then New York, whose 1959 *The Shape of Jazz to Come* introduces the harmolodic concept (improvisation that is not bound to a fixed harmonic structure but moves freely through tonal centers) and whose 1960 *Free Jazz* — a 37-minute double-quartet improvisation that gives the sub-genre its name — is the form's most ambitious early statement; Cecil Taylor in New York, whose piano work treats the instrument percussively and whose harmonic vocabulary draws as much on Henry Cowell and Stockhausen as on jazz piano predecessors (the 1956 *Jazz Advance* is the early statement, the 1966 *Unit Structures* and *Conquistador!* are the mature peaks); and the Coltrane quartet's late period, beginning with *Ascension* (1965) and continuing through *Meditations*, *Live at the Village Vanguard Again!*, *Live in Japan*, *Interstellar Space* (the duo with Rashied Ali), and the posthumously issued *Stellar Regions*. Albert Ayler's spiritual-folk-free hybrid (*Spiritual Unity* 1964, *Bells / Prophecy* 1965, the New York *Greenwich Village* sessions) is the most emotionally exposed wing of the form. The Art Ensemble of Chicago and the broader AACM (Association for the Advancement of Creative Musicians) generation — Roscoe Mitchell, Joseph Jarman, Anthony Braxton, Muhal Richard Abrams, Henry Threadgill — produce the form's most theoretically rigorous wing in the late 60s and early 70s. This is one of the files where Scaruffi's voice is closest to dominant: he treats free jazz as one of the great twentieth-century artistic movements and rates the canonical records (Ornette's *Free Jazz*, Coltrane's *Ascension*, Taylor's *Unit Structures*, Ayler's *Spiritual Unity*) at the top of his entire jazz scale. Fantano's coverage is thinner but his stated criteria — sonic singularity, emotional rawness, harmonic adventurousness — line up with the canonical free placements unusually cleanly, which makes the extrapolation work easier here than in some of the more melodic sub-genres.

**Soul Jazz** is the groove. Through the late 1950s and the 1960s, a wing of the hard-bop mainstream — initially the Hammond B-3 organists (Jimmy Smith, Jack McDuff, Big John Patton, Larry Young, Charles Earland, Reuben Wilson, the entire Blue Note and Prestige organ-trio catalog), then the Cannonball Adderley quintet, then the Lee Morgan / Bobby Timmons / Horace Silver hard-bop axis when it was working in its "funky" mode — fused jazz harmonic vocabulary with explicit gospel and rhythm-and-blues groove and produced the most commercially accessible jazz of the era. Jimmy Smith's *The Sermon!* (1958), Cannonball's *The Cannonball Adderley Quintet in San Francisco* (1959, with the Bobby Timmons "This Here" hit), Horace Silver's *Song for My Father* (1965), Lee Morgan's *The Sidewinder* (1964) — the records crossed over to Black radio and to non-jazz audiences in ways the dense hard-bop canon usually did not. The file's heaviest cross-listing tension is with hard bop proper; the working rule is that records made primarily for jazz-club audiences and rated by jazz critics anchor in hard bop, while records that crossed over to R&B charts or to non-jazz Black audiences anchor in soul jazz. The form has a long tail through the 1970s into jazz-funk territory (Donald Byrd's *Black Byrd*, the entire CTI label's commercial output, Grover Washington Jr.'s early sides) that the file works through carefully because the line between late soul jazz, jazz-funk, and the smooth-jazz wing that gets its own later file is genuinely fuzzy.

**Latin Jazz** is the parallel tradition. The Afro-Cuban / jazz crossover predates the bop revolution — Mario Bauzá brought the Cuban *son montuno* and *guaguancó* rhythmic vocabulary into the Cab Calloway and Chick Webb bands in the late 1930s, and his subsequent musical direction of Machito's Afro-Cubans (formed 1940) is the foundational document — but the form crystallizes commercially in the late 1940s with Dizzy Gillespie's collaboration with Chano Pozo (the "Manteca" / "Cubana Be / Cubana Bop" sides covered in the bebop file) and consolidates over the next four decades through three distinct waves. The Mongo Santamaría / Cal Tjader / Tito Puente / Eddie Palmieri / Ray Barretto axis runs through the 1950s and 60s — the Fania All-Stars period (late 60s through 70s) brings the form to its commercial peak with the salsa explosion, and the Latin-jazz wing of that movement (Palmieri's *The Sun of Latin Music* 1974, Barretto's *Indestructible* 1973, the Fania Live at the Cheetah recordings) produces the file's central 1970s material. The post-Fania continuation through the 1980s and 90s (Paquito D'Rivera, Arturo Sandoval, Chucho Valdés and Irakere, Gonzalo Rubalcaba, Danilo Pérez, the entire post-revolutionary Cuban jazz exodus) extends the canon through the present. The file is the one in this part where Scaruffi's coverage is thinnest and where the editor has had to lean on the broader Latin-music critical apparatus (Rondón's *El Libro de la Salsa*, the Fania scholarship) to construct placements both critics could defend.

**Spiritual Jazz** is the mystical-political turn. From roughly 1964 (Coltrane's *A Love Supreme*) forward, a wing of the post-bop avant-garde — Coltrane himself in his late period, Pharoah Sanders (the entire Impulse! catalog from *Tauhid* 1967 through *Karma* 1969 with "The Creator Has a Master Plan" and "Hum-Allah-Hum-Allah-Hum-Allah," through *Black Unity* and *Wisdom Through Music* and *Elevation* and *Love in Us All*), Alice Coltrane (the post-John Strata-East and Impulse! catalog: *A Monastic Trio*, *Universal Consciousness*, *Journey in Satchidananda*, the late ashram-based devotional records), Don Cherry's world-music-inflected work, the Sun Ra Arkestra in its Saturn-and-Impulse! period, the Strata-East label's entire roster (Charles Tolliver, Stanley Cowell, Cecil McBee, the *Dollar Brand* / Abdullah Ibrahim sessions), and the more recent revival generation (Kamasi Washington's *The Epic*, the Pharoah Sanders / Floating Points *Promises*, the contemporary London scene around Shabaka Hutchings) — produces a body of music that fuses extended-form Coltrane-lineage modal-and-free improvisation with explicit religious, political, and Pan-African content. The form's central artistic claim is that jazz can be a spiritual practice rather than a popular entertainment, and the records make the claim seriously and at length. This is the file where Scaruffi and Fantano are most unusually aligned — the Coltrane lineage is one of the few places where Fantano's direct critical work goes deep into pre-1990 jazz, and his recent reviews of Kamasi and Floating Points are some of his strongest jazz writing. The file also has the volume's clearest demonstration that the contemporary spiritual-jazz revival is not a rupture but a continuation; the canonical 1960s and 70s records and the 2010s and 2020s records share more than the period gap would suggest.

Five sub-genres, one decade and a half of generative work, five durable canons. The reading instruction for this part is the same as for Part One: the dependencies run forward (free jazz needs the modal context, spiritual jazz needs the free and modal context behind it, soul jazz reads against the hard-bop mainstream rather than separately from it), and reading the sub-genres in the order they appear here is the right way through. The cross-listings between the five files in this part are the densest in the volume; the editor has tried to be generous with the cross-references and explicit about the anchor decisions.

---

### Modal Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Modal jazz is the rare subgenre where one album so dominates the conversation that the only real debate is about everything underneath it. *Kind of Blue* (1959) is the canonical anchor — both critics agree it tops the list, and there is no serious case for displacing it. The argument begins at #2.

Scaruffi opened with a Coltrane-heavy slate. He treats the classic quartet — Coltrane, McCoy Tyner, Jimmy Garrison, Elvin Jones — as the highest expression of post-Davis modal practice, and he wanted four Coltrane records in the top ten (*A Love Supreme*, *Crescent*, *Live at the Village Vanguard*, *My Favorite Things*). He also pushed hard for Wayne Shorter's *Speak No Evil* and Herbie Hancock's *Maiden Voyage* as the Blue Note canon, with McCoy Tyner's *The Real McCoy* as the post-quartet pivot.

Fantano's positions had to be partly extrapolated [FLAG]. He has talked about *Kind of Blue* and *A Love Supreme* on stream as foundational records and given Coltrane's catalog general reverence, but he has not produced a ranked list of modal jazz specifically. From his published Coltrane and Davis commentary plus his general bias toward records that "feel like one statement," I extrapolate he would weight *A Love Supreme* and *Maiden Voyage* highly and be lukewarm on *Live at the Village Vanguard* (sprawling, transitional) versus *Crescent* (compact, focused).

The first big fight: Scaruffi wanted both *Crescent* AND *Live at the Village Vanguard*; Fantano (extrapolated) preferred consolidating to one Coltrane quartet record beyond *A Love Supreme*. Compromise: *Crescent* in, *Vanguard* to honorable mentions.

The second fight: *A Love Supreme*'s placement. Spiritual jazz file will not claim it — the central modal achievement belongs here. Both critics agreed.

The third fight: Bill Evans. Scaruffi recognizes Evans on *Kind of Blue* but doesn't treat any of his trio records as primarily modal. Fantano (extrapolated) likely would not push for him here either. Resolved: no Evans solo entry; his contribution lives inside *Kind of Blue*.

The fourth fight: Pharoah Sanders' *Karma*. Scaruffi sees it as more spiritual/free than modal. Coordinated exclusion — it goes to spiritual jazz.

The fifth fight: *Milestones* (1958) vs Davis's other pre-*Bitches Brew* modal work. Scaruffi insisted on *Milestones* as the genuine pre-*Kind of Blue* modal proof-of-concept; Fantano (extrapolated) less attached. Scaruffi won on weight of historical argument.

Coin-flips resolved two ties: Coltrane's *Coltrane* (1962) vs *Africa/Brass*; and Andrew Hill's *Point of Departure* vs Joe Henderson's *Inner Urge*.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Miles Davis — *Kind of Blue* (1959)

There is no argument here and there has not been one for sixty-five years. *Kind of Blue* is the album that turned modal jazz from a theoretical experiment by George Russell and a personal pivot by Davis into a permanent reorganization of how jazz could be written, played, and heard. Recorded across two sessions at Columbia's 30th Street Studio with sketches Davis brought in the morning of the date, the record pulls Bill Evans back into the band specifically to anchor the harmonic palette in modal voicings, lets Cannonball Adderley and John Coltrane play against scales rather than chord changes, and trusts the rhythm section of Paul Chambers and Jimmy Cobb to hold the floor while the soloists take their time.

What both critics agree on, and what Scaruffi has written about extensively, is that the genius of *Kind of Blue* is restraint as compositional method. "So What" and "Flamenco Sketches" don't move; they breathe. The chord changes are reduced to a minimum so the soloists have to invent melody, color, and phrase length from the inside out. Coltrane plays some of the most concentrated solos of his career here precisely because he can't hide behind harmonic complexity. Adderley sounds liberated. Evans plays the introductions like he is laying down a new grammar.

The record sold steadily for decades and is the best-selling jazz album of all time, but its commercial dominance has obscured its difficulty. *Kind of Blue* asks the listener to accept that nothing dramatic is going to happen, and that within "nothing dramatic" lives every emotional possibility. Scaruffi treats it as the moment jazz became a music of atmosphere as much as virtuosity. Fantano, in his rare jazz commentary, has called it the entry point that doesn't lie about what jazz is. Both right. Number one without a coin flip.

##### 2. John Coltrane — *A Love Supreme* (1964)

*A Love Supreme* is where modal jazz becomes a vehicle for something larger than itself, and the reason it anchors here rather than in the spiritual jazz file is that the spiritual content is delivered entirely through modal architecture. The four-movement suite — "Acknowledgement," "Resolution," "Pursuance," "Psalm" — is built on a small bass figure that Jimmy Garrison turns into a mantra, McCoy Tyner's quartal voicings, and Elvin Jones playing polyrhythms that sound like devotional rhythm rather than swing. Coltrane himself chants the title phrase. The record is a prayer, but it is a prayer constructed out of the harmonic vocabulary Davis opened up five years earlier.

Scaruffi has written that this is the peak of the classic quartet, and that what makes it monumental is the integration of every player into a single intention. There are no stunt solos, no showpiece tradeoffs. Tyner's left hand and Jones's ride cymbal create a continuous field that Coltrane plays through rather than over. "Psalm" is famously a wordless reading of Coltrane's own poem printed in the liner notes — saxophone as recitation, melody dictated by syllable.

Fantano, when he has spoken about Coltrane on stream, has named *A Love Supreme* as the obvious starting point and has been clear that its reputation is earned rather than inflated [FLAG: extrapolated from general commentary, not a ranked review]. Both critics agreed it sits at #2 without needing a coin flip. The only question was whether to claim it for spiritual jazz instead. Coordinated decision: it belongs here. The spiritual file gets *Karma*, *Journey in Satchidananda*, late Coltrane. *A Love Supreme* is the central modal achievement of the 1960s and its absence from this list would be unjustifiable on any reading of the form.

##### 3. Herbie Hancock — *Maiden Voyage* (1965)

*Maiden Voyage* is the prettiest modal record ever made and that is not a lightweight observation. Hancock, in the middle of his tenure with the second Miles Davis quintet, brought Freddie Hubbard, George Coleman, Ron Carter, and Tony Williams into Rudy Van Gelder's studio and recorded a suite of pieces organized around a maritime concept. The title track's two-chord vamp — a suspended ninth shifting against a static bass — is one of the most recognizable openings in jazz, and it works because Hancock understood that modal harmony's gift is the creation of suspended emotional states.

Scaruffi rates Hancock's Blue Note period as the strongest run any modal pianist had outside Tyner, and he treats *Maiden Voyage* as the record where Hancock fully separated his compositional voice from Davis's. "Dolphin Dance," "Little One," and "Survival of the Fittest" each find a different way to use modal centers as compositional anchors rather than improvisational frameworks. Hubbard's solos here are among his most patient, and Williams's drumming is the texture that lets the whole record float.

Fantano (extrapolated) would likely place *Maiden Voyage* in his top five — it has the unified-statement quality he tends to reward, and Hancock is one of the few jazz figures with crossover recognition in his audience [FLAG]. The critics agreed on top-five placement; the only debate was whether to put it above *Crescent*. Hancock won that one on consensus accessibility plus compositional density. The record is also the cleanest answer to anyone who claims modal jazz collapses into static atmosphere — *Maiden Voyage* is structurally precise from first bar to last.

##### 4. John Coltrane — *Crescent* (1964)

*Crescent* is the classic quartet's most underrated record, recorded just months before *A Love Supreme* and arguably the studio album where the band's interplay reached its most refined balance. The title track unfolds at a tempo that should be too slow to sustain, and it sustains anyway because Tyner's voicings and Jones's brushwork create a continuous undertow. "Wise One" is the most vulnerable composition Coltrane ever recorded — a ballad that uses modal harmony to keep the melody from resolving into sentiment.

Scaruffi has long argued that *Crescent* is the record to play for someone who already knows *A Love Supreme* and wants to understand what made the quartet a unit rather than a vehicle for Coltrane's solo voice. The B-side opens with "Bessie's Blues," which sounds like a throwback to bop until you notice how Tyner is reharmonizing the changes underneath, and then "Lonnie's Lament" and "The Drum Thing" — a feature for Jones — close the record in a meditative register that anticipates the spiritual material to come.

Fantano's position here is the most uncertain on the list [FLAG: extrapolated]. He has not, to my knowledge, given *Crescent* a public ranking. From his stated preferences for compact, focused statements over sprawling live documents, I infer he would prefer *Crescent* to *Live at the Village Vanguard* if forced to pick one post-*Love Supreme* quartet record. That extrapolation is what put *Crescent* at #4 and pushed *Vanguard* to honorable mentions. If Fantano has actually called *Vanguard* superior in some podcast episode I missed, this ordering would flip. The decision is a coin-flip-equivalent and is flagged accordingly in the resolutions section.

##### 5. Wayne Shorter — *Speak No Evil* (1965)

*Speak No Evil* is the Blue Note album where modal jazz finally produced a composer-led record that could stand alongside *Kind of Blue* and *Maiden Voyage* as a self-contained artistic statement. Shorter, also serving in the Davis quintet at the time, brought Hancock, Carter, Hubbard, and Elvin Jones — borrowed from the Coltrane band — into Van Gelder's studio for a session that combined modal harmonic openness with the kind of harmonic ambiguity Shorter had been developing in his compositions for Art Blakey.

Scaruffi treats Shorter as the most sophisticated composer of the Blue Note modal era, and *Speak No Evil* as the record where his compositional language fully cohered. "Witch Hunt" opens with a riff that hovers between modes; "Fee-Fi-Fo-Fum" plays modal language against bop phrasing; "Speak No Evil" itself uses a slow, descending vamp to create harmonic suspense; and "Infant Eyes" is one of the loveliest ballads in the modal repertoire, built on a series of unresolved cadences that keep the melody perpetually airborne.

What separates *Speak No Evil* from a strong session date is the ensemble logic. Hubbard and Shorter front-line together with a kind of conversational restraint that borrows from the Davis quintet's interplay. Hancock comps with the same liquid sense of voice-leading he brings to *Maiden Voyage*. Jones, freed from the Coltrane quartet's volume demands, plays at a beautifully reduced dynamic level. Both critics agreed on a top-five placement; the debate was whether it should go above *Maiden Voyage*. Hancock's compositional unity gave him the edge, but the margin was thin. *Speak No Evil* is the strongest #5 you could ask for.

##### 6. McCoy Tyner — *The Real McCoy* (1967)

*The Real McCoy* is the record that proved Tyner had a compositional and bandleading voice fully equal to his accompanist work with Coltrane. Recorded after Tyner left the quartet, with Joe Henderson on tenor, Ron Carter on bass, and Elvin Jones again on drums, the album takes the harmonic vocabulary Tyner had developed under Coltrane — quartal voicings, pedal-point modality, the use of sus chords as resting points — and turns it into a set of compositions that don't need a saxophonist of Coltrane's stature to justify them.

Scaruffi has written about Tyner as the pianist who most fully internalized the implications of *Kind of Blue* and then pushed them past Davis's own use of them. *The Real McCoy* is the proof: "Passion Dance" opens the record with a vamp that Tyner attacks with the full physical force he had developed playing alongside Coltrane, and Henderson immediately answers with a solo that establishes him as one of the few tenors who could play in that harmonic space without sounding like a Coltrane imitation. "Contemplation" slows everything down into the quietest possible modal meditation. "Four by Five" and "Search for Peace" complete a record that is balanced in every way *Coltrane* itself sometimes wasn't.

Fantano (extrapolated) likely has less specific commentary on Tyner [FLAG], but the record's craftsmanship and its position as the post-quartet pivot make a strong case for top-ten placement that Fantano's general jazz framework would accept. The placement at #6 reflects a clear consensus that this is the strongest non-Davis, non-Coltrane modal record after *Maiden Voyage* and *Speak No Evil*.

What's worth underlining is the historical timing. *The Real McCoy* was recorded in April 1967, three months before Coltrane's death, at a moment when the leader had already moved well past the modal vocabulary the quartet had perfected. Tyner had left because that newer direction — closer to free playing, with Pharoah Sanders and Rashied Ali in the band — wasn't his language. *The Real McCoy* is therefore the record where Tyner deliberately holds the modal line that Coltrane himself was abandoning, and in doing so he establishes the template for almost every post-1967 modal pianist. Mulgrew Miller, Kenny Kirkland, Geri Allen, and the entire neo-traditional school of the 1980s and 90s play out of this record's harmonic vocabulary. Without *The Real McCoy*, modal jazz might have ended with Coltrane's late period; with it, the language gets a parallel track that survives into the present.

##### 7. Miles Davis — *Milestones* (1958)

*Milestones* is the proof that *Kind of Blue* was not an accident. Recorded a year earlier with the same rhythm section (Chambers, Cobb soon to replace Philly Joe Jones) and the great sextet of Coltrane, Adderley, and Davis, the album's title track is the first major modal composition Davis recorded — built on two modes (G Dorian and A Aeolian) with no functional chord changes, a structural choice that George Russell had been theorizing and that Davis was first to put on a major-label record.

Scaruffi insisted on *Milestones* being in the top ten on historical grounds: without this record there is no *Kind of Blue*, and without the experiment of "Milestones" the title track there is no "So What." Around it, the rest of the album is more conventional hard bop ("Dr. Jackle," "Sid's Ahead," "Two Bass Hit"), but the title cut alone establishes the entire vocabulary the next decade of jazz would draw from.

Fantano (extrapolated) probably wouldn't push for *Milestones* on his own [FLAG] — it's a more transitional, less unified record than *Kind of Blue*, and his bias toward whole-statement albums would weigh against it. But he would accept its inclusion on canon grounds. Scaruffi carried the argument: leaving *Milestones* off would amount to pretending modal jazz arrived fully formed in 1959, which is bad history. Placement at #7 reflects the compromise — high enough to acknowledge primacy, low enough to acknowledge that as a complete record it is less than the descendants it made possible.

##### 8. John Coltrane — *My Favorite Things* (1961)

*My Favorite Things* is where Coltrane first publicly committed to the modal direction and announced the classic quartet to the world. The title track — the Rodgers and Hammerstein show tune from *The Sound of Music*, transformed into a thirteen-minute soprano saxophone meditation built on two modes alternating beneath a vamp — is the record that made Coltrane a star outside the jazz audience and gave modal jazz its first crossover hit.

Scaruffi treats this as the moment Coltrane found the band that would carry him through the rest of his major work — Tyner, Jones, and Steve Davis on bass (Jimmy Garrison would replace him soon after). The arrangement of "My Favorite Things" is a kind of ritual repetition that turns the song's melody into a mantra long before *A Love Supreme* would do it explicitly. "Summertime" gets a similar treatment. "But Not for Me" and "Everytime We Say Goodbye" are more conventional but show the band already operating with the kind of unanimity that would become its signature.

Fantano (extrapolated) likely accepts *My Favorite Things* as canon and would not fight for higher placement [FLAG]. The record is structurally less unified than *A Love Supreme* or *Crescent* — it's still partly a standards date. But the title cut alone earns top-ten placement on cultural and modal-historical grounds. Both critics agreed on inclusion; the only question was whether it should go above *Milestones*. Scaruffi's historical argument for *Milestones*'s primacy won, but barely. *My Favorite Things* sits at #8 as the bridge between Davis's first modal experiments and the Coltrane quartet's full flowering.

##### 9. John Coltrane — *Coltrane* (1962)

*Coltrane*, the self-titled 1962 Impulse! record, is the album where the classic quartet — now with Garrison locked in on bass — first sounds fully like itself in the studio. It contains "Out of This World," a fifteen-minute reworking of a Harold Arlen song that turns it into a modal vehicle in the lineage of "My Favorite Things"; "Soul Eyes," one of the most beautiful ballads in the Coltrane book; "Tunji," a Yoruba-titled original that signals the band's growing engagement with non-Western modal sources; and "Miles' Mode," a tribute to Davis built on a twelve-tone row that becomes a modal vamp.

Scaruffi has written about *Coltrane* as a transitional record between the early quartet's standards-driven program and the original-composition focus that would dominate from *Crescent* forward. The playing is uniformly excellent — Tyner's left hand is fully developed, Jones is starting to push toward the polyrhythmic density that defines the band's later work, Garrison is establishing his vocabulary of double-stops and pedal points.

The placement at #9 came out of a coin flip against *Africa/Brass* (1961). Scaruffi initially leaned *Africa/Brass* for its more ambitious orchestrations and Eric Dolphy's arrangements, but the record's inconsistency — the big-band tracks don't quite match the quartet material — pushed it down. *Coltrane* is more self-contained and more representative of where the modal quartet was going. The coin flip went to *Coltrane*. The reasoning is fully laid out in the resolutions section. Fantano's position is unknown [FLAG]; this placement is essentially Scaruffi's call with extrapolated Fantano consent on grounds of canonical recognition.

##### 10. Andrew Hill — *Point of Departure* (1964)

*Point of Departure* is the record that earns the tenth slot by extending modal jazz into territory the Davis-Coltrane axis didn't fully explore. Hill — the pianist whose Blue Note records sit somewhere between modal and free — assembled an unusual sextet (Eric Dolphy, Joe Henderson, Kenny Dorham, Richard Davis, Tony Williams) and produced a record that uses modal centers as launching pads for compositions that include free passages, shifting meters, and the kind of harmonic ambiguity that would shortly produce the Blue Note avant-garde.

Scaruffi rates Hill's mid-60s Blue Note records as among the most ambitious modal-adjacent work of the period, and *Point of Departure* as the most fully realized of them. "Refuge," "New Monastery," "Flight 19," and "Dedication" each take a modal premise and twist it. Dolphy's bass clarinet work here is among the best of his career. Williams's drumming, fresh from the Davis quintet, is intricate without ever overplaying.

The placement here came from a coin flip with Joe Henderson's *Inner Urge* (1966), another modal-leaning record with a strong Tyner feature. Scaruffi argued *Point of Departure* on the grounds of compositional ambition and the Dolphy contribution; Fantano (extrapolated) probably would have leaned the same way given his historical preference for records that push their genre's edges [FLAG]. *Point of Departure* won. *Inner Urge* went to honorable mentions.

The selection of *Point of Departure* over a more "pure" modal record is a deliberate statement: by 1964, modal jazz was already mutating into the post-bop language that would define the rest of the decade, and the tenth slot should reflect that mutation rather than another album that simply extends the *Kind of Blue* template. The list is stronger for it.

Hill's own playing on the record deserves a closer look. He had a comping style that owed almost nothing to Tyner's quartal approach — instead of stacking fourths, he placed dissonant clusters at unpredictable points in the bar, leaving holes where another pianist would have laid down a vamp. That approach forced Dolphy, Henderson, and Dorham into a more conversational mode of soloing, which is part of why the front-line interplay on *Point of Departure* sounds nothing like the Davis or Coltrane bands of the same period. The record is modal in its harmonic foundations but already partly unmoored from the rhythmic regularity that *Kind of Blue* and *Maiden Voyage* depend on. That tension is why it earns the slot.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

**Coltrane *Coltrane* (1962) vs *Africa/Brass* (1961) for slot #9.** *Africa/Brass* is the more ambitious project — Coltrane's first Impulse! date, with arrangements by Eric Dolphy and McCoy Tyner for a large ensemble built around the quartet. But the record is inconsistent: the big-band tracks ("Africa," "Greensleeves," "Blues Minor") don't fully integrate the orchestral writing with the quartet's modal language, and the result is a record that points toward grandeur without fully achieving it. *Coltrane* is the more unified statement and the cleaner representation of where the quartet was. Coin flip won by *Coltrane*. *Africa/Brass* to honorable mentions.

**Andrew Hill *Point of Departure* vs Joe Henderson *Inner Urge* for slot #10.** Both are 1964-66 Blue Note records that sit on the modal/post-bop boundary. *Inner Urge* has the stronger title track and a McCoy Tyner feature that connects directly to the classic quartet vocabulary, but the rest of the album is more conventional. *Point of Departure* is more uneven but pushes harder at the form, and Eric Dolphy's contribution is irreplaceable. Coin flip went to Hill on grounds of compositional ambition and historical reach. *Inner Urge* to honorable mentions.

**Crescent vs Live at the Village Vanguard for slot #4.** This was the most contested decision on the list. Scaruffi made the case for *Vanguard* on grounds of live intensity and the inclusion of "Chasin' the Trane," a sixteen-minute trio performance with Garrison and Roy Haynes that is one of the most extreme modal-blues hybrids Coltrane ever recorded. The counter-argument: *Vanguard* is a transitional document (recorded with Eric Dolphy still in the band), not a self-contained statement, and Fantano's likely preference would be the more focused *Crescent* [FLAG: extrapolated]. *Crescent* won the slot. *Vanguard* to honorable mentions, where it is the strongest entry.

**Whether to claim *A Love Supreme* for spiritual jazz or modal jazz.** Coordinated decision rather than a coin flip. The spiritual jazz file will get Pharoah Sanders (*Karma*, *Journey in Satchidananda*), Alice Coltrane (*Journey in Satchidananda*, *Ptah, the El Daoud*), and late John Coltrane (*Meditations*, *Om*). *A Love Supreme* anchors here as the central achievement of modal practice in the 1960s. The record's spiritual content is delivered through modal architecture, and removing it from the modal canon would distort the genre's history.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **John Coltrane — *Live at the Village Vanguard* (1962).** "Chasin' the Trane" alone earns it a near-miss slot. The strongest record left off the list.
- **John Coltrane — *Africa/Brass* (1961).** First Impulse! date, ambitious orchestrations, inconsistent execution. Lost the coin flip for #9.
- **Joe Henderson — *Inner Urge* (1966).** Modal-leaning Blue Note date with McCoy Tyner. Lost the coin flip for #10.
- **Bill Evans Trio — *Sunday at the Village Vanguard* (1961).** Not primarily modal but Evans's harmonic language post-*Kind of Blue* is essential context. Excluded for genre fit.
- **Herbie Hancock — *Empyrean Isles* (1964).** The record before *Maiden Voyage*; "One Finger Snap" and "Cantaloupe Island" are modal classics, but the album is less unified than its successor.
- **Wayne Shorter — *JuJu* (1964).** Shorter's first modal-leaning Blue Note date with the Coltrane rhythm section minus Coltrane. Strong but *Speak No Evil* is the better statement.
- **McCoy Tyner — *Sahara* (1972).** Post-quartet Tyner pushing into spiritual-modal territory. Could overlap with spiritual jazz file.
- **Yusef Lateef — *Eastern Sounds* (1961).** Modal jazz with explicit non-Western influence, an early bridge to what would become "spiritual jazz" proper.
- **Freddie Hubbard — *Hub-Tones* (1962).** Hubbard as leader on a strong modal-hard-bop session.
- **John Coltrane — *Ballads* (1963).** Not a modal record per se, but the classic quartet's tone palette here is essential context for understanding *Crescent* and *A Love Supreme*.

---

### Free Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

This is Scaruffi's home turf. The Italian critic has spent five decades arguing — at length, in his unmistakable run-on syntax — that the late-50s/60s free jazz revolution was the most important development in 20th-century music after the Second Viennese School. He treats Ornette Coleman's quartet, Cecil Taylor's percussive piano, Albert Ayler's gospel-shred, the AACM's compositional avant-garde, and the European fire-music of Brötzmann not as a niche but as a redefinition of what improvised music could mean. His top-15 came in fast and confident: *Free Jazz*, *Unit Structures*, *Spiritual Unity*, *Ascension*, *Out to Lunch!*, *The Magic City*, *Karma*, *Machine Gun*, *Conquistador!*, *People in Sorrow*, *Bells*, *The Shape of Jazz to Come*, *Tauhid*, *Fire Music*, *Interstellar Space*.

Fantano is harder to pin. His direct free jazz commentary is sparse — he came up on indie rock and hip-hop, and his jazz canon skews toward post-bop and the spiritual/cosmic edge that bleeds into the contemporary scene (Kamasi, Shabaka, Sons of Kemet). EXTRAPOLATED FLAG: his picks here are inferred from scattered praise for Coltrane's late period, his openness to long-form avant-garde records (Swans, Tim Hecker), and his stated affinity for music that "commits to its premise." His list leans toward records that have crossed into wider critical recognition: *A Love Supreme*-adjacent spiritual jazz (he'd push *Karma* and *Tauhid* high), *Out to Lunch!* (the canonical "gateway" free record), *The Shape of Jazz to Come*, *Ascension*, and *Spiritual Unity*. He's likely cooler on Cecil Taylor's density and Brötzmann's pure noise.

Six rounds of debate. Areas of agreement: Coleman, Ayler's *Spiritual Unity*, Coltrane's *Ascension*, Dolphy, Sanders. Areas of friction: how high to rank Cecil Taylor (Scaruffi: top three; Fantano: outside top ten); whether *Machine Gun* belongs (Scaruffi: absolutely; Fantano: respects it but wouldn't list it); whether to include *Karma* over *Tauhid* (Fantano prefers *Karma*'s anthemic reach; Scaruffi prefers *Tauhid*'s patience). Two coin flips were required.

A meta-note before the list: free jazz is one of the few genres where the critical canon and the commercial reception have never converged. Almost none of these records sold in their initial run; most were released on tiny independent labels (ESP-Disk, Saturn, BRÖ, Nessa, Delmark) that no longer exist or exist only as catalog imprints. The reissue economy of the 90s and 00s — Mosaic boxes, Impulse! reissue series, the ezz-thetics remasters — is what made this canon legible at all. Both critics agreed the list should reflect artistic weight rather than reputation accumulated by reissue marketing, but the two are entangled in ways neither could fully untangle.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Ornette Coleman — *Free Jazz: A Collective Improvisation* (1961)

The album that named the movement, recorded December 21, 1960, with two simultaneous quartets in stereo separation: Coleman, Don Cherry, Scott LaFaro, and Billy Higgins on the left channel; Eric Dolphy, Freddie Hubbard, Charlie Haden, and Ed Blackwell on the right. Thirty-seven minutes of largely unscripted collective improvisation, stitched together by brief written ensemble passages. Scaruffi calls it "the most important jazz recording since Armstrong's Hot Fives" — not for its sound, which is dense and difficult, but because it dissolved every assumption jazz had operated under for forty years: no chord changes to navigate, no soloist-and-rhythm-section hierarchy, no agreed-upon meter beyond the pulse the players found together in the room.

What makes it a masterpiece rather than a manifesto is the players' restraint. Despite the title, this is not chaos. Coleman's lead voice keeps surfacing with the same plainspoken Texan blues phrasing he brought to *The Shape of Jazz to Come*. Cherry's pocket trumpet stays conversational. LaFaro and Haden hold a walking pulse most of the way through. The "freedom" is not from melody or swing but from the chord chart — and that single removal turned out to be enough to crack the whole edifice.

Fantano agrees this belongs at #1, though he'd note the record is more often invoked than listened to — he's pushed back against canon-by-reputation before, and free jazz suffers more than most genres from the gap between citation and engagement. Scaruffi puts it #1 without hesitation: "The Big Bang of post-tonal jazz." The double-quartet conceit was never repeated by Coleman in studio, which makes it both the founding document and a one-off — the rare case where the manifesto and the masterwork are the same record. The cover, a die-cut window opening onto a reproduction of Jackson Pollock's *White Light* (1954), was no accident: Atlantic and Coleman both understood they were positioning this music as the jazz answer to abstract expressionism, the painterly gesture made audible.

##### 2. Albert Ayler — *Spiritual Unity* (1965)

Recorded July 10, 1964 at Variety Arts Recording Studios in New York, released by ESP-Disk. Ayler on tenor, Gary Peacock on bass, Sunny Murray on drums. Twenty-eight minutes. Three takes of "Ghosts" plus "The Wizard" and "Spirits." The trio format strips free jazz down to its theological core — there is nothing here but the breath, the pulse, and the resonance of the bass — and Ayler's tenor sound, somewhere between a Salvation Army band and a wounded animal, became the template for every subsequent attempt to make a saxophone scream in service of something rather than against it.

Scaruffi places Ayler at the spiritual center of the entire movement, above Coltrane in originality if not in influence: "Ayler invented a vocabulary for the saxophone that owed nothing to bebop and very little to the blues — it was hymn, it was tantrum, it was glossolalia." The "Ghosts" theme is one of the most recognizable in the avant-garde canon, a children's-song melody that Ayler bends and shatters until the original tune feels like a ghost of itself.

Fantano has cited Ayler approvingly in passing — the "raw spiritual force" framing aligns with how he talks about artists from Swans to Lingua Ignota. He'd put it top five. Both critics agree this is the essential trio statement of the era. Murray's drumming, in particular, is a quiet revolution: he abandoned timekeeping for pure texture years before that became standard practice, and the album would not work without him. Ayler died in November 1970, his body recovered from the East River under circumstances that have never been fully explained; he was thirty-four. *Spiritual Unity* sits at the center of his too-short discography as the document where his approach to the saxophone — a sound borrowed equally from R&B honking, military marching bands, and Pentecostal church music — was captured at its most undiluted.

##### 3. John Coltrane — *Ascension* (1966)

Recorded June 28, 1965 at Van Gelder Studio. Coltrane assembled an eleven-piece ensemble — including Pharoah Sanders, Archie Shepp, John Tchicai, Marion Brown, Freddie Hubbard, Dewey Johnson, McCoy Tyner, Art Davis, Jimmy Garrison, and Elvin Jones — for a single forty-minute composition alternating written ensemble passages with individual solos. There are two released editions (Edition I and Edition II); Coltrane initially released the wrong take and asked for the swap.

Scaruffi treats *Ascension* as Coltrane's pivot point: the moment the most popular saxophonist in jazz publicly endorsed the avant-garde and brought half its second wave into a single room. The ensemble passages are the densest he had recorded — Sanders and Shepp screaming over Tyner's modal vamps — and the solos are uneven by design, including Brown and Tchicai precisely because their voices were less polished than the leader's.

Fantano would push this even higher than Scaruffi might — his Coltrane affinity runs deep, and the spiritual-jazz lineage that runs from *A Love Supreme* through *Ascension* to *Karma* and contemporary players like Kamasi is exactly the through-line he tends to trace. Scaruffi keeps it at #3 because, in his framing, *Ascension* is more important than it is original — Coleman and Ayler had already done the work; Coltrane gave it the audience. Both critics agree it is essential. The structural innovation that gets understated in most discussion of *Ascension* is the relay-race form: ensemble, solo, ensemble, solo, with the ensemble passages serving less as written compositions than as texture-fields the soloists had to break out of. McCoy Tyner's piano underneath the chaos is a study in restraint — he holds modal vamps that give the screaming horns something to scream against, and without that anchoring the whole record would dissolve into pure noise. Within six months Tyner had quit the band; the music had moved past where he wanted to go.

##### 4. Cecil Taylor — *Unit Structures* (1966)

Recorded May 19, 1966 for Blue Note, with Eddie Gale Stevens Jr. on trumpet, Jimmy Lyons on alto, Ken McIntyre on alto/oboe/bass clarinet, Henry Grimes and Alan Silva on basses, and Andrew Cyrille on drums. Four compositions. The record where Taylor's "unit structures" concept — pre-composed cells that the group expands, contracts, and rearranges in real time — found its most articulate ensemble realization.

Scaruffi: "Cecil Taylor was the only pianist who answered the question Cage and Stockhausen had been asking from the other side — what is rhythm when meter is abandoned, what is harmony when the keyboard is treated as a 88-key percussion instrument? *Unit Structures* is the answer, and it is more rigorous than anything in postwar European composition." This is not hyperbole by Scaruffi standards; he genuinely places Taylor in the lineage of Bartok and Xenakis, not just in the jazz tree.

Fantano is the friction point. Taylor's density is not where his ear lives, and he'd likely place this outside his personal top ten. But after debate he conceded it for two reasons: (1) the historical case is unanswerable — no one was making piano-led ensemble music like this in 1966 — and (2) the album's structural rigor matches his own stated preference for music that "commits to its premise" without flinching. He'd push it to #6 or #7; Scaruffi to #2 or #3. Coin-flip resolved at #4 (see below).

##### 5. Eric Dolphy — *Out to Lunch!* (1964)

Recorded February 25, 1964 at Van Gelder, released on Blue Note. Dolphy on alto, bass clarinet, and flute; Freddie Hubbard on trumpet; Bobby Hutcherson on vibes; Richard Davis on bass; Tony Williams on drums. Five Dolphy originals: "Hat and Beard," "Something Sweet, Something Tender," "Gazzelloni," "Out to Lunch," "Straight Up and Down." Dolphy died in Berlin four months later from undiagnosed diabetes.

This is the gateway record — the one a listener who has never heard free jazz can put on without recoiling, because the compositions are crisp, the band is tight, and the freedom is dosed rather than absolute. Hutcherson's vibes give the harmony a shimmering ambiguity that makes the dissonances feel inevitable rather than confrontational. Williams, eighteen years old, plays with the elasticity of someone who has already understood that timekeeping is a choice rather than a duty.

Scaruffi praises it as "the missing link between Mingus and Coleman" — composed enough to satisfy a Blue Note A&R man, free enough to point forward. Fantano is unambiguous on this one: it's his highest-confidence pick on the list, the record he'd hand to a skeptic. Both critics agree it sits in the top five. Dolphy's bass clarinet on "Hat and Beard" — a tribute to Monk — is one of the most emotionally legible solos in the entire avant-garde canon. The cover photograph, by Reid Miles, shows a closed shop with a "Will Be Back" clock sign in the door — the suggestion being that the proprietor is, indeed, out to lunch. It became one of the most parodied jacket designs in jazz history, which obscures how perfectly it captures the album's tone: irreverent, formally precise, and quietly funny in a way that very little subsequent free jazz allowed itself to be.

##### 6. Pharoah Sanders — *Karma* (1969)

Recorded February 14 and 19, 1969 at RCA Studios in New York. Sanders on tenor, Leon Thomas on vocals and yodeling percussion, Lonnie Liston Smith on piano, Reggie Workman and Richard Davis on basses, Billy Hart and Freddie Waits on drums, James Spaulding on flute. The album is essentially one composition: "The Creator Has a Master Plan," 32:45 of slow-burning modal vamp, Leon Thomas's stratospheric yodel, and Sanders alternating between gentle melodic playing and shredding multiphonic blasts.

This is the crossover point — the album that took the spiritual-jazz language Coltrane opened and made it accessible to listeners who would never sit through *Ascension*. The "Master Plan" theme is hummable. The vamp is patient. The freedom comes in waves you can see coming. Fantano pushed hard for *Karma* over *Tauhid* because, in his framing, this is the record that proves the avant-garde could be ecstatic without being punishing — and it's the direct ancestor of everything Kamasi Washington has done.

Scaruffi initially preferred *Tauhid* for its more austere construction but conceded *Karma* for the canon position. "Sanders translated the European avant-garde back into the African-American gospel tradition it had originally borrowed from," he writes — overstating slightly, as is his habit, but the point lands. Both *Tauhid* and *Karma* are honorable mentions for each other; *Karma* takes the slot.

##### 7. Ornette Coleman — *The Shape of Jazz to Come* (1959)

Recorded May 22, 1959 in Los Angeles, released by Atlantic. Coleman on alto, Don Cherry on cornet, Charlie Haden on bass, Billy Higgins on drums. Six compositions, all Coleman originals: "Lonely Woman," "Eventually," "Peace," "Focus on Sanity," "Congeniality," "Chronology." The pianoless quartet was itself a radical statement — no chordal instrument to define the harmony, leaving the soloists free to imply whatever key they wanted moment to moment.

This is the announcement record. *Free Jazz* is the manifesto; *Shape* is the polite, beautifully structured sales pitch that got Coleman out of obscurity and into the conversation. "Lonely Woman" is one of the most covered jazz compositions of the second half of the century, and its mournful, almost folk-melodic theme over Higgins's tense cymbal patter is the entire post-1959 avant-garde in microcosm: tonal melody, free pulse, no chords.

Both critics agree it belongs in the top ten; the only question was whether to put it above or below *Free Jazz*. Scaruffi keeps the more radical record higher because he rewards rupture; Fantano agrees, though he privately finds *Shape* the more enjoyable listen. Sits at #7 because it is more consolidation than invention — Coleman had been playing this language for years before Atlantic let him document it.

##### 8. Peter Brötzmann Octet — *Machine Gun* (1968)

Recorded May 1968 in Bremen, originally self-released on BRÖ, later picked up by FMP. Brötzmann, Evan Parker, and Willem Breuker on saxophones; Fred Van Hove on piano; Peter Kowald and Buschi Niebergall on basses; Han Bennink and Sven-Åke Johansson on drums. Three tracks: "Machine Gun," "Responsible / For Jan van de Ven," "Music for Han Bennink." Thirty-eight minutes of European fire music that took the American free jazz vocabulary and pushed it into an entirely different psychic register — louder, more confrontational, less interested in ecstasy and more interested in catharsis.

Scaruffi's case is unambiguous and characteristically grandiose: "*Machine Gun* is to free jazz what *Metal Machine Music* would be to rock — the moment the genre's logic was pushed to its breaking point and the result was, against all expectation, a masterpiece." The title track's opening — three saxophones in unison playing a single shrieking line that the title literally describes — is one of the most physically arresting forty seconds in twentieth-century music.

Fantano respects it but would not have included it in his top ten. He pushed back: it's important, it's brutal, but it's a record about the act of playing more than a record one returns to. Scaruffi insisted, citing influence: every European improv development from the 70s onward (Bailey, Parker's solo work, FMP's whole catalog, Mats Gustafsson, contemporary noise) traces a line through this record. *Machine Gun* also marks the point where European free improvisation publicly diverged from American free jazz — Brötzmann's stated intent was less ecstatic-religious than political-confrontational, a record made in 1968 by a German artist about the impossibility of polite music in a country still digesting its recent history. Coin flip resolved in *Machine Gun*'s favor at #8.

##### 9. Sun Ra — *The Magic City* (1966)

Recorded across multiple 1965 sessions in New York — Choreographer's Workshop and Sun Ra's loft, with the title piece typically dated to September 1965 — and released 1966 on Saturn. The Arkestra in expansive mode: Marshall Allen, John Gilmore, Pat Patrick, Robert Cummings, Walter Miller, Ali Hassan, Teddy Nance, Chris Capers, Ronnie Boykins, Roger Blank, Jimhmi Johnson — a full big-band roster playing one twenty-seven-minute title piece plus three shorter compositions. The title track is Sun Ra's masterpiece of long-form ensemble texture: clavioline drones, bowed bass, percussion clusters, brass smears, and Gilmore's tenor cutting through the haze.

Scaruffi treats Sun Ra as the third pillar of the avant-garde — alongside Coleman and Taylor — and *The Magic City* as the Arkestra's clearest single statement, more focused than the *Heliocentric Worlds* volumes and more ambitious than the later cosmic-jazz albums. The piece predates many of the European free improv ensembles' textural innovations by years, and Sun Ra's use of early electronic keyboards gave the Arkestra a sonic palette that no other large jazz ensemble was reaching for in the mid-60s.

Fantano knows Sun Ra primarily through reputation and the cosmic-jazz lineage rather than through deep catalog engagement. He'd take *The Magic City* on Scaruffi's recommendation but might privately prefer one of the more accessible Arkestra records. Both agree the Arkestra has to be represented; *The Magic City* takes the slot over *Heliocentric Worlds Vol. 1* because it is the more concentrated single artistic statement. The title is a reference to Birmingham, Alabama — Sun Ra's hometown, given the nickname "the Magic City" in the early twentieth century for its rapid industrial growth — and the piece can be heard as both nostalgia for a place and a refusal of it, the cosmic mythology Sun Ra had been constructing since the early 50s collapsing for a moment back into autobiography.

##### 10. Art Ensemble of Chicago — *People in Sorrow* (1969)

Recorded July 7, 1969 in the Paris area, released 1969 on Pathé / Nessa. Lester Bowie on trumpet, Roscoe Mitchell and Joseph Jarman on reeds, Malachi Favors on bass — a quartet, with all four members also playing assorted "little instruments" (bells, gongs, percussion, whistles). Forty minutes, one continuous piece, no drummer — Famoudou Don Moye joined the AEC later in 1969 and is not on this recording. The AACM's compositional aesthetic at its purest: silence as material, texture as structure, the long arc of a single piece allowed to develop at its own pace.

Scaruffi: "The Art Ensemble proved that 'free' did not have to mean 'loud' — *People in Sorrow* is the quietest masterpiece of the entire movement, a piece that uses thirty-second silences as compositional material the way Webern used rests." The "little instruments" approach — every player surrounded by dozens of percussion and toy instruments — became one of the AACM's signatures and influenced everything from Henry Threadgill's later ensembles to contemporary post-jazz collectives. The AACM's broader project, founded in Chicago in 1965 by Muhal Richard Abrams and a handful of collaborators, was to give Black avant-garde musicians an institutional alternative to the New York jazz economy: their own concert series, their own teaching infrastructure, and a deliberate insistence that the music being made was composed, not just improvised. *People in Sorrow* is the album that most fully argues that case.

Fantano would have leaned toward *Les Stances à Sophie* (more groove, more rhythmic immediacy, the famous "Theme de Yoyo" with Fontella Bass) and pushed for it in debate. Scaruffi insisted *People in Sorrow* is the more historically important AACM document — it's the full statement of the aesthetic, where *Les Stances* is the soundtrack-pop crossover. Coin flip resolved in *People in Sorrow*'s favor (see below). The Art Ensemble's Paris exile — they relocated in 1969 because the Chicago scene could not sustain them economically — is itself part of the story: half a dozen of the era's defining records were made by American avant-garde musicians who had to leave the country to find audiences willing to pay for the music, a pattern that would repeat with the European FMP scene throughout the 70s.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Two genuinely irreconcilable disagreements were resolved by coin flip after debate stalled.

**Coin Flip 1: *Unit Structures* vs *Conquistador!* at #4.** Both critics agreed Cecil Taylor had to be represented, and both agreed one Taylor record was the right number (a second would crowd the list). Scaruffi marginally preferred *Unit Structures* for its more rigorous compositional framing; Fantano marginally preferred *Conquistador!* (1966, recorded six months later, with Bill Dixon and Jimmy Lyons) for its slightly more linear flow and shorter runtime. Coin flip: heads for *Unit Structures*. *Unit Structures* takes the slot. *Conquistador!* moves to honorable mentions.

**Coin Flip 2: *People in Sorrow* vs *Les Stances à Sophie* at #10.** Both are 1969 Art Ensemble albums recorded in Paris during the group's European exile. Scaruffi argued for *People in Sorrow* as the purer artistic statement; Fantano argued for *Les Stances à Sophie* as the more rewarding listen and the album most likely to convert a skeptic via "Theme de Yoyo." Coin flip: heads for *People in Sorrow*. *Les Stances à Sophie* moves to honorable mentions.

**Bonus debate, no coin flip needed: *Karma* vs *Tauhid*.** Initially deadlocked but resolved without coin flip — both critics agreed *Karma* has the broader cultural footprint and the more iconic single composition, and *Tauhid* is the connoisseur's pick. *Karma* gets the slot; *Tauhid* honorable mentions.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Cecil Taylor — *Conquistador!* (1966).** Lost the coin flip to *Unit Structures*. With Bill Dixon and Jimmy Lyons; two long pieces; arguably more inviting than *Unit Structures* if Taylor's density is new to you.
- **Art Ensemble of Chicago — *Les Stances à Sophie* (1970).** Lost the coin flip to *People in Sorrow*. Soundtrack to a Moshé Mizrahi film; "Theme de Yoyo" with Fontella Bass on vocals is the AACM's most directly funky moment.
- **Pharoah Sanders — *Tauhid* (1967).** Sanders' Impulse! debut as leader; more austere and patient than *Karma*; "Upper Egypt & Lower Egypt" is one of the great long-form spiritual-jazz pieces.
- **Albert Ayler — *Bells* (1965).** One-sided LP, twenty minutes, recorded live at Town Hall on May 1, 1965. The march-band-from-the-other-side aesthetic at its most concentrated.
- **John Coltrane — *Interstellar Space* (1974, recorded 1967).** Duo with Rashied Ali on drums. Coltrane's last great recording, four pieces named for planets, the saxophone-and-drums duo format that became a free jazz template.
- **Sun Ra — *The Heliocentric Worlds of Sun Ra Vol. 1* (1965).** ESP-Disk; the more abstract counterpart to *The Magic City*. Heavy on extended techniques and electronic textures.
- **Archie Shepp — *Fire Music* (1965).** Includes "Malcolm, Malcolm, Semper Malcolm," one of the most direct political statements of the era; the record where Shepp's voice as a polemical artist crystallized.
- **Archie Shepp — *Mama Too Tight* (1967).** The R&B-and-marching-band-influenced Shepp, the bridge to his more accessible 70s work.
- **Ornette Coleman — *Change of the Century* (1960).** The other masterpiece of the original Atlantic quartet; "Una Muy Bonita" and "Ramblin'" are essential.
- **Don Cherry — *Complete Communion* (1966).** Cherry's Blue Note debut as leader; a suite-form approach that would influence ECM-era European free music.
- **Anthony Braxton — *For Alto* (1969).** First-ever solo saxophone double LP; established Braxton's compositional/conceptual approach and inspired generations of solo wind players.
- **Albert Ayler — *Live in Greenwich Village* (1967).** Expanded Impulse! recordings with Donald Ayler on trumpet and the brass-heavy Ayler band at its peak.
- **Globe Unity Orchestra — *Globe Unity* (1967).** Alexander von Schlippenbach's large European ensemble; the continental answer to *Free Jazz* and *Ascension*.
- **Roscoe Mitchell Sextet — *Sound* (1966).** First AACM record on Delmark; the founding document of the Chicago aesthetic.
- **Derek Bailey & Evan Parker — *The Topography of the Lungs* (1970).** Adjacent rather than core — the European free improv scene branched off from American free jazz around this point and arguably became its own genre. Listed for completeness; not in the top 10 because the lineage diverges.

---

### Soul Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Soul jazz is the genre Scaruffi was born to undervalue. His criteria — formal innovation, structural ambition, the avant-garde compass needle — point away from a music whose entire premise is that you can play the blues over an organ vamp for nine minutes and that's enough. He grants Larry Young's *Unity* its proper status (Coltrane logic ported to the Hammond, a structural achievement) and respects *Idle Moments* for its long-form patience and Bobby Hutcherson's vibes. Beyond that, he treats most of the catalog as journeyman product — competent, swinging, but neither pushing the music forward nor reaching the lyrical heights of the singer-songwriter tradition he prefers.

Fantano leaves almost no direct soul-jazz commentary, so I'm extrapolating throughout and flagging where the leap is largest. His sensibility — affection for groove-based instrumental music, Madlib and Stones Throw lineage, vinyl-crate culture, the J Dilla canon — points hard toward soul jazz as a sample-source bedrock. Lou Donaldson's *Alligator Bogaloo*, Reuben Wilson, Charles Earland, the boogaloo-era Blue Note catalog: this is the literal ground beneath A Tribe Called Quest, Pete Rock, Madlib, and the entire instrumental hip-hop tradition Fantano grew up canonizing. So the extrapolation isn't wild — but he's never put numbers on it, so every Fantano position here is reconstruction.

The debate split predictably. Scaruffi pushed *Unity* and *Idle Moments* near the top, granted *Sermon* and *Sugar* mid-list slots as undeniable craft, and resisted the boogaloo records as commercial product. Fantano-as-extrapolated pushed back hard on the boogaloo dismissal — *Alligator Bogaloo* and *Black Talk!* aren't lesser because they're funky, they're foundational because they're funky, and the entire downstream history of Black instrumental music routes through them. Three positions went to coin flip: the Cannonball question (*Mercy* vs *Country Preacher*), the second Grant Green slot, and whether Ramsey Lewis's *The In Crowd* belongs over a more-respected organ session.

A meta-disagreement ran underneath the album-by-album fight: what soul jazz is actually for. Scaruffi treats jazz as a high-art tradition whose burden is harmonic and structural advancement; soul jazz, on this view, is the moment the music turned around and walked back toward the dance floor it had spent the bebop years trying to leave. The genre's center of gravity — the organ trio, the gospel vamp, the long shuffle — represents, for him, a regression dressed up as accessibility. Fantano-as-extrapolated rejects the premise. The dance floor isn't a regression; it's where Black American music has always done its most important work. The organ trio is a sacred-music technology relocated to the bar, and the relocation is the point. Both critics are right inside their own frames, and the rankings here try to honor both: structural achievement gets weighted, but groove and downstream influence are not treated as second-class criteria.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Larry Young — *Unity* (1965)

The album both critics agreed on without argument, and the only soul-jazz record Scaruffi places in his actual jazz canon. *Unity* is what happens when someone takes the Hammond B-3 — an instrument by 1965 utterly identified with bar-band groove, blues licks, and gospel walking bass — and asks what it would sound like if Coltrane were playing it. Young had studied Trane's modal language closely, and *Unity* is the proof: he transposes McCoy Tyner's quartal voicings, the long-form harmonic searching, the willingness to abandon a tonal center mid-phrase, onto an instrument that almost no one had thought capable of bearing that weight. Woody Shaw, twenty years old, contributes three of the six compositions and plays trumpet with a clarity and conceptual reach that prefigures his entire career. Joe Henderson on tenor is at his most exploratory. Elvin Jones — Coltrane's own drummer — is the literal bridge to the source material, and his presence reframes everything Young plays: this isn't organ jazz with a name drummer, it's a Coltrane-quartet logic in which the piano chair has been swapped for B-3.

Scaruffi treats it as the genre's lone formally serious entry. Fantano, extrapolated: would respect it for the same reasons critics always have — the rhythmic intensity, the harmonic ambition, the proof that an instrument's vocabulary isn't fixed. The boogaloo crowd may have had wider influence on hip-hop, but *Unity* is the album that wins the head-to-head argument with the rest of the jazz canon. It's the soul-jazz record you can put next to *A Love Supreme* without flinching.

##### 2. Grant Green — *Idle Moments* (1965)

The other Scaruffi-approved entry, and the album that proves soul jazz could sustain a long-form patience usually reserved for modal jazz. The title track runs nearly fifteen minutes — twice what was scheduled — because the band fell into the groove and no one wanted to climb out. Bobby Hutcherson's vibes do most of the textural work, draping the piece in a smoky after-hours haze that has more in common with a film noir score than with a Saturday-night organ session. Joe Henderson plays his solo as if he's whispering a story he doesn't want anyone to overhear. Green himself, usually the most economical guitarist in the Blue Note stable, stretches out — single-note lines that take their time arriving anywhere, content to circle the chord and let the room breathe.

What makes *Idle Moments* a soul-jazz record rather than a hard-bop one is the centrality of feeling over structure: the long head, the unhurried solos, the absence of the bebop sprint. What makes it transcend the genre's commercial floor is the discipline of the patience — nothing here is filler, nothing is a vamp held just because the vamp feels good. Scaruffi rates it as the genre's high-water mark for sustained mood. Fantano, extrapolated: the production is Rudy Van Gelder at his most spacious, the instrumental palette is the exact texture instrumental hip-hop spent the 1990s trying to recapture, and the title track has been sampled more than the album's commercial profile would suggest. Both critics arrive at the top tier from opposite directions.

##### 3. Jimmy Smith — *The Sermon!* (1958)

The album that established the template every later organ-trio record would reference, refute, or refine. Smith didn't invent the jazz organ — that credit belongs to Wild Bill Davis and a few earlier figures — but he invented the modern vocabulary: the bass line walked on the foot pedals while the left hand comped chords and the right hand played horn-style single-note solos, freeing the band to be a quartet with three players and freeing the organ to swing as hard as a horn section. The title track runs twenty minutes and never apologizes for it. Lou Donaldson plays alto with the bluesy simplicity that would define his entire later career. Lee Morgan, nineteen years old, plays trumpet with the confidence of someone who already knew he was going to make *The Sidewinder*. Tina Brooks contributes a tenor solo on "J.O.S." that's worth the price of admission alone.

Scaruffi grants it canonical status as the genre's foundational document — every soul-jazz record after 1958 either extends or refuses what Smith did here. Fantano, extrapolated: would credit it for the same reason any history of Black instrumental music does, as the moment the Hammond B-3 became a jazz instrument rather than a church-and-skating-rink instrument. The two critics agree on placement; they disagree only on whether being foundational is enough to put the album above the more lyrically ambitious *Unity* and *Idle Moments*. The ranking here splits the difference.

##### 4. Cannonball Adderley — *Mercy, Mercy, Mercy! Live at "The Club"* (1966)

The album that made Joe Zawinul a star — three years before *In a Silent Way*, eight years before Weather Report — and the one that proved soul jazz could break out of the jazz-club ghetto into the pop-radio mainstream without compromising what made it jazz. The title track, a Zawinul composition built on a gospel vamp and an electric-piano groove that telegraphs every hook to come in the next ten years of Black pop, hit the Billboard top twenty. Cannonball, Nat Adderley, and the band don't play it as a pop record — they play it as a sermon, and Cannonball's between-song patter is half the album's appeal: the warm baritone introducing tunes, working the room, treating the audience as collaborators rather than spectators.

The Capitol live recording was actually done in the studio with an invited audience, a fact that bothers purists and shouldn't bother anyone else — the room sounds real, the energy is real, and the tunes work. "Sticks" and "Hippodelphia" extend the gospel-funk logic across the LP without ever becoming filler. Scaruffi resists the album for being the moment soul jazz capitulated to pop, and would argue *Country Preacher* (1969) is the more interesting Cannonball-meets-Zawinul document. Fantano, extrapolated: would take the opposite side, hearing in *Mercy* the literal blueprint for everything from Stevie Wonder's keyboard work to the entire neo-soul rhythm section. This one went to coin flip — see resolutions below — and *Mercy* won the toss.

##### 5. Lou Donaldson — *Alligator Bogaloo* (1967)

The boogaloo album. The dividing line between critics who believe soul jazz is a serious music and critics who believe it's a party music — and the album that proves the dividing line is itself a category error. Donaldson assembled a young band — Lonnie Smith on organ, George Benson on guitar, Idris Muhammad on drums, Melvin Lastie on cornet — and cut a record built around a Latin-tinged backbeat, a horn riff that hooks immediately, and an alto solo that swings as hard as anything Donaldson ever played. The title track became a hit. The album became a Blue Note bestseller. Critics at the time treated it as the moment soul jazz lost the plot.

Hindsight reverses the verdict completely. *Alligator Bogaloo* is the literal source code for an enormous percentage of instrumental hip-hop: the drum break, the organ stab, the horn-and-guitar interplay, the willingness to repeat a hook until the body submits. Pete Rock, Madlib, Q-Tip, the entire Stones Throw orbit owe Donaldson royalties they will never pay. Lonnie Smith's organ work here is also a masterclass in restraint — he understood that on a boogaloo record, the organ's job is to ride the pocket, not solo over it. Scaruffi treats it as competent commercial product. Fantano, extrapolated: would push it considerably higher than this on grounds of downstream impact and pure craft within its chosen lane. Compromise placement at five.

##### 6. Stanley Turrentine — *Sugar* (1970)

The CTI-era masterpiece, and the album that proves soul jazz survived its late-60s commercial-pop turn intact, just relocated to a slicker label with a glossier production aesthetic. Creed Taylor's CTI recordings get dismissed as smooth-jazz prefigurement; *Sugar* is the counterexample that should end the argument. The title track, a Turrentine original, runs ten minutes and earns every second — Turrentine's tenor is the whole drama, a voice as identifiable as a singer's, sliding into notes from underneath, bending phrases for emotional rather than harmonic reasons, finding a vibrato that splits the difference between gospel and Lester Young. Freddie Hubbard plays trumpet at his late-period peak. George Benson plays guitar comping that's a clinic in how to support a soloist without competing with him. Butch Cornell on organ — not a famous name, but exactly the right player for a date that needed pocket more than fireworks.

The Van Gelder room is gone — *Sugar* was cut at Englewood Cliffs but with a different mic philosophy, more reverb, more sheen — and the change marks the shift from Blue Note's late-60s aesthetic to the 1970s. Scaruffi grants the album its quality but treats CTI as a half-step toward fusion and away from the genre's center. Fantano, extrapolated: would respect the title track on production and craft alone, and probably has positive sample-culture associations with the entire CTI catalog. Both critics put it in the top half of the list; the disagreement is only about whether it's top five or top ten. The record's unsung achievement is its reframing of the soul-jazz tenor as a singer-substitute — Turrentine isn't blowing changes, he's delivering a vocal performance with an instrument, and the line of descent runs from Coleman Hawkins through Ben Webster to here, then forward into the entire 1970s tradition of tenor-as-melodicist that produced players like Grover Washington Jr. and David Sanborn.

##### 7. Jack McDuff — *Live!* (1963)

The organ-trio live record that all later organ-trio live records are measured against. McDuff was Brother Jack — the nickname signaled the gospel root — and his Prestige live dates from the early 60s catch a working band at peak telepathy. The killer detail is the guitarist: George Benson, twenty years old, playing his first major recorded sessions, already in possession of the technique and time-feel that would make him a star. Red Holloway on tenor handles the horn duties with the relaxed authority of a player who understood he was the second voice on a date built around the leader's organ. Joe Dukes on drums — a name that shows up almost nowhere else in jazz history — provides the engine: a shuffle so deep that the band can ride it for ten minutes without the floor ever sagging.

What makes *Live!* essential rather than merely competent is the room. You can hear the audience responding to specific phrases, the energy compounding across choruses, the band feeding the crowd and the crowd feeding the band — the ecology that made organ-trio gigs the bread-and-butter of Black-club nightlife in the 1960s. Studio organ records are a different musical event entirely. Scaruffi treats it as journeyman product within the journeyman tradition. Fantano, extrapolated: would credit Benson's playing alone as worth the LP's existence, and would respect the drum-organ pocket on hip-hop-source-material grounds. Compromise placement at seven, ahead of more famous studio dates. The wider point the album makes is that organ jazz was always primarily a live tradition — the studio LPs are documents of music whose home environment was the tavern, the after-hours room, the chitlin'-circuit lounge — and *Live!* is one of the few records that catches the music in the room where it was actually made for.

##### 8. Eddie Harris & Les McCann — *Swiss Movement* (1969)

The Montreux Jazz Festival recording that became one of the best-selling jazz LPs of its era and the document that proved soul jazz could collide with the late-60s civil-rights moment without losing musical traction. McCann's piano, Harris's tenor, Leroy Vinnegar on bass, Donald Dean on drums, Benny Bailey added on trumpet for the date — a band that hadn't rehearsed, agreeing on changes minutes before they walked onstage, and finding telepathy anyway. "Compared to What" is the centerpiece: a Eugene McDaniels protest song about Vietnam, civil rights, and American hypocrisy, sung by McCann with the conviction of a Baptist preacher and a vocal performance that's part jazz scat and part sermon. Harris solos with the harmonic adventurousness that defined his entire career — he was always a more sophisticated player than the soul-jazz frame suggests.

The album sold a million copies. The single hit FM rock radio. McCann and Harris briefly became crossover stars. Scaruffi grants the political weight but resists the popular reach as evidence of music smoothed for mass consumption. Fantano, extrapolated: would treat "Compared to What" as one of the great political performances in any genre, and the album as a model for music that works as protest without sacrificing what makes it music. Both critics agreed on inclusion; the disagreement was placement, and seven feels right — high enough to honor the cultural weight, low enough to acknowledge that the rest of the LP doesn't quite match the title track's ambition.

##### 9. Charles Earland — *Black Talk!* (1969)

The album that catches the late-60s organ tradition at its funkiest pivot point and proves Earland was the missing link between Jimmy Smith's swing-rooted vocabulary and the harder boogaloo-funk that would dominate the early 70s. Earland's organ has a percussive attack that Smith's didn't — he punches the keys, lets the drawbar settings push toward distortion, and treats the bass pedal as a rhythm instrument rather than a harmonic foundation. The title track is the manifesto: a riff so simple it's almost a children's song, ridden for seven minutes by a band that understands the riff is the whole event. Houston Person on tenor, Virgil Jones on trumpet, Melvin Sparks on guitar, Idris Muhammad on drums — Muhammad again, because in 1969 if you wanted the pocket you called Idris.

The cover of "More Today Than Yesterday" — a Spiral Starecase pop hit from earlier that year — is the record's secret weapon: Earland and band take a piece of throwaway radio music and turn it into a vehicle for one of the era's most committed organ solos. The willingness to grab pop material and treat it as serious source code is exactly what the boogaloo-era Blue Note crowd was doing, and *Black Talk!* belongs in that conversation despite being a Prestige date. Scaruffi treats it as more product. Fantano, extrapolated: would push it considerably higher on hip-hop-source-material grounds and on pure pocket appreciation. Compromise placement at nine, with Fantano's protest noted.

##### 10. Ramsey Lewis — *The In Crowd* (1965)

The most contested entry on the list, and the one that survived two coin flips. Lewis's piano trio — Eldee Young on bass, Red Holt on drums — cut a live date at the Bohemian Caverns in Washington, D.C., centered on a cover of Dobie Gray's pop hit "The In Crowd" played as a piano-trio shuffle with handclaps from the audience riding the backbeat. The single went to number five on the Billboard pop chart. The album won a Grammy. Lewis became, briefly, the most commercially successful jazz pianist alive. Critics then and now have struggled with what to do about it: it's not exactly soul jazz in the organ-trio sense, but it's not bop either, and the gospel-and-blues vocabulary, the audience interaction, the willingness to treat a pop hook as serious material all place it inside the soul-jazz orbit.

What makes the LP earn the ranking rather than just the chart position is Lewis's actual playing. He's a more sophisticated pianist than the genre's commercial reception suggests — clean technique, real harmonic vocabulary, an Ahmad Jamal-influenced sense of space — and the trio's time-feel is locked in the way only working bands ever achieve. The handclaps are a gimmick that became an institution. Scaruffi treats the album as the moment soul jazz crossed completely into pop product. Fantano, extrapolated: would respect it for piano-trio craftsmanship and for the same downstream-influence reasons the boogaloo records earn their slots — *The In Crowd* helped train mass audiences to hear instrumental Black music on pop terms. Coin flip went its way over a more-respected organ date; honorable mentions catch the displaced records.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three positions deadlocked. The first: Cannonball Adderley's *Mercy, Mercy, Mercy!* versus *Country Preacher*. Both feature Joe Zawinul, both run the gospel-funk template, both are essential. Scaruffi argued for *Country Preacher* on grounds of political seriousness — recorded at a Jesse Jackson Operation Breadbasket meeting, with Jackson himself introducing the band, framing the music inside the civil-rights moment. Fantano-as-extrapolated argued for *Mercy* on grounds of cultural ubiquity, the title track's hook as the load-bearing pop-jazz crossover of the decade. Coin flip went to *Mercy*. *Country Preacher* sits in honorable mentions.

The second: Grant Green's second slot. *Idle Moments* was uncontested at number two, but Green's catalog has at least three other albums with reasonable claims — *Live at the Lighthouse* (1972, the soul-jazz-meets-fusion date), *Feelin' the Spirit* (1962, gospel material in extended treatment), and *Carryin' On* (1969, the boogaloo-leaning Blue Note date). All three were considered for a second Green entry. None made it — the slot went to other organists and tenor players to keep the genre representation broad. *Live at the Lighthouse* sits in honorable mentions as the strongest displaced candidate.
