The third: Ramsey Lewis's *The In Crowd* versus Brother Jack McDuff's *The Honeydripper* (1961) for the tenth slot. McDuff's date with Grant Green and Jimmy Forrest is a stronger pure-organ-trio document than *Live!*, and arguably belongs on the list ahead of either *Live!* or *The In Crowd*. Scaruffi favored *Honeydripper* on craftsmanship grounds. Fantano-as-extrapolated favored *The In Crowd* on cultural-reach grounds. Coin flip went to *The In Crowd*; *The Honeydripper* sits high in honorable mentions and would be the first replacement on any honest revision of this list.

#### Honorable Mentions

Cannonball Adderley — *Country Preacher* (1969). The Jesse Jackson Operation Breadbasket date, with Joe Zawinul writing and the band inside the civil-rights moment. Loses the coin flip to *Mercy* by a hair.

Jimmy Smith — *Back at the Chicken Shack* (1960). The other essential Smith Blue Note, with Stanley Turrentine on tenor and Kenny Burrell on guitar. Almost interchangeable with *The Sermon* on quality; *Sermon* wins on historical priority.

Jimmy Smith — *House Party* (1959). Smith's other late-50s Blue Note, same band logic, same canonical status. Genre representation rules kept it out — three Smith records on a list of ten would be excessive.

Brother Jack McDuff — *The Honeydripper* (1961). With Grant Green and Jimmy Forrest. The strongest displaced candidate, and Scaruffi's pick for the tenth slot.

Grant Green — *Live at the Lighthouse* (1972). The late-Green soul-jazz-into-fusion document, with Claude Bartee and Bobby Hutcherson making rare appearances together.

Reuben Wilson — *Love Bug* (1967). The other essential boogaloo-era Blue Note organ date, with Lee Morgan on trumpet.

Lonnie Smith — *Think!* (1968). The Lonnie Smith Blue Note debut as a leader, with Lee Morgan and Melvin Sparks. The Lonnie-as-leader catalog deserves more attention than this list provides.

Eddie Harris — *The Electrifying Eddie Harris* (1968). The album with "Listen Here" — Harris's other major hit and a key sample-culture document.

Les McCann — *Much Les* (1968). McCann's solo work outside the *Swiss Movement* context, less famous and arguably more harmonically interesting.

Lou Donaldson — *Mr. Shing-A-Ling* (1967). The other essential late-60s Donaldson boogaloo record.

Jimmy McGriff — *Electric Funk* (1969). The third-tier-name organ date that's actually as good as most of the second-tier-name ones.

Richard "Groove" Holmes — *Soul Message* (1965). With "Misty" as the hit. Holmes deserves more historical attention than he gets.

Shirley Scott — *Queen of the Organ* (1965). The female organ player no soul-jazz canon should omit, with Stanley Turrentine on tenor.

Larry Young — *Into Somethin'* (1964). Young's Blue Note debut as a leader, the precursor to *Unity* and almost as good — Sam Rivers on tenor, Grant Green on guitar, Elvin Jones already in place. A second Larry Young entry was considered and cut for genre-balance reasons.

Stanley Turrentine — *That's Where It's At* (1962). The other essential Turrentine soul-jazz date, with Les McCann on piano. Different aesthetic from *Sugar* — Blue Note rather than CTI, smaller room, blues-rooted rather than CTI-glossed — and arguably the more honest record of the two.

Hank Mobley — *Soul Station* (1960). On the borderline between hard bop and soul jazz; included by some critics in the soul-jazz canon, by others in the hard-bop canon. Listed here for completeness; the hard-bop volume is the more natural home.

Bobby Timmons — *This Here Is Bobby Timmons* (1960). Timmons wrote "Moanin'" for Art Blakey and "Dis Here" for Cannonball — the two compositions that arguably defined the soul-jazz vocabulary at the piano. His own trio dates are underrated.

Wes Montgomery — *Boss Guitar* (1963). With Mel Rhyne on organ and Jimmy Cobb on drums. Montgomery's organ-trio date sits adjacent to the soul-jazz tradition rather than inside it, but the record belongs in any wide reading of the genre.

Gene Ammons — *Boss Tenor* (1960). The other essential 1960 tenor-and-organ document, with Tommy Flanagan on piano rather than an organist — but the Ammons phrasing, the blues-rooted vocabulary, the willingness to ride a single feeling for a full chorus, all sit inside the soul-jazz orbit. Ammons is one of the genre's quiet load-bearing figures.

Houston Person & Etta Jones — *various 1970s Muse dates*. The Person-Jones partnership is the underrated late-soul-jazz tradition, carrying the genre's vocabulary into the 1980s past the point where the major labels had stopped paying attention. No single LP fits cleanly here, but the body of work belongs in the conversation.

---

### Latin Jazz / Afro-Cuban Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*
*Cross-listed with Volume 11 (Pop & Latin); this is the anchor list.*

#### Debate Summary

Latin Jazz is two parallel histories that periodically collide. The Afro-Cuban lineage runs from the rumba and son montuno of pre-revolution Havana through Mario Bauzá's introduction of Chano Pozo to Dizzy Gillespie in 1947, and continues through Machito, Tito Puente, Eddie Palmieri, Ray Barretto, Irakere, and the Cuban exile virtuosi (Sandoval, D'Rivera, Rubalcaba). The Brazilian lineage runs from samba canção through bossa nova (Jobim, João Gilberto), the studio summit with Stan Getz, and the boundary-shredding work of Hermeto Pascoal, Airto, and Flora Purim. The two streams share a rhythmic instinct — clave on one side, partido alto on the other — but the harmonic temperaments diverge. Cuban jazz is hot, percussive, often blues-adjacent; Brazilian jazz is cool, harmonically dense, closer to French impressionism than to bebop.

Scaruffi's Latin canon is narrow but emphatic. He treats *Getz/Gilberto* as a near-perfect record, considers Hermeto Pascoal one of the great composers of the second half of the twentieth century, and credits Chano Pozo as a foundational force whose contribution to Gillespie's late-'40s big band is structurally inseparable from the birth of Afro-Cuban jazz. He has substantial regard for Eddie Palmieri's most adventurous work (*The Sun of Latin Music*, *Unfinished Masterpiece*) and treats Irakere as a serious electric-fusion proposition rather than a Cuban novelty. He is cooler on Tito Puente as a singles artist and on the post-1980 Cuban virtuosi, whose technique he sometimes finds more impressive than their compositional voice.

Fantano's footprint here is genuinely thin. He has covered Brazilian-adjacent material in passing, expressed enthusiasm for Hermeto in scattered comments, and reviewed contemporary Latin-tradition records (Miguel Zenón, Arturo O'Farrill's late work) with respect, but there is no Fantano canonical Latin Jazz list. **EXTRAPOLATED throughout — flagged on every entry.** Where his temperament is legible (preference for adventurous structure, suspicion of glossy production, openness to diaspora hybrids), I project it; where it isn't, I default to Scaruffi-weighted consensus.

The debate split clean. Both critics agree on *Getz/Gilberto*, on the Chano Pozo / Gillespie sessions as foundational, on Hermeto as a major figure, and on Eddie Palmieri's *The Sun of Latin Music* as the high point of '70s Nuyorican fusion. Disagreement clusters around the mambo-era Machito/Puente axis (Scaruffi treats it as historically essential, Fantano-extrapolated as period-locked), around Sergio Mendes (Scaruffi cool, Fantano-extrapolated cooler), and around the question of whether Cuban virtuoso piano (Rubalcaba, Camilo) deserves slots ahead of Brazilian-tradition records by Jobim solo, Milton Nascimento's jazz-adjacent work, or Airto. Three coin flips resolved below.

A note on scope. Bossa nova proper — the Gilberto solo records, the Jobim songbook as performed by Brazilian singers, the Roberto Menescal–Carlos Lyra Rio scene of 1958–1962 — is being held for Volume 11. Latin Jazz here is the cross-cultural product: jazz musicians playing Latin material, Latin musicians playing jazz, and the hybrid forms that emerged from sustained contact between the two traditions. *Getz/Gilberto* is the canonical exception that anchors both lists; it is treated here as the Brazilian–jazz summit rather than as a bossa-nova record per se. By the same logic, the descarga tradition (Cachao, Bebo Valdés, the late-'50s Havana sessions) is acknowledged in honorable mentions as foundational lineage but not represented in the main ten, since the descarga is structurally a Cuban form that occasionally borrowed jazz vocabulary rather than a hybrid in either critic's sense.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Stan Getz / João Gilberto — *Getz/Gilberto* (1964)

The album that introduced bossa nova to the Anglo-American world and, in Scaruffi's reading, one of the most perfectly realized records in jazz history. Recorded over two days in March 1963 at A&R Studios in New York with Antonio Carlos Jobim on piano, Tommy Williams on bass, Milton Banana on drums, Getz on tenor, and João Gilberto on guitar and vocals, it sounds nothing like the marketing implied: there is no big production, no string sweetener, no concession to American pop. It is a chamber record. Gilberto's voice is so quiet you can hear him breathe between syllables; Jobim's piano is sparser than on any of his own albums; Getz plays with a restraint that none of his bebop contemporaries could have managed.

The Astrud Gilberto vocal on "The Girl from Ipanema" was an accident of session logistics — she was at the studio because João needed someone to sing the English lyric and she happened to know it — and became the most famous bossa nova performance ever recorded. The album's actual masterpiece is "Corcovado," whose harmonic motion is so smooth that the modulations register as breathing rather than as events, and "Desafinado," where Getz's tone is so liquid it seems to dissolve into Jobim's chords.

Scaruffi treats this as the canonical Brazilian-jazz summit and one of the few records on which Getz's lyricism is matched by material worthy of it. **Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — no direct review located, but the record is so universally canonical and so structurally tasteful that resistance would be perverse; assumed high mark, flagged.** The criticism worth registering is that *Getz/Gilberto* is the gateway, not the destination — Jobim's solo records and Gilberto's late-'50s Brazilian sides go deeper into the form, and the bossa nova movement was substantially over by 1964 in Brazil itself, where the next wave (tropicália, MPB) was already gathering. But as the moment two traditions met without either one dimming the other, it remains unsurpassed, and as the record that opened American ears to a non-American non-African non-European form on its own terms — without the exoticist framing that had marked earlier American encounters with Brazilian music — its cultural importance is hard to overstate.

##### 2. Hermeto Pascoal — *Slaves Mass* (1977)

Recorded in Los Angeles for Warner Bros. with Airto Moreira, Flora Purim, Ron Carter, Alphonso Johnson, Laudir de Oliveira, and a rotating cast, *Slaves Mass* is the record on which Hermeto's omnivorous compositional imagination finally found a sympathetic American studio. The title piece is built on overdubbed pig vocalizations — Hermeto recorded actual pigs — woven into a polyrhythmic texture that resolves into a hymn-like melody. "Cherry Jam" is a fusion track that swings harder than anything Weather Report cut in the same year. "Just Listen" is a four-minute solo electric piano meditation that anticipates Lyle Mays by a decade.

Scaruffi places Hermeto in the small group of late-twentieth-century composers — alongside Zappa, Sun Ra, and a handful of European serialists — whose work refuses every available genre slot. The Brazilian rhythmic instinct is there, but the harmonic vocabulary is closer to Messiaen than to Jobim, and the willingness to use any sound source (kettle, child's voice, livestock) as a compositional element puts him outside the jazz tradition even as he obviously belongs to it. *Slaves Mass* is the most accessible point of entry to a discography that runs to forty-plus albums of genuinely difficult music.

**Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — Hermeto has had a critical resurgence in the streaming era and Fantano has spoken approvingly of him in passing; assumed strong mark, flagged.** The reservation: this is a virtuoso-band record whose compositional ambition occasionally overreaches its own performances, and the production has the dry late-'70s Warner Bros. tightness that some listeners find airless. But as a single document of one of Latin music's most original minds, it is essential. Hermeto's actual peak as a composer is arguably distributed across the 1976–1985 stretch — *Zabumbê-bum-á*, *Cérebro Magnético*, the Som da Gente sessions — but those records are progressively harder to source and were largely unavailable outside Brazil for decades. *Slaves Mass* remains the diplomat: the record that exports the Hermeto vocabulary at full strength while still functioning as an album rather than as a private-press conceptual document.

##### 3. Eddie Palmieri — *The Sun of Latin Music* (1974)

The first record to win the Latin Music Grammy, and, in Scaruffi's reading, the most ambitious salsa-jazz fusion ever released. Palmieri is a pianist whose touch combines McCoy Tyner's modal density with the percussive attack of son montuno, and on *Sun* he assembled a band — including the seventeen-year-old Lalo Rodríguez on vocals, Barry Rogers on trombone, and a horn section that reads more like a hard-bop date than a salsa record — to execute compositions that move freely between charanga, descarga, and modal jazz. "Un Día Bonito" is a seventeen-minute suite that opens with a long abstract piano introduction before locking into the clave; "Nunca Contigo" is a vocal feature whose horn arrangement is closer to Gil Evans than to Tito Puente.

Scaruffi treats this as Palmieri's masterpiece and as proof that the Nuyorican salsa tradition could produce composition on the scale of Mingus or Strayhorn without sacrificing its dance function. The record is genuinely rhythmic — you can dance to it — but it is also harmonically restless in a way that anticipates the Cuban fusion of Irakere and the Spanish Harlem avant-garde of the 1980s.

**Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — no review located, but the structural ambition and willingness to break dance-record conventions match his stated preferences; assumed high mark, flagged.** The risk with *Sun* is the production, which is dry even by mid-'70s standards and which sometimes flattens the band's dynamic range. But the compositions are strong enough to survive the mix, and the album's reputation has only grown in the half-century since release. The other point worth noting is the political register of the record. Palmieri made *Sun* during the height of the Nuyorican cultural moment — the same years that produced Piri Thomas's *Down These Mean Streets* and the Young Lords' East Harlem organizing — and the album reads as a deliberate compositional argument that the salsa tradition could carry the same artistic weight as any other body of music being made in New York in the mid-1970s. That argument has been won.

##### 4. Dizzy Gillespie — *Afro* (1954)

The Norman Granz–produced summit between Dizzy's big band and Chico O'Farrill's compositional voice, *Afro* is the studio realization of what the 1947 Town Hall concert with Chano Pozo had only sketched. The "Manteca Suite," reorchestrated by O'Farrill across four movements, takes the original Pozo–Gillespie–Fuller riff and develops it through harmonic territory that the 1947 band could not have navigated. Side B contains "A Night in Tunisia" with Cuban percussion, several O'Farrill originals, and a band performance that sits squarely between bebop and the still-emerging Afro-Cuban jazz idiom.

Scaruffi treats the 1947–1954 Gillespie/Pozo/O'Farrill axis as the foundational moment of Afro-Cuban jazz — the moment when the bebop language was opened up to a non-Western rhythmic logic that it had previously avoided. *Afro* is the most fully realized studio document of that opening, and it has the additional virtue of being a complete album rather than a singles compilation, which gives it more compositional weight than the 1947 sessions can carry.

**Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — no direct review located; included on historical-foundational grounds with assumed neutral-to-positive reception, flagged.** The album occasionally feels like a transitional document — neither fully bebop nor fully the Latin jazz that would emerge in the 1960s — but its historical position is unimpeachable, and the O'Farrill arrangements have a sophistication that few of the imitators that followed could match.

##### 5. Irakere — *Irakere* (1979)

Recorded live at the Newport and Montreux festivals in 1978 and released by Columbia in 1979, this is the album that introduced the West to the Cuban fusion tradition that had developed in isolation under the embargo. Chucho Valdés on piano, Paquito D'Rivera on saxophone, Arturo Sandoval on trumpet, and a rhythm section that included Carlos Emilio Morales on guitar and Jorge Varona on percussion played a music that combined Weather Report–style electric fusion with batá drumming, son montuno bass figures, and a harmonic vocabulary trained on Rachmaninoff and Bartók as much as on Coltrane.

Scaruffi treats Irakere as proof that the Cuban tradition had continued to evolve in parallel with American jazz rather than freezing at the moment of revolution. The compositions are dense, the arrangements ambitious, and the playing — particularly Valdés's piano and D'Rivera's alto — operates at a virtuoso level that few American fusion bands of the period could match. The version of "Misa Negra" on the album is one of the great extended fusion compositions of the late 1970s.

**Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — no direct review located, but the structural sophistication and the politically charged context match his sensibility; assumed positive mark, flagged.** The reservation: the live recording quality is uneven, and some of the band's arrangements sound dated in the way that all late-'70s electric fusion does. But the compositional voice and the ensemble execution are strong enough to overcome the production limitations.

##### 6. Antonio Carlos Jobim — *Wave* (1967)

Jobim's most fully realized solo album, recorded for CTI with Claus Ogerman arrangements that sit somewhere between bossa nova, third-stream jazz, and orchestral impressionism. The title track, "Triste," "Look to the Sky," and "Dialogo" are among the most harmonically sophisticated compositions in the Brazilian songbook, and Ogerman's strings are deployed with a restraint that distinguishes the record from the more saccharine Brazilian-pop arrangements of the same period.

Scaruffi treats Jobim as the major Brazilian composer of the twentieth century and *Wave* as his strongest single record — stronger than the more famous *The Composer of Desafinado Plays* (1963) and stronger than the late-period *Stone Flower*. The album is short (thirty-three minutes), the playing is unforced, and the compositions are uniformly first-rate.

**Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — bossa nova falls outside his usual coverage, but he has shown affection for harmonically sophisticated singer-songwriter material and would likely respond to Jobim's compositional voice; assumed positive mark, flagged.** The criticism worth registering is that *Wave* sits closer to easy listening than the more austere *Getz/Gilberto*, and the Ogerman arrangements occasionally tip into lounge territory. But the writing is so strong that the genre proximity does not damage the album.

##### 7. Mongo Santamaría — *Afro Roots* (1972)

A compilation that gathers the 1958–1959 Fantasy sessions Santamaría recorded with Willie Bobo on percussion, Cal Tjader sitting in on vibes, and a pickup band of Cuban and California-based players, *Afro Roots* is the most concentrated document of the late-'50s West Coast Afro-Cuban jazz scene. The original "Afro Blue" appears here in its first studio version — predating the more famous Coltrane interpretation by five years — and the album as a whole is a tutorial in the rhythmic vocabulary that would underpin the next two decades of Latin jazz.

Scaruffi credits Santamaría as one of the key conduits between the Cuban tradition and American jazz, and treats the early Fantasy material as historically more important than the later, more commercial Columbia and Atlantic records. **Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — no review located, but the historical-percussion focus and the genuinely good compositions place this comfortably in the canon; assumed neutral-to-positive mark, flagged.**

The album's status as a compilation rather than a unified artistic statement is its main weakness — the sequencing is more documentary than aesthetic, and the recording quality varies across the included sessions. But "Afro Blue" alone justifies inclusion, and the rhythm-section work across the album is the foundation on which most subsequent Latin jazz percussion is built.

##### 8. Machito and His Afro-Cubans — *Kenya* (1957)

Recorded for Roulette with arrangements by A. K. Salim and Mario Bauzá and featuring Cannonball Adderley as guest soloist on several tracks, *Kenya* is the most successful integration of bebop horn-section writing with the Afro-Cuban rhythm-section tradition that Bauzá had been developing since the early 1940s. Machito's band had been the laboratory in which the synthesis was worked out — Bauzá's recruitment of Pozo, his arrangements for the band's late-'40s sides, his insistence on the clave as the organizing principle even when the horns played bebop lines — and *Kenya* is the moment at which the synthesis became fully musical rather than merely conceptual.

The Adderley solos are excellent (he plays with a fluency in the Cuban rhythmic context that Charlie Parker on the 1949 *Afro-Cuban Jazz Suite* sessions had not quite achieved), and the arrangements by Salim and Bauzá give the band a structural variety that the earlier Machito records sometimes lacked. Scaruffi treats this as the high point of the Machito discography and as one of the most important Latin jazz records of the 1950s.

**Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — period-locked big-band Latin jazz is genuinely outside his coverage and the swing-era timbre might not appeal; assumed neutral mark, flagged.** The album's mid-'50s production and the relative obscurity of some of the sidemen have kept it from the broader recognition it deserves, but its musical quality is high and its historical position is foundational.

##### 9. Gonzalo Rubalcaba — *Discovery: Live at Montreux* (1990)

Recorded at Montreux in July 1990 with Charlie Haden on bass and Paul Motian on drums, *Discovery* is the album that introduced the Cuban virtuoso piano tradition to a mainstream jazz audience. Rubalcaba, then twenty-seven, plays with a technical command that reads as Tatum-level until you realize the harmonic vocabulary is closer to late Bill Evans, and the rhythmic conception — even on standards like "Well You Needn't" and "Mima" — is grounded in Cuban son rather than in American swing.

Haden and Motian, both at the peak of their late-period telepathy, give Rubalcaba the kind of supportive freedom that allows the pianist to take risks without losing the trio's structural coherence. The version of Hernandez's "Perfidia" is one of the most beautiful Latin-standard performances in the trio repertoire.

Scaruffi has been more guarded about Rubalcaba than about the older Cuban masters — the technique sometimes outruns the compositional voice — but he treats *Discovery* as the strongest single document of Rubalcaba's early career and as proof that the Cuban tradition has continued to produce world-class players into the present. **Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — modern jazz piano trio with structural sophistication matches his sensibility; assumed positive mark, flagged.**

The risk with Rubalcaba is over-elaboration — his later records sometimes drown their material in technique — but on *Discovery* the trio context disciplines him, and the result is one of the great live piano-trio records of the 1990s.

##### 10. Airto Moreira — *Fingers* (1973)

Recorded for CTI in 1972 with Hugo Fattoruso on keyboards, David Amaro on guitar, Ringo Thielmann on bass, and Flora Purim on vocals, *Fingers* is the Brazilian fusion record that most fully integrated the percussion-forward Brazilian tradition with the electric jazz vocabulary that Miles Davis had opened up with *Bitches Brew* (on which Airto had played). The compositions are short, percussive, and harmonically open; the playing is consistently strong; and Purim's vocals — wordless on most tracks — function as another rhythmic-melodic element rather than as a foreground.

Scaruffi places *Fingers* among the strongest Brazilian fusion records of the 1970s, ahead of the more famous Sergio Mendes records and on a par with the better Hermeto sides. The record is short (thirty-five minutes), unpretentious, and consistently musical.

**Fantano EXTRAPOLATED — no review located, but the percussion-forward fusion approach and Airto's later collaboration with contemporary jazz figures suggest favorable disposition; assumed positive mark, flagged.** The reservation: some of the keyboard sounds are dated in the way that all early-'70s Fender Rhodes records can be, and the production is dry even by CTI standards. But the compositions and the band chemistry survive the period limitations, and the record has aged better than most of its contemporaries.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

**Coin flip 1: Tito Puente *Dance Mania* (1958) vs. Antonio Carlos Jobim *Wave* (1967) for slot 6.** Scaruffi favored *Wave* on compositional grounds; Fantano-extrapolated split (Brazilian songwriting affinity vs. Puente's mambo-canonical status). Coin landed on *Wave*. *Dance Mania* moves to honorable mentions; it remains the canonical mambo-era band record but reads more as a dance-floor document than as a Latin jazz statement, which is genuinely a reasonable reason to prefer Jobim in a jazz-anchored list.

**Coin flip 2: Mongo Santamaría *Afro Roots* vs. Cal Tjader *Soul Sauce* (1965) for slot 7.** Scaruffi favored Mongo on lineage grounds (Cuban primary vs. American interpreter); Fantano-extrapolated split. Coin landed on Mongo. Tjader is the cleaner pure-jazz record and *Soul Sauce* contains some of his strongest playing, but the Mongo sides have the historical primacy — "Afro Blue" originates here — and the rhythmic authority that come from being inside the tradition rather than visiting it. Tjader to honorable mentions.

**Coin flip 3: Gonzalo Rubalcaba *Discovery* vs. Michel Camilo *Why Not!* (1985) for slot 9.** Both Cuban-tradition virtuoso piano records from the late-'80s/early-'90s window; Scaruffi cooler on both than on the older masters; Fantano-extrapolated neutral on both. Coin landed on Rubalcaba. The Haden–Motian rhythm section gives *Discovery* a structural and listening weight that Camilo's record, strong as it is, cannot quite match. Camilo to honorable mentions.

#### Honorable Mentions

**Tito Puente — *Dance Mania* (1958).** The mambo-era band record, recorded for RCA Victor with the Puente Orchestra at peak. As a Latin jazz statement it is less compelling than as a dance document, but the ensemble playing is extraordinary and the album defined the New York Latin sound for a decade.

**Cal Tjader — *Soul Sauce* (1965).** Tjader's most successful record and one of the cleanest examples of West Coast Latin jazz, with a band that included Donald Byrd and Jimmy Heath and a title track built on Dizzy Gillespie's "Guachi Guaro."

**Michel Camilo — *Why Not!* (1985).** Camilo's American debut, with Lincoln Goines and Dave Weckl. Less harmonically adventurous than Rubalcaba but more rhythmically aggressive; an excellent record that loses to *Discovery* on accompanist quality.

**Chico O'Farrill — *Cuban Blues: The Chico O'Farrill Sessions* (1996 compilation).** The Verve box that gathers O'Farrill's 1950–1954 work as composer and arranger for Machito, Gillespie, and his own bands. Compilation status keeps it out of the main list, but the writing is foundational.

**Ray Barretto — *Acid* (1968).** The Fania boogaloo–jazz record that defined the late-'60s Nuyorican sound. More Latin soul than Latin jazz strictly speaking, but the band playing is extraordinary and the influence on the '70s Fania catalog is enormous.

**Flora Purim — *Butterfly Dreams* (1974).** Purim's solo masterpiece, with Airto, Joe Henderson, George Duke, and Stanley Clarke. Brazilian-fusion vocal record that holds up better than most of its contemporaries.

**Paquito D'Rivera — *Tico! Tico!* (1989).** D'Rivera's most successful post-defection record, with a band that includes Danilo Pérez and Claudio Roditi. Period-piece in places but the playing is consistently first-rate.

**Sergio Mendes & Brasil '66 — *Equinox* (1967).** The strongest of the Brasil '66 records and the closest Mendes came to an actual jazz record rather than a pop-bossa hybrid. Scaruffi cool on Mendes; included for completeness.

**Astrud Gilberto — *The Astrud Gilberto Album* (1965).** Gilberto's solo debut, with Jobim arrangements. More vocal-pop than Latin jazz but historically essential.

**Danilo Pérez — *Panamonk* (1996).** Pérez's Monk-tribute record with John Patitucci and Terri Lyne Carrington. The strongest of the post-Rubalcaba Latin-jazz piano records and the one most successful at integrating the Latin and American jazz traditions on equal footing.

**Arturo Sandoval — *Danzón (Dance On)* (1994).** Sandoval's GRP debut as a leader, recorded after his 1990 defection. Technique sometimes outruns material, but the trumpet playing is extraordinary and the Cuban-jazz arrangements are first-rate.

**Hermeto Pascoal — *A Música Livre de Hermeto Pascoal* (1973).** Hermeto's earlier solo record, more conceptually radical than *Slaves Mass* but less consistently realized. The two records together form the core of the Hermeto discography of the 1970s.

**Eddie Palmieri — *Unfinished Masterpiece* (1975).** The follow-up to *Sun of Latin Music*, less consistent but with several individual tracks (the title piece, "Random Thoughts") that match the predecessor's high points.

**Bebo Valdés & Israel "Cachao" López — *Descarga Cubana* sessions (1957, various releases).** The Havana descarga jam sessions that documented the Cuban jazz tradition just before the revolution. More important historically than as listening records, but essential to the lineage.

**Brad Mehldau — *Largo* (2002).** Listed in the prompt as a Latin-side debatable; treated as genuinely debatable. Has Latin-rhythmic moments but is fundamentally a singer-songwriter–adjacent jazz record; excluded from main list as too marginal to the Latin tradition to take a slot from a core entry.

---

### Spiritual Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Spiritual jazz is the genre where Scaruffi has the most settled canon and Fantano has the least published commentary. Scaruffi's writing on Pharoah Sanders, Alice Coltrane, and Don Cherry stretches back decades and treats the post-1965 Coltrane diaspora as one of the great peaks of recorded music. He privileges trance, drone, modal stasis, ritual length, and Afro-Eastern fusion — the further from bebop chord-changes a record gets, the more interested he becomes. Fantano's spiritual jazz footprint is genuinely thin: he reviewed Kamasi Washington's *The Epic* favorably, has spoken warmly about Alice Coltrane in retrospective contexts, and treats the Coltrane lineage as foundational without grading it. Most pre-1980 picks here are extrapolated from his stated affinities (drone, long-form, devotional textures, Afrocentric politics) and are flagged accordingly.

A complicating wrinkle: spiritual jazz is the only sub-genre on the volume's roster where the canonical body of work was almost entirely produced inside a roughly fifteen-year window (1965–1980), against a backdrop of Black liberation politics, Afrocentric religious revival, the post-Bandung Pan-African imagination, the Vietnam-era youth-counterculture appetite for non-Western mysticism, and the late-John-Coltrane catalog's simultaneous artistic and commercial breakthrough. Removing any one of those contextual layers leaves the genre unintelligible. The records below were not made as "spiritual jazz" — the term was applied retroactively in the 1990s and 2000s by reissue labels (Soul Jazz, Strut, Now-Again) and the dance-floor DJs who built the modern listening canon — but they are linked by an attempt to make jazz instrumental music carry the work that Black church music, Indian classical traditions, and the Egyptian/Middle Eastern modal vocabulary had been carrying for the players' families for generations. That retroactive framing is part of why critics from outside the working-jazz tradition (Fantano's lineage, broadly) tend to engage with the genre at the canon level rather than at the deep-cut level, and it shapes the asymmetry between the two critics here.

We agreed Coltrane's *A Love Supreme* — which technically founds the entire genre in 1965 — is anchored to the Modal Jazz file by editorial decision and excluded here, freeing a slot. We agreed *Karma* is the genre's center of gravity. We agreed Alice Coltrane is the second pillar and that *Journey in Satchidananda* is her most consensus-friendly entry. We agreed Don Cherry's *Brown Rice* belongs near the top because it's where Pan-African fusion crystallizes. From there, the debate split four ways: (1) which Pharoah belongs alongside *Karma* — *Tauhid*, *Black Unity*, or *Thembi*; (2) which Alice belongs alongside *Satchidananda* — *Universal Consciousness* (Scaruffi's pick) or *Ptah, the El Daoud* (more accessible, Fantano-extrapolated); (3) whether Kamasi Washington's *The Epic* deserves a top-10 slot or honorable mention; (4) Strata-East and Black Jazz label representation — Doug Carn vs Gary Bartz vs Charles Tolliver. We coin-flipped two of these. The list below leans Scaruffi-canonical with Fantano's two confirmed enthusiasms (Kamasi, the broader Coltrane reverence) preserved.

A note on what is missing: the genre's deep catalog includes substantial work by Strata-East (Charles Tolliver, Stanley Cowell, Clifford Jordan), Black Jazz (Walter Bishop Jr., Gene Russell, Kellee Patterson), Tribe (Phil Ranelin, Wendell Harrison, Marcus Belgrave), and the international parallel scenes — Don Cherry and Maffy Falay's Sevda in Sweden, Mtume Umoja Ensemble, Idris Ackamoor's Pyramids on tour through East Africa — that any honest expanded list would have to acknowledge. The honorable mentions section sketches some of this but is not exhaustive; the long tail of small-label spiritual jazz is a body of work that no top-10 can responsibly compress, and we treat the omissions as a problem to flag rather than to resolve.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Pharoah Sanders — *Karma* (1969)

Both critics agree this is the keystone. *Karma* is essentially one composition — the 32-minute "The Creator Has a Master Plan" — built on Leon Thomas's yodeling chant of "the creator has a master plan / peace and happiness for every man," James Spaulding's flute, Lonnie Liston Smith's modal piano, Reggie Workman's bass drone, and Pharoah's tenor moving from melodic balm to pure overblown shriek and back. The track's structure — devotional theme, rupture into free squall, return to chant — became the template for the entire genre.

Scaruffi places this among the most important post-Coltrane recordings and reads it as the moment Coltrane's spiritual project escaped Coltrane's death and proved itself transmissible. His admiration is for the way Pharoah refuses to choose between melody and noise: the screams aren't an interlude, they're the prayer at maximum intensity. Leon Thomas's yodel — a technique he traced to Central African pygmy music — locates the spirituality outside Western liturgy and inside a constructed Pan-African folk tradition.

Fantano hasn't reviewed *Karma* directly, but it's the album most often cited when he discusses the Coltrane lineage as foundational. **[FLAGGED: Fantano top-10 inclusion extrapolated.]** His enthusiasm for long-form, mood-saturated, vocal-inflected jazz (visible in his treatment of Kamasi) maps cleanly onto what *Karma* is doing. There is no critical universe in which *Karma* doesn't make this list; the only question was whether to lead with it or with Alice Coltrane, and the chronology and influence both pointed here.

The B-side, "Colors," is short, gentle, and often forgotten — but it's the proof that Pharoah could have made an entire career of pastoral devotional music if he'd wanted to. He chose ecstasy instead.

##### 2. Alice Coltrane — *Journey in Satchidananda* (1971)

The consensus Alice Coltrane entry. Recorded with Pharoah Sanders on soprano, Cecil McBee on bass, Tulsi on tamboura, and Alice on harp and Wurlitzer, *Satchidananda* names itself after Swami Satchidananda, Alice's guru, and threads Indian classical drone through modal jazz harmony with a delicacy that's almost shocking after the density of late John Coltrane. The title track's harp glissandi over McBee's ostinato bass is one of the most identifiable sounds in the genre.

Scaruffi treats Alice as Coltrane's true heir — not Pharoah, not Sanders's sidemen, but the widow who took the harp into territory her husband never reached. He reads *Satchidananda* as the moment jazz harp stopped being a novelty (Dorothy Ashby) and became a primary devotional instrument. The combination of tamboura drone with Pharoah's restrained soprano is, in his framing, the genre's most successful East-West synthesis.

Fantano has spoken affectionately about Alice Coltrane in retrospective contexts and the harp-and-drone aesthetic resurfaces in his enthusiasm for ambient and contemporary devotional music. **[FLAGGED: Fantano-specific commentary on this album not on record.]** Both critics agreed *Satchidananda* over *Universal Consciousness* for the consensus slot because *Satchidananda* is where the Alice Coltrane sound is fully formed but still legible to listeners coming from modal jazz. *Universal Consciousness* (which makes the list below) is the more radical document.

The closing track "Isis and Osiris," recorded live at the Village Gate, brings in oud and adds an Egyptian frame that prefigures Don Cherry's Pan-African moves four years later.

##### 3. Don Cherry — *Brown Rice* (1975)

Where the genre stops being post-Coltrane and becomes something else: world music before the term existed, fourth-world before Jon Hassell named it. Don Cherry on pocket trumpet, voice, harmonium, piano, and tambura; Frank Lowe on tenor; Charlie Haden on bass; Billy Higgins and Verna Gillis on percussion and vocals. The title track's loping electric bass groove, Cherry's whispered vocal mantra, and the thick haze of harmonium drone make it a bridge between spiritual jazz and what would become electric Miles, dub, and ambient.

Scaruffi rates this very highly. His framing: Cherry took the Coltrane spiritual project and globalized it, refusing to anchor in Indian, African, or Middle Eastern music exclusively, instead building a syncretic vocabulary where each tradition is a color in the palette. "Malkauns" — a 13-minute raga-based piece — is the clearest example of what he means.

Fantano has discussed Don Cherry warmly in the context of the Codona trio and Cherry's broader 70s output. **[FLAGGED: Fantano on *Brown Rice* specifically not on record, but his general affinity for genre-collapsing fourth-world music suggests inclusion.]** *Brown Rice* lost a coin-flip discussion against *Mu* (Cherry/Blackwell duo) for the Cherry slot — see Coin-Flip Resolutions.

The album was originally released as *Brown Rice* on EMI Italy in 1975 (catalog 3C 064-18107) and reissued in 1977 in the US on Horizon Records as *Don Cherry* (SP-717); the Italian original sat in relative obscurity for years before Soul Jazz and others reissued it and made it a touchstone for the 2000s spiritual-jazz revival. Sampled by Madlib, covered by Sun Araw, beloved of every record-store clerk under 35.

##### 4. Pharoah Sanders — *Tauhid* (1967)

Pharoah's first leader date for Impulse, recorded eight months before John Coltrane's death and released the year of it. "Upper Egypt & Lower Egypt" — the side-long opener — begins with seven minutes of sparse percussion, bells, and Sonny Sharrock's distorted electric guitar before Pharoah's tenor enters at the eleven-minute mark with a melody so plainly beautiful it feels like a different musician altogether. The structural lesson — patience, then theme, then ecstasy, then return — is the spiritual-jazz playbook in microcosm.

Scaruffi reads *Tauhid* as the proof that Pharoah was a composer and not just a screamer. The Sharrock guitar — proto-noise, a decade before Sonic Youth — is a detail he returns to, because it positions Pharoah at the front of an avant-garde lineage that runs through No Wave and beyond. The Egyptian framing (the title means "oneness" in Arabic, referencing tawhid, the Islamic concept of God's unity) is also where the genre's Afro-Islamic strand begins, well before Yusef Lateef's similar moves got the bigger audience.

Fantano top-10 inclusion **[FLAGGED: extrapolated]**. The reasoning: if Fantano endorses *Karma*, the prequel that establishes the Pharoah voice belongs adjacent. *Tauhid* beat *Black Unity* for this slot in the debate because it's the more compositionally varied record — *Black Unity* is one continuous 38-minute piece and demands a kind of attention that puts it closer to free jazz proper.

The closing "Aum/Venus/Capricorn Rising" turns the attention to the cosmos and points directly at where Sun Ra was already working.

##### 5. Alice Coltrane — *Universal Consciousness* (1971)

The radical Alice. Where *Satchidananda* welcomes the listener in, *Universal Consciousness* — recorded the same year — closes the door behind them. Alice on Wurlitzer organ and harp; a string quartet (Leroy Jenkins, John Blair, Julius Brand, Joan Kalisch) arranged by Ornette Coleman; Jack DeJohnette and Rashied Ali on drums; Jimmy Garrison on bass. The strings shriek and slide microtonally; the organ rolls in massive devotional waves; there is no concession to anything resembling jazz convention.

Scaruffi rates *Universal Consciousness* as Alice's masterpiece, above *Satchidananda*. His reading: this is the only album where the Western chamber tradition (the string quartet) is fully absorbed into the Coltrane spiritual idiom rather than sitting decoratively on top of it. The Ornette arrangements — credited to him, executed by Alice's strings — are some of the strangest writing in his catalog and rarely discussed.

Fantano-extrapolated **[FLAGGED]**. The honest version: *Universal Consciousness* is on the list because Scaruffi is right about it and Fantano hasn't been heard from. In a genuinely contested debate it would have lost to a more accessible Alice record (*Ptah, the El Daoud*) but Scaruffi's certainty here outweighs Fantano's silence.

The 7-minute "Hare Krishna" closes the album with Alice's organ doubling the chant melody at cathedral volume. It's the loudest devotional track in the genre and one of the few that earns the comparison to John Coltrane's "Acknowledgement."

##### 6. Sun Ra — *Space Is the Place* (1973)

Sun Ra's catalog is too vast for any single album to represent it, but *Space Is the Place* — the studio album, soundtrack to the 1974 film of the same name — is the Ra record that most clearly belongs in the spiritual jazz frame rather than the free or big-band frames. The 21-minute title track, with June Tyson chanting "space is the place / there's no limit to the things that you can do," is a cosmic-Afrocentric devotional in everything but name.

Scaruffi treats Sun Ra as one of the great American composers and threads his work through nearly every avant-garde discussion. His framing of *Space Is the Place* is that it's the most legible entry point for listeners who find the Saturn-era recordings impenetrable: the structures are looser than the late-50s Chicago albums but more organized than the El Saturn private-press chaos of the late 60s.

Fantano has referenced Sun Ra appreciatively but rarely reviewed him. **[FLAGGED: top-10 placement extrapolated.]** The argument for inclusion: Sun Ra invented the cosmic-spiritual aesthetic that Pharoah and Alice inherited; omitting him from a spiritual jazz top-10 is a category error regardless of which critic is asked. *Space Is the Place* lost a coin-flip against *Lanquidity* (1978) for the Ra slot — see resolutions.

"Sea of Sounds" and "Rocket Number Nine" round out the album with the Arkestra at its tightest.

##### 7. Lonnie Liston Smith & the Cosmic Echoes — *Astral Traveling* (1973)

The pivot point between spiritual jazz and the smoother fusion that would become quiet storm and acid jazz. Lonnie Liston Smith — Pharoah's pianist on *Karma* and *Thembi* — assembled the Cosmic Echoes (his brother Donald on vocals and percussion, Joe Beck on guitar, Cecil McBee on bass, David Lee on drums) for an album so soft and pretty it feels like a betrayal of the genre's intensity. It isn't. It's the proof that spiritual jazz could be ambient before ambient.

Scaruffi rates Smith's early Flying Dutchman trilogy (*Astral Traveling*, *Cosmic Funk*, *Expansions*) highly and singles out *Astral Traveling* as the most coherent. His reading: the Fender Rhodes drift on the title track is one of the first uses of electric piano as a primary devotional instrument, and the album's mood — weightless, drifting, prayerful — is achieved without any of the ecstatic-screaming machinery that defines Pharoah.

Fantano has briefly cited the Cosmic Echoes catalog in discussions of jazz-fusion influence on hip-hop and neo-soul. **[FLAGGED: top-10 placement extrapolated.]** Sampled extensively by hip-hop producers (Pete Rock, Madlib, Jay Dilla), *Astral Traveling* is the spiritual jazz album with the deepest crossover footprint into genres Fantano covers more directly.

The title track is the canonical entry — six minutes of Rhodes shimmer, McBee's ostinato bass, and a melody that genuinely sounds like leaving the body.

##### 8. Pharoah Sanders — *Black Unity* (1971)

A single 37-minute composition. Two basses (Cecil McBee and Stanley Clarke), two drummers (Norman Connors and Billy Hart), Carlos Garnett on second tenor, Joe Bonner on piano, Lawrence Killian on percussion. The piece moves through extended modal vamps, free-blowing duels, and percussion breakdowns without ever settling — it's the most relentless album in Pharoah's catalog and the one where the political content (the title) is most explicit.

Scaruffi rates *Black Unity* near the top of Pharoah's catalog and uses it as evidence that spiritual jazz could carry explicit Black liberation politics without becoming agitprop. The structure — long-form, no breaks, two rhythm sections trading and overlapping — anticipates the dance-music maximalism that wouldn't surface for another decade.

Fantano-extrapolated **[FLAGGED]**. The honest case for inclusion: a top-10 with three Pharoahs (*Karma*, *Tauhid*, *Black Unity*) reflects his actual stature in the genre rather than an artificial diversification quota. Scaruffi would put four Pharoahs on the list. We capped at three.

The album's reputation grew steadily through the 80s and 90s and it's now the Pharoah record most cited by DJs and producers building long-form sets — its sheer duration and groove-orientation make it functional in ways *Karma* isn't.

##### 9. Doug Carn — *Infant Eyes* (1971)

The Black Jazz label representative. Doug Carn on Fender Rhodes, organ, and arrangements; his then-wife Jean Carn on vocals; a small band of Bob Frazier on trumpet, Al Hall Jr. on trombone, George Harper on sax and flute, Henry Franklin on bass, and Michael Carvin on drums. The album reworks compositions by Wayne Shorter ("Infant Eyes"), McCoy Tyner ("Contemplation," "Search for Peace"), and John Coltrane ("Acknowledgement," "Welcome") with new lyrics by Doug or Jean — turning instrumental jazz standards into devotional vocal works.

Scaruffi is a champion of the Black Jazz catalog (the Oakland-based, Black-owned label founded by Gene Russell in 1971) and rates *Infant Eyes* as its best release. His reading: by lyricizing the Coltrane and Shorter melodies, Carn made the spiritual content of those compositions explicit, and Jean Carn's voice — operatic, gospel-rooted, agile — is a rare instrument that can deliver devotional lyrics without sliding into either jazz-singer mannerism or church performance.

Fantano top-10 inclusion **[FLAGGED: extrapolated heavily.]** The case: the Black Jazz / Strata-East label-roster moment is genre-defining, and any honest top-10 needs at least one representative. *Infant Eyes* won the slot against Gary Bartz's *I've Known Rivers* and Charles Tolliver's *Music Inc.* in coin-flip — see resolutions.

The cover of "Acknowledgement" with new lyrics — written from the standpoint of the pilgrim rather than the saint — is the album's centerpiece and the one moment where the entire spiritual jazz project explains itself in plain English.

##### 10. Kamasi Washington — *The Epic* (2015)

The modern revival. A nearly three-hour triple album recorded with Washington's West Coast Get Down collective (Stephen Bruner aka Thundercat on bass, Cameron Graves on piano, Ronald Bruner Jr. on drums, plus a 32-piece string orchestra and 20-voice choir). Released on Brainfeeder, it positioned spiritual jazz back inside the contemporary cultural conversation — riding the wake of Washington's contributions to Kendrick Lamar's *To Pimp a Butterfly* the same year.

Fantano reviewed *The Epic* favorably on release and singled out the ambition and the commitment to old-school spiritual jazz forms (long-form modal vamps, choral textures, devotional themes) at a moment when nothing else in jazz was operating at that scale. This is one of his only confirmed spiritual-jazz recommendations and the strongest case for his vote on this list. **[FANTANO-CONFIRMED.]**

Scaruffi has been notably quiet on Washington — he reviews far fewer post-2000 jazz records and his existing writing on the genre stops well before this revival. **[FLAGGED: Scaruffi top-10 inclusion not confirmed.]** The argument for inclusion against his probable absence: the modern-revival slot is genre-honest, Washington's *The Epic* is the consensus pick for it, and a top-10 that ignores the present looks less like a canon than a museum.

Standout tracks: "Change of the Guard," "Re Run Home," and the cover of Debussy's "Claire de Lune" recast for the full ensemble. The album's flaws — repetition, occasional bombast — are the price of its scope, and most spiritual-jazz listeners agreed to pay it.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three irreconcilable disputes were resolved by coin flip:

1. **Don Cherry slot: *Brown Rice* vs *Mu* (Cherry/Blackwell duo, 1969).** Scaruffi rates *Mu* very highly and considers it Cherry's most undiluted statement. Fantano-extrapolated preference goes to *Brown Rice* for its electric textures and crossover footprint. Coin flip: *Brown Rice* won. *Mu* moves to honorable mentions.

2. **Sun Ra slot: *Space Is the Place* vs *Lanquidity*.** Scaruffi has more written commentary on *Space Is the Place*; *Lanquidity* (1978) is the cult favorite that fits the spiritual-jazz frame even more cleanly with its drifting, near-ambient textures. Coin flip: *Space Is the Place* won. *Lanquidity* moves to honorable mentions.

3. **Black Jazz / Strata-East label slot: *Infant Eyes* (Doug Carn) vs *Music Inc.* (Charles Tolliver / Strata-East) vs *I've Known Rivers* (Gary Bartz / Prestige).** Three-way debate with no clear consensus. Coin flip among the three: Carn won. Tolliver and Bartz move to honorable mentions.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Pharoah Sanders — *Thembi* (1971)** — the fourth Pharoah album that almost made the list; "Astral Travelling" is Lonnie Liston Smith's first writing credit and prefigures *Astral Traveling*.
- **Alice Coltrane — *Ptah, the El Daoud* (1970)** — the most accessible Alice, with Pharoah and Joe Henderson trading on the title track. Lost the Alice-accessible slot to *Satchidananda*.
- **Don Cherry & Ed Blackwell — *Mu (First Part)* (1969)** — lost the Cherry coin flip.
- **Sun Ra — *Lanquidity* (1978)** — lost the Sun Ra coin flip.
- **Charles Tolliver / Music Inc. — *Live at Slugs' Vol. 1* (1972)** — Strata-East flagship; lost the label-slot coin flip.
- **Gary Bartz NTU Troop — *Harlem Bush Music: Taifa* (1971)** — chant, Black-nationalist politics, modal-into-free; lost the label-slot coin flip.
- **Albert Ayler — *Love Cry* (1968)** — late Ayler that bridges into the spiritual frame; arguably belongs in Free Jazz.
- **Yusef Lateef — *The Blue Yusef Lateef* (1968)** — the Eastern strand of the genre.
- **Idris Muhammad — *Power of Soul* (1974)** — the funk-spiritual crossover; produced by Bob James.
- **Phil Ranelin — *The Time Is Now!* (1974)** — Tribe Records' best-known release; Detroit's answer to Strata-East.
- **John Coltrane — *Meditations* (1966)** — would compete for #1 but is anchored to the Modal Jazz / Free Jazz files by editorial decision.
- **Pharoah Sanders & Floating Points — *Promises* (2021)** — the last word from Pharoah; the second strongest case for a modern-revival slot after *The Epic*. Cross-listed with the Nu Jazz / Future Jazz file, where it sits in the top three.
- **Charles Tolliver / Music Inc. — *Impact* (1975)** — the Strata-East big-band statement; horns over horns, a Scaruffi favorite for its insistence on collective scale.
- **Stanley Cowell — *Musa: Ancestral Streams* (1974)** — the Strata-East solo-piano-and-mbira record that anchored the label's experimental edge; deeply devotional without ever raising its voice.
- **Pyramids — *Birth/Speed/Merging* (1976)** — Idris Ackamoor's Bay Area collective, recorded after a long African tour; the most directly ethnomusicologically engaged record in the genre's American catalog.
- **Carlos Garnett — *Black Love* (1974)** — Pharoah's tenor-section foil on *Black Unity* and *Live in Paris*, leading his own group with Mtume on percussion and Reggie Lucas on guitar.
- **Joe Henderson — *Power to the People* (1969)** — the political-spiritual edge of post-bop, with Herbie Hancock and Ron Carter; arguably belongs in modal jazz.
- **Lloyd McNeill — *Asha* (1969)** — flutist, painter, poet; private-press devotional jazz that Soul Jazz reissued in the 2010s and that quietly reshaped the genre's canon.
- **Sun Ra — *Lanquidity* (1978)** — the cosmic-ambient Arkestra, lost the Sun Ra coin flip but is the strongest case for a "spiritual ambient" framing of late Ra.
- **Marion Brown — *Afternoon of a Georgia Faun* (1970)** — ECM's earliest Black-American devotional record; quiet, exploratory, foundational for the European reception of the spiritual lineage.

---

## Part Three — Adjacent and Modern

The seven sub-genres in this part are the records that don't fit the linear bop-to-free-to-spiritual narrative. Some of them predate the narrative (Vocal Jazz / Standards, which has been running parallel to instrumental jazz since the 1920s and runs through the bop era as its own continuous tradition); some of them branch off the narrative at sharp angles (Jazz Fusion's electric collision with rock, Third Stream's classical reach, ECM's European chamber aesthetic); some of them continue the narrative past where Part Two ended (Avant-garde / Loft Jazz as the post-free 1970s New York scene's continuation); some of them sit uncomfortably alongside the narrative (Smooth Jazz as the much-maligned commercial branch); and some of them re-enter the narrative from the electronic-music side (Nu Jazz / Future Jazz and the contemporary London / Los Angeles revival). Seven sub-genres, organized loosely by their relationship to the central jazz lineage rather than by chronology — the file order moves from the deepest-rooted (Vocal Jazz, which predates almost everything) through the most stylistically distant (Smooth Jazz) to the most contemporary (Nu Jazz / Future Jazz).

**Vocal Jazz / Standards** is the parallel tradition. From the 1920s forward — running through the swing era, through the bop revolution, through the LP era's "songbook" period, and through the present-day standards-revival wing — vocalists carrying the Tin Pan Alley / Great American Songbook material have constituted their own tradition inside jazz. The earliest layer is the classic blues / vaudeville-blues singers (Bessie Smith, Ma Rainey, Ethel Waters) whose material overlaps with the Pre-War Blues file in Volume 1 but whose post-1925 recordings increasingly belong to a jazz-vocal tradition rather than a blues tradition proper. The mid-period is the Billie Holiday / Ella Fitzgerald / Sarah Vaughan / Carmen McRae / Anita O'Day / Dinah Washington / Betty Carter axis — the canonical female jazz vocalists of the 1940s through 1960s, each with a substantial album catalog and each with at least one record that anchors the file's top ten. Ella's Verve Songbook series (the Cole Porter, Rodgers and Hart, Ellington, Gershwin, Mercer, Berlin, and Kern songbooks, recorded 1956-1964 under Norman Granz's production) is the file's central artifact-cluster. Holiday's Verve and Decca records and the late *Lady in Satin* (1958) are the file's emotional center. Sinatra's Capitol-era concept albums (*In the Wee Small Hours* 1955, *Songs for Swingin' Lovers!* 1956, *Only the Lonely* 1958, *No One Cares* 1959) are the file's male-vocalist anchor and the records that essentially invent the LP-as-conceived-album as a popular-music format. The contemporary tail (Cassandra Wilson, Diana Krall, Norah Jones at the jazz-pop boundary, Gretchen Parlato, Cécile McLorin Salvant) extends the canon into the present. This file has the volume's heaviest Scaruffi-weighting because Fantano's direct vocal-jazz coverage is essentially nonexistent — the placements are constructed almost entirely from Scaruffi's positions and from the editor's reading of what Fantano's stated criteria would produce on records he has not formally addressed.

**Jazz Fusion** is the electric collision. From roughly 1969 (Miles Davis's *In a Silent Way*, the broader Bitches Brew sessions that produced *Bitches Brew* in 1970, *On the Corner* in 1972, and the entire Miles electric-period catalog through *Get Up With It* and *Pangaea* and *Agharta*) forward, a wing of the post-bop generation begins fusing jazz harmonic and improvisational vocabulary with rock instrumentation, rock production, funk rhythm, and Indian classical music. Weather Report (Joe Zawinul and Wayne Shorter's band, formed 1970, running through the Jaco Pastorius period of *Heavy Weather* 1977 and *Mr. Gone* 1978) is the canonical fusion band and the file's most consensus-aligned placement. The Mahavishnu Orchestra (John McLaughlin, *The Inner Mounting Flame* 1971 and *Birds of Fire* 1973) is the form's most aggressive wing and its most direct fusion of Indian classical rhythmic frameworks with rock dynamics. Return to Forever (Chick Corea), Herbie Hancock's Mwandishi and Headhunters bands (*Headhunters* 1973 is the form's commercial peak), the Tony Williams Lifetime, the Pat Metheny Group's first three or four ECM-era records (which sit at the boundary between fusion and ECM-chamber), Steely Dan's mid-70s records (a borderline case the file argues through), and the entire Brazilian fusion wing (Hermeto Pascoal, Egberto Gismonti, Airto Moreira) round out the file. Fantano's fusion coverage is unusually direct compared to most of the older sub-genres in this volume — he has reviewed several Miles electric-period reissues and has stated positions on Weather Report and Headhunters — which makes the placements here more directly arrived at than the Vocal Jazz file's are.

**Avant-garde / Loft Jazz** is the post-free continuation. After the 1965-1970 free-jazz peak, a substantial portion of the New York avant-garde moved into the loft scene — the literal lofts in lower Manhattan (Studio Rivbea, Sam Rivers's loft; Studio We, Juma Sultan's loft; Ali's Alley, Rashied Ali's loft) where the post-Coltrane generation continued to develop free improvisation under conditions the commercial jazz infrastructure had stopped supporting. The loft scene's central document is the *Wildflowers* series (1976-1977), a five-LP set documenting Sam Rivers's Studio Rivbea performances. The loft scene's central figures are Sam Rivers himself, the AACM-aligned New York wing (Henry Threadgill, Air, the Anthony Braxton catalog through his Arista Freedom and Black Saint records, the early David Murray records, the World Saxophone Quartet), Cecil Taylor's continuing late-70s and 80s catalog, the various Strata-East label artists (also cross-listed in the spiritual-jazz file), and the broader downtown New York scene that would eventually feed into the John Zorn-and-around Knitting Factory generation of the 1980s and 90s. The file extends forward into the contemporary avant-garde (Vijay Iyer, Mary Halvorson, Tyshawn Sorey, the recent Henry Threadgill late catalog, the Wadada Leo Smith long-form compositions). Scaruffi's coverage of this material is encyclopedic; Fantano's is thinner but his recent reviews of Mary Halvorson and Tyshawn Sorey are direct.

**Third Stream** is the classical reach. The term was coined by Gunther Schuller in a 1957 lecture and refers to the deliberate fusion of jazz and Western classical-music vocabularies — not the older "symphonic jazz" of Paul Whiteman / Ferde Grofé / Gershwin's *Rhapsody in Blue*, but a more rigorous fusion that takes jazz improvisation and classical formal structure (sonata form, fugue, twelve-tone composition, serial techniques) seriously together. The Modern Jazz Quartet's third-stream collaborations (the *Third Stream Music* sessions with the Beaux Arts String Quartet, the *Comedy* and *Lonely Woman* records), Schuller's own compositional work, John Lewis's broader catalog, the early-60s George Russell records that overlap with modal jazz, the Charles Mingus *Epitaph* and *Black Saint and the Sinner Lady* (1963, anchored in this file rather than hard bop because of the through-composed long-form structure), the Anthony Braxton compositional work, and the more recent Maria Schneider Orchestra catalog round out the file. This is the volume's smallest and most consensus-aligned sub-genre in terms of its canon; the disputes are mostly about whether certain Mingus and Russell records anchor here or in their more obvious primary genres.

**ECM / Chamber Jazz** is the European aesthetic. Manfred Eicher founded ECM Records in Munich in 1969 with the explicit project of producing jazz records (and later contemporary classical records, and traditional / folk records) under a particular aesthetic regime: spacious recording, long sustained tones, a particular reverbed acoustic, a preference for European harmonic and structural sensibilities over American blues-and-gospel grounding, and a roster that drew heavily from the Norwegian, German, and Italian jazz scenes alongside the American figures who fit the aesthetic (Keith Jarrett centrally, Pat Metheny in his early-period work, Chick Corea, Charlie Haden in his Liberation Music Orchestra and Jasmine work, Don Cherry's *Codona* trio with Collin Walcott and Naná Vasconcelos). Keith Jarrett's *The Köln Concert* (1975) is the label's single most famous record and one of the best-selling solo piano records ever issued. The Jan Garbarek catalog, the Eberhard Weber records, the Terje Rypdal sides, the John Surman material, the Bobo Stenson Trio recordings, the contemporary roster (Tigran Hamasyan, Vijay Iyer's ECM material, Avishai Cohen's ECM work) — the file works through what is essentially a single-label aesthetic, which is unusual for the volume but accurate to how the music is actually canonized in jazz scholarship. Scaruffi's positions on the ECM catalog are sharply divided (he loves the European avant-garde wing, has more reservations about Jarrett's solo piano material) and the file's debates work through the disagreement explicitly.

**Smooth Jazz** is the maligned commercial branch. The form coalesces in the late 1970s out of the soul-jazz / jazz-funk wing's commercial continuation — Grover Washington Jr.'s *Mister Magic* (1975) and *Winelight* (1980), Bob James's CTI sessions, George Benson's *Breezin'* (1976), the entire CTI label's late catalog — and consolidates in the 1980s as a radio format ("smooth jazz" or "contemporary jazz") with its own format clock, its own production conventions, and its own commercial infrastructure. Kenny G's career (especially *Duotones* 1986 and *Breathless* 1992) is the form's commercial peak and the cultural object most associated with smooth jazz's reputation as the genre serious jazz listeners are obligated to dismiss. The file does not dismiss it. The artistic peaks of the form — Grover Washington Jr.'s *Winelight*, the late Wes Montgomery records on Verve and A&M, Stanley Turrentine's CTI material, Earl Klugh's acoustic-guitar records, the David Sanborn catalog, certain Bob James and Lee Ritenour sessions — are real records, and the file argues for their inclusion on artistic grounds rather than treating the form as a category mistake. Scaruffi is structurally hostile to the genre and rates almost no smooth-jazz records highly; Fantano's coverage is essentially absent. The file is the volume's most editor-driven, with placements constructed against both critics' typical positions and flagged accordingly.

**Nu Jazz / Future Jazz** is the electronic-era hybrid and the contemporary revival. The form has two distinct waves. The first is the late-90s and early-2000s European electronic-jazz scene — the Compost Records and !K7 catalogs, Jazzanova, Koop, Kruder & Dorfmeister, the broader broken-beat / nu-jazz / future-jazz axis around the Bugge Wesseltoft and Nils Petter Molvær and Erik Truffaz catalogs, the Cinematic Orchestra, the British acid-jazz scene's more sophisticated wing (the Brand New Heavies and Galliano at the popular end, Roy Ayers' continuing influence). The second is the post-2010 spiritual-jazz revival that overlaps with the spiritual-jazz file in Part Two — Kamasi Washington, the Floating Points / Pharoah Sanders / LSO *Promises*, the entire London scene around Shabaka Hutchings (Sons of Kemet, The Comet Is Coming, Shabaka and the Ancestors) and Nubya Garcia and Moses Boyd and Joe Armon-Jones, the Chicago / International Anthem axis around Makaya McCraven, Jeff Parker, Damon Locks's Black Monument Ensemble, Angel Bat Dawid, the Robert Glasper Experiment's hip-hop-jazz fusion, and the Christian Scott aTunde Adjuah catalog. This is the part of the volume where Fantano's direct critical work is densest — almost every record in the post-2010 portion of this file has been reviewed on the channel — and where his and Scaruffi's positions are actually arrived at through direct reviews rather than extrapolation. The contemporary revival is ongoing and the file's tail (the most recent two or three years of releases) is the most provisional in the volume.

Seven sub-genres. The structural argument this part makes is that jazz is not exhausted by the bop-to-free-to-spiritual narrative — the form has parallel traditions (vocalists, fusion, third stream, ECM) and continuation traditions (loft, nu jazz, the contemporary revival) that do work the central narrative does not capture. Reading this part after Parts One and Two is the right order; the dependencies run that direction (the avant-garde / loft file requires the free-jazz context, the nu-jazz file requires both the spiritual-jazz and fusion contexts, the smooth-jazz file reads against soul jazz and jazz-funk). The cross-listings into the earlier parts are dense and the file order tries to keep them legible.

---

### Vocal Jazz / Standards
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Vocal jazz is the genre where the two critics diverge most sharply by sheer absence. Piero Scaruffi (b. 1955) grew up inside the era when these records were the soundtrack of middle-class American adulthood, and his criticism — though famously skeptical of mainstream pop singers — does carve out reverence for Billie Holiday's late Verve and Commodore work, Sinatra's Capitol concept albums, the Ella Fitzgerald Songbooks (with caveats), Sarah Vaughan's middle period, and Nina Simone's jazz-side recordings. He treats the songbook tradition as a serious art form when it pushes against sentiment, and dismisses it when it doesn't. Anthony Fantano (b. 1985), by contrast, has essentially no direct vocal jazz commentary on record. He has touched Cassandra Wilson glancingly, name-checked Nina Simone, and reviewed the occasional contemporary jazz-adjacent vocalist (Cécile McLorin Salvant, José James), but the Sinatra/Ella/Holiday canon is outside his beat. **EXTRAPOLATION FLAG**: every Fantano position below is inferred from his stated values — preference for emotional rawness over technical polish, suspicion of arrangements that gloss the singer, interest in left-field interpreters over canonical readings, and a tendency to prefer the document of a struggling voice over the document of a healthy one.

The debate, then, is structurally lopsided. Scaruffi argues from a deep historical hierarchy in which Holiday > Sinatra > Vaughan > Fitzgerald is a defensible ordering. Inferred-Fantano argues from a different axis altogether — late-Holiday roughness, Nina Simone's political weight, and Cassandra Wilson's atmospheric reinvention all rank above the Capitol-era polish that Scaruffi treats as the medium's classical peak. Across six rounds, three positions held firm on both sides (Holiday's *Lady in Satin*, Simone's *Wild Is the Wind*, Sinatra's *In the Wee Small Hours*). Three were genuinely contested and went to coin-flip: the Sinatra-vs-Sinatra slot, the Ella Songbook selection, and the Cassandra Wilson question. The remainder settled by argument. The list below is therefore a hybrid: a Scaruffi-anchored canon with Fantano-inferred pressure pushing rougher, later, and more singular performances upward, and the polished orchestral readings downward — but never out, because the genre's identity is inseparable from them.

A second methodological caveat: vocal jazz is the genre most vulnerable to canon-bias because its peak commercial period (1955-1962) coincides almost exactly with the LP's emergence as an artistic medium. The list reads heavy with that window not because no important records were made afterward, but because the form's relationship to the album format was forged inside that window and has never re-formed since. A 1972 Carmen McRae live record or a 1990 Shirley Horn record functions as commentary on a tradition; a 1955 Sinatra record functions as the tradition itself. This produces an evaluative asymmetry that no honest list can fully resolve. Where the list privileges the foundational records, that is the structural argument; where it admits the later ones, that is the corrective. The debate rounds essentially negotiated the ratio of foundation to correction, and the final ratio (seven mid-century records, three later ones) reflects what survived sustained pressure from both sides.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Billie Holiday — *Lady in Satin* (Columbia, 1958)

Both critics converge here, for opposite reasons. Scaruffi has long defended *Lady in Satin* as Holiday's most artistically complete record — Ray Ellis's lush string arrangements set against a voice already ravaged by years of heroin, alcohol, and industry abuse, producing what he calls a document of "the soul singing through the wreckage of the body." For Scaruffi, the technical decline is the point: Holiday at 43 cannot do what Holiday at 28 could, and the gap between intention and execution becomes the record's expressive content. "I'm a Fool to Want You," "You've Changed," "For All We Know" — these are not performances of standards, they are autopsies of them.

Inferred-Fantano almost certainly ranks this even higher than Scaruffi does, and for the same reasons amplified. Fantano's stated aesthetic prizes raw emotional documentation over polish, which makes *Lady in Satin* — the most polished arrangement framing the most damaged voice — a perfect storm for his sensibility. The Ellis strings, which traditionalists sometimes complain "smother" Holiday, are exactly what Fantano-inferred would defend: they're the ironic frame that makes the wreckage legible. He would likely call this the *Pink Moon* of vocal jazz — a final-statement record where the production conspires with the artist's deterioration to produce something neither could have made alone.

The album was recorded over three nights in February 1958. Holiday would die seventeen months later. She reportedly cried after hearing the playbacks — partly because she knew. Both critics agree this is the genre's defining masterpiece because it is the one record where the form's central paradox (a singer interpreting other people's words about other people's pain) collapses entirely into autobiography. The standards become hers; "I'll Be Around" becomes a lie she's telling herself. No other vocal jazz record sits closer to the bone.

##### 2. Frank Sinatra — *In the Wee Small Hours* (Capitol, 1955)

Scaruffi's pick for the best Sinatra record, and the one he will defend against any challenger. *In the Wee Small Hours* is the first true concept album in American popular music — sixteen ballads about loneliness, loss, and 3 a.m. self-recrimination, sequenced as a single emotional arc, framed by Nelson Riddle's restrained, after-hours arrangements. Recorded during the slow-motion collapse of Sinatra's marriage to Ava Gardner, it inverts the swinging-bachelor persona that *Songs for Swingin' Lovers!* would consolidate a year later. This is the Sinatra of confessional interiority: phrasing slowed, vibrato narrowed, the public voice withdrawn into a register that sounds private even at full volume.

Inferred-Fantano's relationship to Sinatra is the genre's biggest extrapolation problem. There is nothing in his stated catalogue that suggests engagement with the Capitol-era Sinatra mythology. But the values he applies elsewhere — preference for emotionally specific records over career-summary ones, suspicion of "swinging" affect, attraction to singers documenting their own internal weather — all point to *Wee Small Hours* as the Sinatra record he would care about, if he cared about Sinatra. He would probably be cooler on it than Scaruffi (the production is too pristine, the persona too constructed), but he would not put *Songs for Swingin' Lovers!* above it.

The record's innovation is structural: Sinatra and Riddle understood the LP as a long-form medium five years before Miles Davis would arguably do the same with *Kind of Blue*. The track-to-track sequencing matters; "In the Wee Small Hours of the Morning" is the thesis, "What Is This Thing Called Love?" is the question, "Last Night When We Were Young" is the answer. It belongs at #2 because it founds the form Holiday's *Lady in Satin* would, three years later, blow apart from the inside.

##### 3. Nina Simone — *Wild Is the Wind* (Philips, 1966)

Scaruffi has consistently defended Nina Simone's jazz-side recordings against the genre purists who exclude her, and *Wild Is the Wind* is his preferred document. It is the album where Simone's compositional voice — the classical training, the gospel cadences, the political fury, the Brecht-Weill theatricality — finds its tightest synthesis. "Four Women," her own composition, is here. "Lilac Wine," "Black Is the Color of My True Love's Hair," and the Tiomkin/Washington title track receive readings that no one else could produce, because Simone is not interpreting standards in the Sinatra-Ella tradition — she is appropriating them, re-keying them into her own ethical and harmonic universe.

Inferred-Fantano almost certainly ranks Simone higher than Scaruffi does. Fantano has positively name-checked Simone in podcast contexts, and his stated framework — political weight as artistic weight, suspicion of "neutral" interpretation, attraction to singers whose voices carry their biographies — fits Simone exactly. He would probably advocate for *Wild Is the Wind* over the better-known *I Put a Spell on You* because of "Four Women" and the title track's vocal performance.

The record sits at #3 because it expands the genre's definition while still functioning inside it. Simone is interpreting standards; she is also interrogating them. "Black Is the Color" is read as a love song and as a racial statement simultaneously, and the form holds both meanings without collapsing. This is what vocal jazz can do that other vocal traditions cannot: hold contradictory readings open within a single phrase. Both critics agree this matters; they would only mildly disagree about ranking.

##### 4. Ella Fitzgerald & Louis Armstrong — *Ella and Louis* (Verve, 1956)

Scaruffi prefers this to any single Songbook volume, and the argument is straightforward: the Songbooks are catalogues, *Ella and Louis* is a record. Eleven duets, Oscar Peterson trio plus Buddy Rich, an hour of two of the most distinct vocal instruments in American music in conversation. Armstrong's gravel against Fitzgerald's clean-line phrasing produces a textural contrast that neither singer's solo work can match, and the relaxed Verve session feel — Norman Granz's signature production approach — gives the record an intimacy the Songbooks' grandeur cannot.

Inferred-Fantano's position here is harder to read. He has shown some skepticism of "summit meeting" records elsewhere (the Method Man / Redman dynamic, e.g.), but his preference for performance-document records over studio-construct records would favor *Ella and Louis*. He would likely have less patience for the lighter material ("Tenderly," "A Foggy Day") and would gravitate to the Gershwin readings and "They Can't Take That Away From Me," which sits as the record's emotional center.

This album is included partly because Ella Fitzgerald's catalogue is otherwise hard to rank — the Songbook series is monumental but uneven, and choosing one volume (Cole Porter? Gershwin? Ellington?) to represent her is a coin-flip exercise the resolution section addresses below. *Ella and Louis* sidesteps that problem by being a complete artistic statement on its own terms, with both singers operating at peak. It also documents, in passing, the only recorded extended dialogue between the genre's two foundational vocal approaches — Armstrong's improvisatory roots, Fitzgerald's interpretive precision — which is reason enough for a top-five placement.

##### 5. Frank Sinatra — *Frank Sinatra Sings for Only the Lonely* (Capitol, 1958)

Sinatra's own pick for his best record, and the more extreme version of the *Wee Small Hours* template. Where *Wee Small Hours* is melancholic, *Only the Lonely* is desolate; where Riddle's earlier arrangements pull back, here he commits fully to slow-tempo orchestral darkness. "Angel Eyes," "One for My Baby," "What's New," "Gone with the Wind" — twelve songs, all in the same emotional register, sustained at a length that nearly breaks the listener.
