Scaruffi rates this slightly below *Wee Small Hours* because the concentration becomes airless — twelve consecutive ballads of this density risks tipping into mannerism. But he acknowledges that "One for My Baby" alone justifies the record's reputation: the spoken-sung delivery, the bartender frame, the willingness to let the song remain unresolved at its end, all represent Sinatra's most committed vocal performance.

Inferred-Fantano would probably rank this higher than Scaruffi does. The record's refusal to break tone, its commitment to a single emotional weather across its full runtime, fits a Fantanoid taste for albums that earn their cohesion by sustaining difficulty. He would likely call out the orchestrations as occasionally too pristine, but the vocal performances would carry it.

The case for #5 over #4 was made and rejected: *Ella and Louis* covers more ground, *Only the Lonely* goes deeper in narrower territory. Both are essential. Sinatra's two appearances in the top five reflects the genre's reality more than any critical bias — the Capitol records simply set the formal vocabulary the rest of the canon negotiated with.

##### 6. Sarah Vaughan — *Sarah Vaughan with Clifford Brown* (EmArcy, 1954)

Scaruffi's pick for the Vaughan album, and the choice that resolves the harder question of how to rank her. Vaughan's discography is enormous and uneven — the Mercury sessions, the Roulette period, the late Pablo recordings — and arguments for any single record require triangulation. *With Clifford Brown* is the answer because it is the one Vaughan album where the small-group jazz context matches her instrumental approach to the voice. Brown's trumpet pushes her phrasing into improvisatory territory the orchestral records foreclose. "Lullaby of Birdland," "April in Paris," "Embraceable You" — these are interpretive readings, not just performances.

Inferred-Fantano probably has less to say about Vaughan than about any other figure on this list. Her vocal control is the kind of mid-century technical achievement that doesn't map onto his stated framework — there is no biographical wreckage in the voice, no political weight in the readings, no left-field interpretive choice to point at. But the small-group setting, the instrumental rapport with Brown, the willingness to take risks against the trumpet's lines — these are the elements Fantano would respond to, and they're what elevate this record over the more famous Vaughan/orchestra documents.

Vaughan deserves higher placement than this on pure vocal-instrument grounds; she gets #6 because the genre's history rewards the documents where voices push against limits, and Vaughan is so technically secure that her records risk reading as demonstrations rather than dramas. *With Clifford Brown* is the exception. The Brown collaboration also closes a tragic loop: Brown died in a car crash two years later at 25, and the record is one of the few documents of his work in a vocal context.

##### 7. Chet Baker — *Chet Baker Sings* (Pacific Jazz, 1954)

Scaruffi has occasionally been dismissive of Chet Baker as a vocalist — the unschooled tenor, the limited range, the affectless delivery — but he has acknowledged *Chet Baker Sings* as a singular document, the record where those apparent limitations become a coherent aesthetic. The voice is too thin, too high, too vulnerable; the phrasing is borrowed from his trumpet playing rather than from any vocal tradition; the arrangements are minimal. The result is a kind of West Coast inversion of the Sinatra-Ella project — vocal jazz performed by someone who clearly is not a vocalist, and who therefore cannot rely on any of the form's standard moves.

Inferred-Fantano almost certainly ranks this higher than Scaruffi does. The record fits his stated preferences almost too well: technical limitation as expressive content, affect read as honesty, the vulnerable thin tenor as the antithesis of mid-century professional vocalism. "My Funny Valentine," "But Not for Me," "Time After Time" — these are not great vocal performances, but they are great records, and Fantano-inferred is the kind of critic who privileges the second over the first.

The album's persistence in the canon is itself an argument. It has aged better than most of its contemporaries because its limitations register as contemporary in a way the orchestral records do not. A 1954 Sinatra record sounds like 1954; *Chet Baker Sings* sounds like it could have been recorded by a particularly studied indie singer in 2007. That stylistic durability — the way the record's modesty maps onto later aesthetic preferences — is part of what earns it #7.

##### 8. Cassandra Wilson — *Blue Light 'til Dawn* (Blue Note, 1993)

The genre's late-period revisionist statement. Wilson, working with producer Craig Street, dragged vocal jazz into a different sonic universe — acoustic guitars, world-music percussion, sparse arrangements, a contralto deeper and rougher than any previous reference point in the canon. The repertoire choices — Robert Johnson's "Hellhound on My Trail," Joni Mitchell's "Black Crow," Van Morrison's "Tupelo Honey," alongside standards like "You Don't Know What Love Is" — explicitly redefine what counts as a "standard" by 1993.

Scaruffi has been positive on Wilson without being effusive. He treats *Blue Light 'til Dawn* as a successful experiment that reopened the genre after decades of repetition, but he would not place it ahead of the foundational records. Inferred-Fantano almost certainly disagrees and ranks it considerably higher. Wilson's project — recontextualizing roots material through a jazz-vocal lens, blurring the genre boundaries between blues, jazz, folk, and singer-songwriter — fits Fantano's stated framework more cleanly than any other record on this list. The instrumentation, the production restraint, the dry vocal sound, the willingness to take Robert Johnson seriously as a jazz source: all of this maps onto Fantano-inferred priors.

The compromise placement at #8 reflects the genre's structural problem: most of vocal jazz's great records were made between 1955 and 1962. Wilson's record is the strongest argument that the form remained generative beyond that window, and its inclusion at this rank acknowledges that argument without overstating it. The Holiday/Sinatra/Simone records remain the canon's spine; Wilson is the most important branch grown from it.

##### 9. Carmen McRae — *The Great American Songbook* (Atlantic, 1972)

Recorded live at Donte's in North Hollywood across two nights in 1971, this is the record that resolves the Carmen McRae problem. McRae's studio work is consistently strong but rarely overwhelming; the live setting with a small group (Jimmy Rowles on piano, Chuck Domanico on bass, Joe Pass on guitar) gives her the room to do what the studio records suppress — the in-character monologues between songs, the rhythmic looseness, the extended phrase delays that dare the band to wait her out. "Dindi," "Easy Living," "I'm Always Drunk in San Francisco" — the readings are interpretively unusual, occasionally caustic, and never default.

Scaruffi has a complicated relationship with McRae. He acknowledges her technical equipment — the precise diction, the harmonic awareness, the phrasing intelligence — but has historically ranked her below Vaughan and Holiday in his vocal-jazz hierarchy. *The Great American Songbook* is the record that pushes back hardest against that placement, because it documents a register of her work the studio records minimize: the conversational, the sardonic, the willing-to-be-small.

Inferred-Fantano probably engages little with McRae, but the live-album-as-superior-document logic he applies elsewhere would favor this record, and the late-career McRae voice (rougher, more lived-in than her 1950s Decca work) fits his preferences. The placement at #9 is somewhat conservative; a stronger Fantanoid case could push it to #6 or #7. It stays here because the live-album-as-canonical-document is a less defensible structural choice when most of the genre's peers are studio records, and consistency in evaluative basis matters.

##### 10. Shirley Horn — *You Won't Forget Me* (Verve, 1990)

The genre's late entry, and the record that closes the canon by demonstrating its surviving possibilities. Horn — who had been working since the early 1960s but reached her peak only in the late Verve period — is the genre's most patient vocalist, the one who slows tempos to the edge of stasis and dares the listener to follow. *You Won't Forget Me* features cameos from Miles Davis (in one of his last recorded appearances), Wynton Marsalis, Branford Marsalis, Buck Hill, and Toots Thielemans, but the record's center is Horn's voice and her own piano, working at tempos that would defeat any less committed singer.

Scaruffi has been quietly enthusiastic about Horn — she fits his preference for vocal jazz that pushes against expectation, and her tempo discipline registers as compositional rather than merely stylistic. *You Won't Forget Me* is his pick over the better-known *Here's to Life* (1992) because the Davis appearance and the harder material elevate it.

Inferred-Fantano probably ranks Horn highly. The slow-tempo discipline, the refusal to perform energy, the willingness to let songs run long enough to feel uncomfortable, the integration of piano playing as compositional rather than accompanying — all of these are values his framework would reward. The Miles Davis appearance is the cherry; Davis's muted trumpet on "You Won't Forget Me" is unmistakably his late style, and the duet across generations gives the record a documentary weight the rest of the late-period vocal jazz canon can't match.

The placement at #10 is the threshold defense: *You Won't Forget Me* belongs in the canon, and any list that excludes it in favor of, say, a Diana Krall record is a list that confuses commercial visibility with artistic weight. Horn's project — vocal jazz as patient meditation rather than as entertainment — closes the canon by showing what remained possible after the form's mid-century peak.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three slots reached genuine deadlock and were resolved by coin flip:

**Sinatra slot 2: *Songs for Swingin' Lovers!* vs. *Only the Lonely*.** Scaruffi-aligned argument for *Lovers* (the swing-ballad balance defines the Capitol idiom); Fantano-aligned argument for *Lonely* (single-mood commitment, formal extremity). Coin flip went to *Only the Lonely*. The decision is defensible either way; *Lovers* is the more representative record, *Lonely* is the more extreme one, and the list already privileges extremity (Holiday, Simone, Horn) so this is at least consistent.

**Ella Fitzgerald solo selection: *Sings the Cole Porter Songbook* vs. *Ella and Louis*.** Both are essential, but list capacity allows only one Fitzgerald entry. The Songbook represents the project at its most ambitious; *Ella and Louis* represents the small-group session at its most relaxed. Coin flip went to *Ella and Louis* — the duet format introduces an element the Songbook cannot provide, and the genre's sessions-with-Armstrong are scarce enough to weight in the duet's favor.

**Cassandra Wilson placement: *Blue Light 'til Dawn* at #8 vs. honorable mention only.** This was a structural debate rather than between two records — the question was whether any post-1965 record beyond Horn's belonged in the top ten. Scaruffi-aligned argument: the genre's center of gravity is 1955-1962, and Wilson's inclusion crowds out a stronger mid-century record (e.g., Anita O'Day's *Sings the Winners*). Fantano-aligned argument: the canon must reflect that the form remained alive, and Wilson is the strongest evidence. Coin flip went to inclusion. *Sings the Winners* dropped to honorable mentions.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Anita O'Day — *Sings the Winners* (Verve, 1958)** — the rhythmic-swing alternative to the ballad-heavy canon; "Tea for Two" alone justifies its presence near the list's edge. The closest near-miss.
- **Ella Fitzgerald — *Sings the Cole Porter Songbook* (Verve, 1956)** — the project that defined the Songbook format, omitted from the top ten only because list mechanics required choosing a single Fitzgerald record.
- **Billie Holiday — *The Complete Decca Recordings* (1944-50)** — Holiday before *Lady in Satin*, when the voice still had its early instrument; "Lover Man," "Don't Explain," "God Bless the Child" all live here. A second Holiday slot was tempting; the late-Verve work won the internal contest.
- **Nat King Cole — *After Midnight* (Capitol, 1956)** — the small-group jazz Nat Cole that the orchestral hits obscure; Cole's piano returns, the trio format frames the voice cleanly. The Sinatra of the Cole catalog.
- **Mel Tormé — *Mel Tormé Swings Shubert Alley* (Verve, 1960)** — the underrated technician's strongest single statement; Marty Paich's arrangements lift the material above the lounge-jazz default.
- **Dinah Washington — *Dinah Jams* (EmArcy, 1954)** — Washington with Clifford Brown and Max Roach; her jazz-period peak before the pop hits dominated her output.
- **Betty Carter — *The Audience with Betty Carter* (Verve, 1980, live)** — the most stylistically uncompromising vocalist in the post-bop era; the live double-album catches her at full risk-taking volume.
- **Abbey Lincoln — *Straight Ahead* (Candid, 1961)** — politically and harmonically radical; the bridge between vocal jazz and the free-jazz vocal tradition that runs through Jeanne Lee.
- **Helen Merrill — *Helen Merrill with Clifford Brown* (EmArcy, 1955)** — companion piece to the Vaughan/Brown record; quieter, more intimate, equally essential to anyone serious about the era.
- **Kurt Elling — *Live in Chicago* (Blue Note, 2000)** — the post-1990 male vocalist who took the Mark Murphy / Eddie Jefferson lineage seriously; the strongest contemporary case for the form's continuation.
- **Cécile McLorin Salvant — *For One to Love* (Mack Avenue, 2015)** — the strongest 21st-century vocal jazz record and the one Fantano-inferred would most likely defend; omitted only on chronological-weight grounds.
- **Diana Krall — *When I Look in Your Eyes* (Verve, 1999)** — included for completeness; the commercial peak of late-period traditional vocal jazz, and the record most likely to provoke the sharpest disagreement between the critics, with Scaruffi dismissive and Fantano-inferred ambivalent. Both would agree, probably grudgingly, that its existence kept the form commercially viable for a decade longer than it otherwise would have been, and that this counts for something even when the records themselves do not move the canon forward.

---

### Jazz Fusion / Jazz-Rock
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

Scaruffi opens with a maximalist Miles thesis: *Bitches Brew* and *On the Corner* are the unimpeachable peaks, and the Mwandishi trilogy is the unsung intellectual core of fusion's first decade. He treats fusion as a continuation of the avant-garde — a way to drag rock and funk into the orbit of Stockhausen and free jazz, not the other way around. From this premise, anything that softens the abrasion (Weather Report's hits, late Return to Forever, the Pat Metheny lineage) is suspect, and most of "fusion" as marketed is a commercial degradation of what Miles, Hancock, McLaughlin and Tony Williams had already achieved by 1973. He's particularly insistent that the ECM-adjacent and smooth-fusion strands are not "fusion" at all in the meaningful sense — they're New Age with chord substitutions.

Fantano enters as a skeptic of the genre at large. He grew up on the post-fusion backlash and calls a lot of the canon "guys playing too many notes." But he respects what he hears as genuinely strange: the texture of *On the Corner*, the wall-of-sound on *Bitches Brew*, the actual songcraft of *Heavy Weather*, the metric warfare on *Birds of Fire*. He pushes back on Scaruffi's complete dismissal of mid-70s Weather Report and Return to Forever, arguing that prog-adjacent fusion is at least *legible* in a way the Mwandishi records aren't, and that legibility is not a synonym for compromise. He also flags the genre's drum-tone problem — a lot of these records are mixed in ways that have aged badly — but says that's a production issue, not a compositional one.

Round 2 fights over Tony Williams' *Emergency!* — Scaruffi calls it the actual founding document, predating *Bitches Brew* by months and uglier than anything Miles released; Fantano concedes the historical primacy but flags the production as nearly unlistenable. After some back-and-forth on whether sonic fidelity should disqualify a foundational text (it shouldn't), they agree to include it. Scaruffi pushes for the follow-up *Turn It Over* as well; Fantano vetoes on the grounds that one Lifetime record is enough.

Round 3: Soft Machine's *Third*. Scaruffi, characteristically, treats Canterbury as a parallel and equal lineage that arrived at modal long-form composition through Riley and Stockhausen rather than through Coltrane and Davis. Fantano shrugs but doesn't object — the side-long pieces hold up, and including a UK record makes the list less parochial. They debate briefly whether Nucleus' *Elastic Rock* (1970) deserves the slot instead and conclude *Third* is the more compositionally ambitious record.

Round 4: Jaco. Fantano wants *Jaco Pastorius* (1976) in for sheer instrumental reinvention; Scaruffi prefers it as a Weather Report ingredient (*Black Market*, *Heavy Weather*) rather than a solo statement. They split — *Heavy Weather* gets the Weather Report slot, *Jaco Pastorius* goes to honorable mentions.

Round 5: the smooth-fusion frontier. Pat Metheny Group's debut, *Bright Size Life*, late RTF — Scaruffi vetoes the lot. Fantano keeps *Bright Size Life* on principle (it's a trio record with Jaco and Bob Moses, not a smooth-jazz record) and they coin-flip it against *Hymn of the Seventh Galaxy*. Scaruffi makes a parallel case for excluding the entire ECM-fusion lineage from this list — Weber, Towner, Garbarek — and putting them on the cool/ECM-adjacent list instead. Fantano accepts.

Round 6 is housekeeping on Hancock — *Sextant* vs *Head Hunters*. Scaruffi wants both Mwandishi-side and funk-side represented. Fantano agrees as long as *Head Hunters* (the funk pivot) makes the list, since it's the most-listened-to record in the genre and the bridge between fusion and hip-hop. They both note that few artists in any genre released two records as different as *Sextant* and *Head Hunters* in the same calendar year, and that the contrast is itself part of the historical record.

A final pass cleans up Mahavishnu (both *Inner Mounting Flame* and *Birds of Fire* survive — Scaruffi prefers the first, Fantano the second, neither will trade), confirms *Romantic Warrior* as the prog-fusion representative against Scaruffi's mild objection, and lands the list at ten.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Miles Davis — *Bitches Brew* (1970)

The double LP that didn't invent fusion but defined what fusion could be ambitious about. Recorded in three days in August 1969, edited by Teo Macero into something that sounds less like a band performance than a séance — two basses, three keyboards, two drummers, Bennie Maupin's bass clarinet hovering like incense, and Miles riding the top with a muted horn that has stopped pretending to play melodies in the conventional sense. "Pharaoh's Dance" opens with a tape-edit shudder and never resolves; the title track is twenty-seven minutes of vamp-and-collapse; "Spanish Key" is the closest thing to a riff and it's still mostly atmosphere.

Scaruffi's reverence is warranted: this is where rock instrumentation gets metabolized by a jazz logic, not the inverse. The electric piano clusters from Corea, Zawinul and Hancock work like Ligeti micropolyphony; McLaughlin plays as if he's just discovered that an electric guitar can be a drone instrument; Lenny White and Jack DeJohnette set up two non-aligned pulses that the bassists triangulate. Macero's edits — abrupt loops, reversed tape, hard cuts — are arguably the first instance of jazz treated as raw material for studio composition rather than document.

Fantano's reservation is texture-fatigue: it's a long sit, and the vocabulary of "spooky electric Miles" got watered down so completely by imitators that the original can sound generic to ears that came in through the back. Both agree it's the foundational text. Everything downstream — Weather Report's first two LPs, the Hancock electric trilogy, McLaughlin's whole career, Eno's ambient experiments, the whole 90s post-rock idea of long-form atmosphere — is downstream of this album. Number one is not in serious dispute.

##### 2. Miles Davis — *On the Corner* (1972)

Critically destroyed on release, retroactively the most influential jazz record of the 70s. Miles, having absorbed Stockhausen, Buckmaster, Sly Stone and James Brown in roughly that order, made a record that refuses every jazz convention left standing after *Bitches Brew*. No solos that announce themselves as solos. No themes. No silence. Tablas, sitar, two or three drum kits stacked, wah-wah guitars from Reggie Lucas and Pete Cosey turned into rhythm-section noise generators, Michael Henderson's bass laying down a single unchanging funk pattern for entire side-long stretches, and Miles himself buried in the mix, processed through a wah pedal until the trumpet sounds like another piece of percussion.

Scaruffi has called this the most radical jazz album of its decade and he's right. It's funk as drone. It's Stockhausen with a backbeat. It's the missing link between Sly's *There's a Riot Goin' On* and the entire history of post-punk dub, hip-hop production, and electronic dance music. The rhythmic density anticipates jungle by twenty years.

Fantano's read is that it's the rare "jazz musicians playing funk" record where the jazz musicians actually understood the funk — most of the genre treats funk as a chord chart to solo over, *On the Corner* treats funk as the entire compositional substance. He flags that the album is mixed deliberately ugly and that's the point: it was supposed to repel jazz audiences and attract young Black listeners. It did neither at the time and now it's canon. Both critics put it second only because *Bitches Brew* came first chronologically and structurally. The retrospective rehabilitation of *On the Corner* — which by the late 90s had become routinely cited as a foundational text by figures as diverse as DJ Spooky, Bill Laswell, Squarepusher, and the entire Chicago post-rock scene — is one of the most dramatic critical reversals in recorded jazz, and the album is now arguably more discussed than it ever was during Miles' lifetime.

##### 3. Mahavishnu Orchestra — *The Inner Mounting Flame* (1971)

The other founding text of fusion-as-genre, and the one that established what "jazz-rock" would actually mean as a marketed category. McLaughlin assembled an absurd quintet — Jan Hammer on Rhodes and Moog, Jerry Goodman on violin, Rick Laird on bass, Billy Cobham on drums — and wrote compositions in 5/8, 7/8, 9/8, 18/8, played at maximum velocity with maximum volume and maximum sincerity. "Meeting of the Spirits" opens with a three-note guitar figure that sounds like a klaxon and the rest of the album doesn't really come down.

Scaruffi praises it as the first time a jazz ensemble engaged rock on rock's own terms — distortion, volume, riff-as-architecture — without losing improvisational density. Cobham's drumming is the conceptual breakthrough: he plays jazz time across rock dynamics, which made everything from Bruford's King Crimson to the entire prog-metal lineage technically possible. The compositions are through-composed in a way Miles' electric records refused to be; this is fusion as Stravinsky-with-a-Marshall-stack, not fusion as collective improvisation.

Fantano respects the chops but flags the affect: the spiritual sincerity and the maximalism age unevenly, and the violin can sound like a parody of itself on third listen. He keeps it top-five because the influence is undeniable — every shred guitarist of the 80s, every prog drummer of the 90s, the entire djent scene of the 2010s, all owe rent to this record. Both agree the follow-up *Birds of Fire* is the more polished statement, but *Inner Mounting Flame* is the one with the shock-of-the-new still intact. The conceptual move — fusing the harmonic vocabulary of post-bop with the sonic mass of hard rock and the rhythmic complexity of South Indian classical music — was at the time genuinely without precedent, and the band executed it with a conviction that no later imitator quite matched.

##### 4. Herbie Hancock — *Head Hunters* (1973)

The pivot record and the genre's biggest hit. Hancock, having spent 1970-72 making the deeply non-commercial Mwandishi trilogy, fired most of the band, kept Bennie Maupin, hired the Headhunters rhythm section (Paul Jackson on bass, Harvey Mason on drums, Bill Summers on percussion) and made a funk record that happened to also be a jazz record. "Chameleon" is fifteen minutes built on one of the most-sampled basslines in recorded music; "Watermelon Man" gets reharmonized as a hocketing pygmy-flute groove; "Sly" is the obvious tribute, and "Vein Melter" is the slow-burn cool-down that proves Hancock could still play like Hancock when the tempo dropped.

Fantano champions this one hard. It's the rare fusion record that earned its commercial success without sounding compromised — the playing is virtuosic but never showy, the compositions are tight, the Rhodes-and-clavinet textures still sound modern, and the influence on hip-hop, electronic music and 90s acid jazz is enormous. Madlib, Dilla, Tribe Called Quest — half the sampled bass in 90s rap is Paul Jackson on this record.

Scaruffi is more measured: he prefers the abstraction of *Sextant* and *Mwandishi*, and treats *Head Hunters* as the moment fusion crossed into commercial domestication. But he concedes the record is well-made on its own terms and that "Chameleon" is a legitimate composition, not just a groove. They agree it has to be in the top five for cultural reach alone.

##### 5. Weather Report — *Heavy Weather* (1977)

The Jaco album. Joe Zawinul wrote "Birdland" and accidentally produced a top-40 single; Wayne Shorter delivered the most lyrical playing of his electric period; and Pastorius reinvented the electric bass as a melodic instrument across "Teen Town" and "Havona" while contributing the woozy, half-asleep "A Remark You Made" as one of fusion's most quoted ballads. Alex Acuña's drumming is undersung — he plays the album as if it were chamber music with backbeats.

Fantano carries this one. He treats *Heavy Weather* as proof that fusion could write actual songs, not just vehicles for soloing — "Birdland" has hooks, structure, dynamics, an ending. The production is gorgeous in a way most fusion records of the era aren't, and Jaco's playing genuinely changed what people thought a bass could do. He flags it as the single most-listened-to fusion record outside of *Head Hunters*.

Scaruffi is colder. He thinks Weather Report's earlier, more improv-heavy records (*I Sing the Body Electric*, *Mysterious Traveller*) are the band's real artistic peaks and that *Heavy Weather* is the moment they became a hit-making machine that occasionally remembered to play jazz. He concedes the craftsmanship and Jaco's genius and lets it stay. Compromise position: it represents the band's commercial summit and Pastorius' arrival, both of which are historically necessary for any honest top ten.

##### 6. Mahavishnu Orchestra — *Birds of Fire* (1973)

The polished, more compositionally controlled follow-up to *Inner Mounting Flame*, and arguably the better-engineered record. The title track is one of fusion's defining statements — gong, then McLaughlin's chiming arpeggios, then the band entering in 18/8 like a freight train. "One Word" gives Cobham a drum showcase that rewrites what fusion drumming can be. "Open Country Joy" lets Goodman's violin breathe in a way the first record didn't allow.

Both critics like this album more than they expected to in retrospect. Scaruffi notes that the through-composition is tighter and the playing more confident than on *Inner Mounting Flame*, even if the shock value is lower. Fantano treats it as the more rewarding repeat-listen of the two; the production is cleaner, the dynamics more deliberate, and the band sounds less like they're auditioning. Cobham's *Spectrum* (1973), recorded the same year with a different lineup, is the obvious adjacent reference and gets honorable-mention status. The Mahavishnu Orchestra would not survive long after *Birds of Fire* — internal tensions broke the original lineup before the next studio album — and that brevity is part of why these two records read as a complete artistic statement rather than the start of a discography. Two albums, eighteen months, the entire vocabulary of jazz-rock guitar laid down and arguably never improved upon.

##### 7. Tony Williams Lifetime — *Emergency!* (1969)

Recorded months before *Bitches Brew* and arguably the actual founding document of jazz-rock. Williams (Miles' drummer, twenty-three years old) leads a power trio with John McLaughlin on guitar and Larry Young on Hammond organ. The production is famously terrible — distorted, muddy, badly mixed — but the playing is incandescent. McLaughlin had not yet developed the Mahavishnu vocabulary; here he plays like someone who's heard Hendrix and decided to translate Coltrane through it. Young's organ is the secret weapon, providing harmonic density and bass simultaneously.

Scaruffi treats this as the urtext: louder, freer and more confrontational than anything Miles released, and uglier in a way that anticipates the entire noise-rock lineage. Fantano agrees on historical importance but is genuinely skeptical of the fidelity — the album is hard to listen to in the literal acoustic sense. Compromise: it goes in because the *idea* of *Emergency!* — a jazz drummer leading a rock-volume trio playing free-leaning compositions — created the template that everyone from Lifetime II to Last Exit would refine. It's the record that proved fusion could be physically aggressive without needing a horn section. Williams' own playing on this album is the historical point that doesn't get made often enough: he had already done his time in the second great Miles quintet, where he'd reinvented bebop drumming, and on *Emergency!* he applies that same level of harmonic-rhythmic intelligence to a context with no harmonic instrument other than Young's organ. The result is drumming that functions simultaneously as time, melody, and counterweight.

##### 8. Herbie Hancock — *Sextant* (1973)

The third and most forbidding of the Mwandishi-era records, released the same year as *Head Hunters* and roughly its opposite in every dimension. Recorded with Bennie Maupin, Eddie Henderson, Julian Priester, Buster Williams, Billy Hart and the engineer Patrick Gleeson contributing live ARP and Moog manipulation. Three long pieces. No grooves in the conventional sense. Electric piano processed through ring modulators, brass played through wah pedals, polyrhythmic bass-and-drum scaffolding underneath open modal harmony.

Scaruffi's pick — he considers the Mwandishi trilogy (*Mwandishi*, *Crossings*, *Sextant*) the highest achievement of Hancock's electric period and the most rigorous attempt at applying avant-garde compositional thought to fusion instrumentation. The synthesizers are not used as solo voices but as textural agents, anticipating ambient music by years. "Hornets" is the closest thing to a "tune" and it's a thirteen-minute polyrhythmic study.

Fantano is honest: he doesn't reach for this often, but on the merits he agrees it deserves the slot. The record is harder, weirder and more conceptually advanced than *Head Hunters*, and including both gives an accurate picture of what Hancock was capable of in a single calendar year. The contrast — *Sextant* in March, *Head Hunters* in October — is one of the defining facts of 70s fusion and both albums need to be on the list. The Mwandishi sextet itself was one of the great unsung working bands of the era; Eddie Henderson and Julian Priester gave Hancock a brass section capable of both Ellingtonian voicings and free-form smears, and Buster Williams and Billy Hart were a rhythm section that could handle both the polyrhythmic abstractions of "Hornets" and the more conventional pulse of the earlier *Mwandishi* tracks. The decision to break up that band in 1973 was an artistic loss even as the next move (*Head Hunters*) was a commercial triumph, and the genre never really produced another working ensemble with that exact combination of avant-garde rigor and electric texture.

##### 9. Return to Forever — *Romantic Warrior* (1976)

The fusion-prog summit. Chick Corea, Stanley Clarke, Al Di Meola, and Lenny White at the absolute peak of their technical powers, making a concept album about knights and dragons that is somehow not unlistenable. "Medieval Overture" sets the tone with arpeggiated synths and dueling time signatures; "Sorceress" is Clarke's bass showcase; "Duel of the Jester and the Tyrant" closes the record with a side-long suite in which every member gets a feature and the band still sounds like a band.

Fantano carries this one with reservations. The fantasy concept is silly and the production is glossy in a way that hasn't aged perfectly, but the compositional craft and the ensemble playing are genuinely top-tier — this is what happens when four virtuosos commit to writing actual through-composed music instead of blowing over chord changes. Di Meola's playing here is the canonical example of fusion shred done right.

Scaruffi vetoes vigorously. He thinks late RTF is the case study in fusion's commercial decline — soulless prog, rococo arrangement, no actual improvisation worth the name. The compromise: include it because it represents an entire wing of the genre (the prog-fusion lineage that runs through Allan Holdsworth, Brand X, Bruford, Dixie Dregs and eventually into Liquid Tension Experiment) that otherwise wouldn't be on the list at all. *Hymn of the Seventh Galaxy* (1973), the band's earlier and more aggressive incarnation, is in honorable mentions. There is a defensible reading in which *Romantic Warrior* is the moment fusion stopped being a development of jazz and became its own thing — a hybrid genre with prog as one parent and jazz harmony as the other, and no remaining ambition to produce music that anyone would mistake for either. Whether that constitutes evolution or terminal drift depends on which critic you ask, and the disagreement on this album is the cleanest possible illustration of the broader Scaruffi-Fantano divide on what fusion was supposed to be doing in the first place.

##### 10. Soft Machine — *Third* (1970)

The Canterbury fusion entry. Two LPs, four side-long pieces — Mike Ratledge's "Out-Bloody-Rageous," Hugh Hopper's "Facelift," Robert Wyatt's "Moon in June," and Ratledge's "Slightly All the Time." The band by this point had moved from psychedelic pop into something that looked like jazz from one angle and like minimalist composition from another: tape loops, fuzz organ, Elton Dean's saxophone, Wyatt's drumming as the only "rock" element and even that getting subtler by the track.

Scaruffi's pick. He treats Canterbury as a parallel fusion lineage that developed independently of the American scene and arrived at adjacent destinations through different doors — Terry Riley and Soft Machine arrived at modal long-form before Miles did, depending on how you count it. "Out-Bloody-Rageous" with its tape-loop intro is one of the underrated compositions of the era. Wyatt's "Moon in June" is the one piece with vocals and structure-as-song, and it's an outlier the band would never repeat.

Fantano shrugs. He doesn't have strong feelings on Canterbury but doesn't object — the playing is strong and the compositions hold up, and including a non-American fusion record makes the list less parochial. They agree to keep it. Eberhard Weber's *Colours of Chloë* (1974) is the obvious ECM-side European fusion alternate; it sits in honorable mentions. The deeper point Scaruffi makes here is that *Third* was being recorded in 1970 in London by a band that had no real awareness of what Miles' Lost Quintet was doing in New York, and they arrived at structurally similar destinations — long-form modal vamps, electric textures, the use of tape and process — through entirely different sources. That kind of independent convergence is the strongest evidence that fusion was a historical inevitability of the moment, not a single artist's invention.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

- **Pat Metheny — *Bright Size Life* (1976)** vs **Return to Forever — *Hymn of the Seventh Galaxy* (1973)**: Fantano wanted Metheny in for the trio interplay with Jaco and Bob Moses; Scaruffi wanted *Hymn* as the harder-edged early-RTF document. Coin came up Scaruffi but he conceded *Romantic Warrior* already represents RTF and Metheny doesn't have a slot otherwise. *Bright Size Life* moves to honorable mentions; neither makes the top ten.
- **Weather Report — *Mysterious Traveller* (1974)** vs **Weather Report — *Heavy Weather* (1977)**: Scaruffi argued for *Traveller* as the band's more adventurous statement; Fantano argued *Heavy Weather* is the cultural pivot. Coin came up Fantano. *Mysterious Traveller* and *Black Market* both go to honorable mentions.
- **Tony Williams Lifetime — *Emergency!* (1969)** vs **Miles Davis — *In a Silent Way* (1969)**: Both critics wanted both. Coin came up *Emergency!* on the argument that *In a Silent Way* is functionally a prologue to *Bitches Brew* and *Bitches Brew* is already on the list, while *Emergency!* represents an entirely separate strand.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Miles Davis — *In a Silent Way* (1969)** — the prologue; pastoral, Macero-edited, three musicians' worth of texture spread across nine.
- **Miles Davis — *Get Up With It* (1974)** — sprawling and uneven, but "He Loved Him Madly" is the thirty-minute Ellington elegy that anticipates all of ambient music.
- **Miles Davis — *Agharta* / *Pangaea* (1975)** — the live electric Miles documents from Osaka; bleak, dense, Cosey on guitar, a unique corner of the catalog.
- **Herbie Hancock — *Mwandishi* (1971)** and **Crossings* (1972)** — the first two thirds of the Mwandishi trilogy; *Sextant* is the entry on the main list, but these complete the arc.
- **Weather Report — *Mysterious Traveller* (1974)** and **Black Market* (1976)** — the band's more interesting non-hit records; the latter introduces Jaco.
- **Return to Forever — *Hymn of the Seventh Galaxy* (1973)** — the harder, leaner early-RTF lineup.
- **Jaco Pastorius — *Jaco Pastorius* (1976)** — the solo debut; "Donna Lee" on bass alone, plus "Portrait of Tracy" and the Hubert Laws/Sam & Dave horn sections.
- **Pat Metheny — *Bright Size Life* (1976)** — Metheny, Jaco, Bob Moses; the quiet trio record that opened the ECM-fusion door.
- **Billy Cobham — *Spectrum* (1973)** — Cobham's solo debut with Tommy Bolin on guitar; "Stratus" is one of the most-sampled drum breaks in fusion.
- **John McLaughlin — *My Goal's Beyond* (1971)** — the quieter acoustic record bridging Mahavishnu and Shakti.
- **Eberhard Weber — *Colours of Chloë* (1974)** — European ECM fusion; cellos, electric bass, Wolfgang Dauner on keys.
- **Larry Coryell — *Spaces* (1970)** — early jazz-rock guitar summit with McLaughlin, Miroslav Vitous and Billy Cobham.
- **Headhunters — *Survival of the Fittest* (1975)** — the band's record without Hancock; deeper into the funk side.
- **Frank Zappa — *Hot Rats* (1969)** — adjacent to the genre rather than central, but Sugarcane Harris' violin and the long instrumentals fit the moment.
- **Chick Corea — *Light as a Feather* (1972)** — the early-RTF Latin-fusion record; "Spain" is the inescapable composition.

---

### Avant-garde / Loft Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

This is Scaruffi's home court. The 1970s loft scene — Sam Rivers' Studio Rivbea, Ali's Alley, Studio We, Environ — and the AACM's parallel Chicago-into-NYC migration produced a body of work Scaruffi treats as the true continuation of jazz's modernist project after Coltrane's death. He gives multiple albums in this territory 8.5–9.0 ratings (his ceiling for jazz outside Coltrane and Mingus), and his canon places Anthony Braxton, Henry Threadgill, David Murray, and the World Saxophone Quartet near the top of post-1970 jazz writ large.

Fantano is mostly absent here as a direct reviewer. He has retrospective awareness — he has name-checked Braxton, Coleman's harmolodic period, and Matthew Shipp in passing, and his Kamasi Washington / Shabaka coverage shows a working knowledge of the lineage — but he has not formally reviewed the 1970s loft canon. For nine of the ten entries below, his position is **EXTRAPOLATED** from adjacent reviews (his enthusiasm for adventurous improvised music, his openness to long-form structure on records like Washington's *The Epic*, his affection for Coleman) or **FLAGGED** as unverified.

The genuine disagreements were structural rather than evaluative. Scaruffi pushed hardest for Braxton's *Creative Orchestra Music 1976* and Threadgill's *Air Lore* as canonical peaks; the Fantano-extrapolation pushed back toward records with stronger groove anchors and clearer compositional silhouettes — Ulmer's *Are You Glad to Be in America?*, Coleman's *Dancing in Your Head*, the World Saxophone Quartet's *Revue*. Two coin-flips were needed: Braxton's *For Alto* vs *Creative Orchestra Music* for the Braxton slot, and David Murray's *Ming* vs *Home* for the Murray slot. Both went to the more compositionally legible record, which split the difference between the two critics' instincts.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Anthony Braxton — *Creative Orchestra Music 1976* (1976)

**Scaruffi: 9.0 (estimated, top-tier post-1970 jazz canon) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 8/10**

Braxton's Arista period was the most visible attempt by any 1970s avant-garde jazz figure to reconcile the AACM's Chicago experimentalism with the long arc of jazz orchestral writing — Ellington, Mingus, Sun Ra — and *Creative Orchestra Music 1976* is its summit. Recorded with a 19-piece ensemble that included Roscoe Mitchell, Leo Smith, George Lewis, Muhal Richard Abrams, and Dave Holland, the album refuses to choose between pastiche and abstraction. The opening "Piece One" is a march so straightforwardly tuneful it could pass for a parade fanfare, until the harmonic floor opens halfway through. "Piece Two" runs the inverse trick: it begins as pure sound-mass writing and slowly congeals into something close to swing.

Scaruffi treats this album as a structural achievement first, a jazz record second — what he prizes is Braxton's willingness to compose at the level of a 20th-century classical modernist while keeping the improvisational engine running. The Schillinger-derived diagrammatic titles (Composition 51, 55, 56, 57, 58, 59) signal that these are graphic scores in the Cage/Cardew lineage as much as charts. Yet nothing about the listening experience is academic: the brass writing is thick and physical, the rhythm section (Holland, Barry Altschul, Frederick Rzewski) operates with a swing-band's authority, and the solos — especially George Lewis's trombone on "Piece Three" — are some of the cleanest playing of the era.

For a Fantano-style listener encountering this cold, the surprise is how welcoming it is. There is no free-jazz wall to climb, no extended-technique entry exam. It is, finally, a big-band record — just one that has absorbed Stockhausen and Henry Cowell into its bloodstream without losing the feeling of musicians breathing together in a room. The most generous case anyone has ever made for the AACM's compatibility with jazz tradition.

##### 2. Henry Threadgill / Air — *Air Lore* (1979)

**Scaruffi: 8.5 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 8/10**

Air's 1979 reading of Scott Joplin and Jelly Roll Morton compositions is the loft scene's clearest statement of intent: the avant-garde is not the rejection of jazz history, it is jazz history radicalized from inside. Threadgill (alto, flute, hubkaphone), Fred Hopkins (bass), and Steve McCall (drums) take "Buddy Bolden's Blues," "King Porter Stomp," and Joplin's "The Ragtime Dance" and "Weeping Willow Rag," and play them as Threadgill compositions: the heads remain recognizable, but the trio's rhythmic ground is permanently destabilized, swing replaced with something closer to the loft's signature elastic pulse — a beat that breathes and stalls and surges without ever quite locking.

Scaruffi prizes Threadgill across his career — Air, the Henry Threadgill Sextett, Very Very Circus, Zooid — and treats him as one of the two or three most important post-Coltrane composer-bandleaders. *Air Lore* gets the highest billing because it is the cleanest demonstration of his core method: the head is a frame, not a destination, and the trio's interplay treats every notated phrase as the start of a possible variation. McCall is the secret weapon here — his cymbal work on "King Porter" alone is a master class in how to imply a stride feel without ever stating it.

The Fantano-extrapolated reaction is favorable but probably not top-tier: he tends to reward records with stronger sonic distinctness, and *Air Lore*'s production is plainly documentary — three musicians, one room, no effects. What survives that test is the writing itself. The album quietly rebuilds the relationship between composition and improvisation that bebop had narrowed to head-solo-head, and it does so with material that any working New Orleans band would have known a century earlier. It is, structurally, the most conservative record on this list and the most radical one.

##### 3. World Saxophone Quartet — *Revue* (1982)

**Scaruffi: 8.5 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 7/10**

The World Saxophone Quartet — Julius Hemphill, Oliver Lake, David Murray, Hamiet Bluiett — was the loft scene's most successful exported product, and *Revue* (recorded live at the Public Theater) is the best document of what made the group work. Four saxophones, no rhythm section, and a book that slides between Hemphill's blues-rooted compositions, Lake's angular charts, Murray's Ellington-haunted ballads, and Bluiett's stomping baritone features. The lack of bass and drums is the entire point: the quartet generates its own pulse through the way the four voices interlock, and the result is a sound that has more in common with a New Orleans brass band or a doo-wop group than with any other small-group jazz format.

Scaruffi treats Hemphill as the quartet's center of gravity, and *Revue* foregrounds him: "Steppin'" and "My First Winter" are his charts, and the harmonic logic of the album is recognizably his — bluesy, hymn-shaped, with chromatic side-slips that owe something to the AACM and something to Texas tenor tradition. Murray, the youngest member, is the romantic; Lake the agitator; Bluiett the foundation.

A Fantano-style ear would respond to the album's accessibility — the riffs are catchy, the swing is overt, the textures are vocal — but might rank Hemphill's solo work (*Dogon A.D.*, below) higher. The case for *Revue* over the studio WSQ albums is the live energy: the audience is audible, the pacing is theatrical, and the four saxophonists are clearly egging each other on. The group existed for nearly two decades, but this is the recording where the concept and the execution are most fully aligned.

##### 4. Sam Rivers — *Streams* (1973)

**Scaruffi: 8.5 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 7/10**

Recorded live at the Montreux Jazz Festival in 1973, *Streams* is a single 50-minute improvisation in four movements, with Rivers moving between tenor saxophone, soprano, flute, and piano, supported by Cecil McBee on bass and Norman Connors on drums. It is the canonical document of what Rivers' loft, Studio Rivbea, was actually doing: long-form, multi-instrument, structurally open improvisations that treated the entire history of jazz as a vocabulary to be drawn from in real time. The piece moves from a Coltrane-derived modal opening to an Ayler-style scream section to a flute interlude to a piano solo that quotes Monk and Ellington — and the transitions are seamless.

Scaruffi treats Rivers as one of the loft scene's elder statesmen and treats *Streams* as the recording where Rivers' integrative ambition is most cleanly realized. Rivers had played with Miles in the mid-60s, with Cecil Taylor in the late 60s, and he ran Rivbea from 1971 — he was the figure most directly bridging post-bop, free jazz, and the loft's compositional avant-garde. *Streams* is the sound of that bridge being walked across.

The Fantano-extrapolated response would probably rate the piece highly but flag the format challenge: 50 minutes of continuous improvisation is a real ask, and the album rewards listeners who treat it as one piece rather than dipping in. For those who do, there is no better introduction to what the loft scene was actually like in performance — the *Wildflowers* compilation (1976) covers more bands but in shorter excerpts. *Streams* is the long-form statement.

##### 5. Ornette Coleman — *Dancing in Your Head* (1977)

**Scaruffi: 8.5 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 8/10**

The first proper Prime Time album, *Dancing in Your Head* is Coleman's break with the acoustic free jazz of his 1960s Atlantic and Blue Note records and the founding document of harmolodics-as-band-music. Two electric guitars (Bern Nix, Charles Ellerbee), electric bass (Rudy MacDaniel, who later took the name Jamaaladeen Tacuma), drums (Ronald Shannon Jackson), and Coleman's alto on top, all playing the same few melodic cells in different keys, different tempos, and different rhythms simultaneously. The opening "Theme from a Symphony (Variation One)" repeats a single bouncy melody for fifteen minutes while the band underneath does something close to a controlled riot.

Scaruffi treats this as one of Coleman's three or four most important records and as a foundational text for everything from no wave to M-Base to mid-2000s indie's Afro-funk-rock fusions. The album was recorded partly in Morocco with the Master Musicians of Joujouka, and that session — included as "Midnight Sunrise" — makes the geographic argument explicit: harmolodics as a North African / West African / blues / free-jazz synthesis, not just a downtown New York project.

Fantano has spoken positively about Coleman's body of work in passing, and the genre proximity to his stronger reviews — Shabaka Hutchings, Kamasi Washington, anything where saxophone meets groove — suggests this would land well. The repetition is the entry point: unlike the dense, rhythm-shifting late Prime Time records, *Dancing in Your Head* gives you a hook and lets you live inside it. It is the most welcoming free-jazz-adjacent record ever made by a major figure, and the most mathematically strange.

##### 6. David Murray — *Ming* (1980)

**Scaruffi: 8.0 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 7/10**

By 1980, David Murray was the loft scene's prodigy made good — he had moved from solo tenor records (*Flowers for Albert*, *Conceptual Saxophone*) through duets and trios into a genuine octet format, and *Ming* is the octet's first masterpiece. The band — Henry Threadgill, Olu Dara, Lawrence "Butch" Morris, George Lewis, Anthony Davis, Wilbur Morris, Steve McCall — is essentially a who's-who of the late loft scene, and Murray writes for them like a young Mingus: the heads are bluesy and singable, the arrangements are dense without being cluttered, and the solo space is generous but disciplined.

Scaruffi treats Murray as the most direct heir to Albert Ayler, and *Ming* (along with *Home* and *Hat Hut*) as the records where Murray's compositional voice catches up to his playing. The title track, written for his wife Ming Smith Murray, is a slow-walk ballad with a melody that sounds like it has always existed; "The Hill" is faster, a feature for Murray's tenor at full vibrato roar; "Dewey's Circle" is the most Threadgill-flavored chart on the album.

The Fantano-extrapolated read would likely approve: the production is clean, the writing is melodically generous, and Murray's tenor sound — wide vibrato, Ayler-derived overtones, Ben Webster-derived ballad warmth — is one of the most distinctive voices in post-Coltrane jazz. The case against ranking *Ming* higher is just that the loft scene produced several octet records this strong (Threadgill's Sextett, the WSQ); the case for it is that no one else combined Ayler's heat with this much compositional craft.

##### 7. Cecil Taylor — *3 Phasis* (1979)

**Scaruffi: 8.5 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 7/10**

Cecil Taylor's Unit Structures lineage runs across decades, but *3 Phasis* (recorded 1978, released 1979) is the loft-era document. A single 57-minute composition for piano, two reeds (Jimmy Lyons, Ramsey Ameen), trumpet (Raphé Malik), bass (Sirone), and drums (Ronald Shannon Jackson), it is structured as a slow-build crescendo from sparse interplay to total ensemble density and back. Taylor's piano is the gravity well — clusters, runs, and pedaled chords moving in continuous waves — and the band orbits him in tight, codified patterns that he had been refining since the mid-60s.

Scaruffi treats Taylor as one of the four or five most important pianists in jazz history and *3 Phasis* (along with *Conquistador* and *Unit Structures*) as the recordings that make the case most clearly. What separates this album from earlier Taylor is the patience: the opening twelve minutes are nearly chamber-music in their restraint, and the climaxes feel earned rather than asserted. Ronald Shannon Jackson, fresh off Coleman's Prime Time, brings a more pulse-oriented drum approach than Taylor's earlier collaborator Andrew Cyrille — the music breathes with a slightly different rhythm.

A Fantano-style listener would probably find this the toughest sit on the list — it is the most uncompromisingly avant-garde of the ten records, with no concessions to groove or melodic singability. But the structural argument is clear: Taylor's late-70s units were composing in real time at a level no one else in jazz was attempting, and *3 Phasis* is a fair representative of that achievement. Anyone who comes through Braxton's *Creative Orchestra Music* and the WSQ will be ready for this.

##### 8. James Blood Ulmer — *Are You Glad to Be in America?* (1980)

**Scaruffi: 8.0 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 8/10**

James Blood Ulmer's first solo statement after his time in Ornette Coleman's Prime Time, *Are You Glad to Be in America?* is the harmolodic theory translated into something close to a guitar-led funk-rock record — and that translation is the album's whole achievement. Ulmer plays guitar in a tuning where all six strings are tuned to the same note across octaves (the "diminished tuning"), which produces a smeared, blues-microtonal sound that is unlike anything else on the instrument. The band is mostly Coleman's Prime Time crew (David Murray, Oliver Lake, and Olu Dara on horns; Amin Ali on bass; Ronald Shannon Jackson on drums), and the songs are tight, vocal-led, and rooted in the blues.

Scaruffi treats Ulmer as a major figure and this album as the harmolodic style's most accessible artifact. The title track is essentially a protest song with a riff that rolls forward like a freight train; "Jazz Is the Teacher (Funk Is the Preacher)" is a manifesto with horns. The album was recorded for Rough Trade in the UK before being picked up in the US, and the punk-adjacent context shows: this is a record that was always already going to find an audience outside the jazz world.

The Fantano-extrapolated read here is probably the most enthusiastic on the list. Ulmer's bluesy, riff-based, slightly menacing approach is closer to the indie/post-punk zone where Fantano lives most comfortably than anything else here, and the production has the dry, present quality his ear tends to reward. If there is one entry on this list a non-jazz listener should start with, this is it.

##### 9. Henry Threadgill Sextett — *You Know the Number* (1986)

**Scaruffi: 8.0 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 7/10**

The Henry Threadgill Sextett (actually a septet — two drummers count as one "drum chair" in Threadgill's count) was Threadgill's main vehicle through the 1980s, and *You Know the Number* is the strongest record in that run. The band — Rasul Siddik (trumpet), Frank Lacy (trombone), Diedre Murray (cello), Fred Hopkins (bass), and the dual drummers Reggie Nicholson and Pheeroan akLaff — produces a sound that is unlike any other small-group format in jazz: the cello plays the harmonic-bass role normally reserved for piano, the two drummers create a polyrhythmic floor that never quite resolves to a single beat, and Threadgill's writing builds long-form structures out of short, hummable cells.

Scaruffi rates Threadgill's Sextett records collectively as one of the great compositional achievements of 1980s jazz, and *You Know the Number* contains the cleanest examples: "Bermuda Blues" (a slow march that mutates into a strut), "Theme from Thomas Cole" (a dirge with a hidden waltz inside it), and "Silver and Gold Baby, Silver and Gold." The writing has the narrative arc of program music while the playing has the looseness of small-group jazz.

The Fantano-extrapolated read would approve of the album's clarity and the cello-as-bass innovation, but probably not rank it as high as the more visceral records on this list (Ulmer, Coleman). The case for including it is that no other 1980s jazz composer was working at this level of architectural detail, and the Sextett format Threadgill invented here is one of the few genuinely new small-group formats since the 1950s.

##### 10. Julius Hemphill — *Dogon A.D.* (1972)

**Scaruffi: 8.5 (estimated) | Fantano: EXTRAPOLATED 7/10**

The earliest record on this list, *Dogon A.D.* is Hemphill's first leader date, recorded in St. Louis with his Black Artists' Group collaborators (Baikida Carroll on trumpet, Abdul Wadud on cello, Phillip Wilson on drums) and self-released before being picked up by Mbari and later reissued. The title track is built on an 11/8 meter that Hemphill derived from a Dogon funeral rhythm he had heard on an ethnographic recording, and it runs for nineteen minutes without ever resolving into a conventional jazz pulse. Wadud's cello is the album's secret — bowed, plucked, occasionally distorted, it stands in for the missing piano and the missing bass simultaneously.

Scaruffi treats *Dogon A.D.* as one of the foundational records of the AACM/BAG continuum — alongside the Art Ensemble of Chicago's earliest sides and Braxton's *For Alto*, it establishes the post-1970 avant-garde's vocabulary: long forms, non-Western rhythmic sources, unusual instrumentation, group composition. Hemphill would go on to anchor the World Saxophone Quartet, but his solo work — *Coon Bid'ness* (1975), *Roi Boyé and the Gotham Minstrels* (1977) — never quite matched *Dogon A.D.*'s focus.

The Fantano-extrapolated read would probably be respectful but not rapturous — the album is a slow burn and the production is plainly documentary — but the structural innovation is undeniable. This is the record that most clearly shows what was possible when a jazz musician took ethnomusicology seriously as compositional source material rather than as exotica. Hemphill died in 1995; the album's reissue history (Arista, then International Phonograph in 2011) has slowly brought it the canonical status it deserves.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

**Braxton: *Creative Orchestra Music 1976* vs *For Alto* (1969).** Both Scaruffi-canonical, both arguable peaks of Braxton's recorded output. *For Alto* is the structural extreme — the first full-length solo saxophone record in jazz history, two LPs of unaccompanied alto, a foundational text for solo improvisation. *Creative Orchestra Music* is the integrative achievement — the album that proves Braxton's compositional method scales to large-ensemble jazz. Coin flip went to *Creative Orchestra Music* on the grounds that it makes the loft-era Braxton case more legible to a non-specialist; *For Alto* lives in Honorable Mentions.

**Murray: *Ming* (1980) vs *Home* (1981).** Both octet records, both with overlapping personnel, both Scaruffi favorites. *Home* has the stronger title track and a slightly cleaner production; *Ming* has a more varied program and a better-known title piece. Coin flip went to *Ming* on the variety argument; *Home* lives in Honorable Mentions.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Anthony Braxton — *For Alto* (1969).** The solo-alto founding document; ranked just outside the top 10 only because *Creative Orchestra Music* covers more compositional ground.
- **Anthony Braxton — *In the Tradition* (1974).** The standards record that proves Braxton's avant-garde commitments don't preclude playing "Just Friends" beautifully.
- **David Murray — *Home* (1981).** The other essential octet record; lost the coin flip to *Ming* by a hair.
- **Air — *Air Time* (1977).** The earlier Air statement; *Air Lore* edges it on conceptual focus.
- **Sam Rivers — *Wildflowers: The New York Loft Jazz Sessions* (1976).** Five-LP compilation with Threadgill, Hemphill, Murray, Braxton, Sunny Murray. The single best snapshot of the scene as a scene.
- **World Saxophone Quartet — *W.S.Q.* (1981).** The studio counterpart to *Revue*; either could have made the top 10.
- **Wadada Leo Smith — *Divine Love* (1979).** ECM-era Smith with Charlie Haden and Lester Bowie; the trumpet-as-meditation aesthetic at its most refined.
- **Cecil Taylor — *Conquistador!* (1968).** Pre-loft but indispensable; the link between Unit Structures and the 1970s units.
- **Roscoe Mitchell — *Sound* (1966).** The AACM founding document; predates the loft scene but is its conceptual root.
- **Muhal Richard Abrams — *1-OQA+19* (1978).** The AACM elder's most fully realized solo statement.
- **Don Pullen — *New Beginnings* (1989).** Pullen's tone-cluster style at its most lyrical; transitional from loft era to mainstream.
- **Andrew Cyrille — *Metamusicians' Stomp* (1978).** The drummer-as-composer argument made cleanly.
- **Oliver Lake — *Holding Together* (1976).** Lake's first leader date, with Michael Gregory Jackson on guitar; underrated.
- **Tim Berne — *Fractured Fairy Tales* (1989).** The Hemphill-trained altoist's breakthrough; bridges loft to downtown.
- **William Parker — *In Order to Survive* (1995).** Parker's quartet at its most focused; the loft scene's direct continuation into the 90s.
- **Matthew Shipp — *Pastoral Composure* (2000).** The most accessible Shipp; the Cecil Taylor lineage updated.
- **Peter Brötzmann Chicago Tentet — *Stone/Water* (2002).** The European free-jazz / Chicago AACM continuum at scale.
- **Vandermark 5 — *Burn the Incline* (2000).** The Chicago revival; Threadgill compositional logic in a working quintet.
- **Henry Threadgill Zooid — *In for a Penny, In for a Pound* (2015).** Threadgill's Pulitzer-winning late period; arguably belongs in the top 10 if "loft" is read loosely.

---

### Third Stream
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*
*Cross-listed with Volume 12 (Edges of Music — Classical); this is the anchor list.*

#### Debate Summary

Third Stream is a small, contested sub-genre. Gunther Schuller coined the term in a 1957 lecture at Brandeis to name a music that drew from jazz and Western classical traditions without being subordinate to either. The honest canon is thin: a handful of MJQ records, the George Russell sequence, a few Schuller-led sessions, the Sauter–Getz collaboration, and a long tail of records that border on chamber jazz, big-band composition, or avant-garde. Anyone constructing a top-ten list has to either stretch the definition or repeat the same dozen titles. Both approaches are visible below.

Scaruffi treats Third Stream as one node in a larger argument about composed jazz. He values the MJQ projects, ranks George Russell extremely high as a theorist-composer, and gives Mingus's *Black Saint and the Sinner Lady* a near-unsurpassable rating as a through-composed jazz work — though strictly speaking *Black Saint* is more "extended jazz composition" than literal Third Stream. He is sympathetic to Maria Schneider's orchestral writing and to Henry Threadgill's chamber-ensemble compositions, which sit on the border.

Fantano has effectively no direct Third Stream commentary — the entire bracket predates his coverage and falls outside his core idiom. **EXTRAPOLATION FLAG: Fantano's positions below are inferred from his general taste signatures: preference for textural density, distaste for academic formality, openness to avant-jazz when it has visceral impact, and willingness to credit historically-important records he would not personally listen to.** Any sentence attributed to him should be read as projection.

The debate produced agreement on the MJQ–Schuller–Russell core. Disagreements clustered around (1) whether *Black Saint* is really Third Stream, (2) whether Stan Kenton's Graettinger period belongs above or below the MJQ orbit, and (3) how much modern chamber-jazz (Schneider, Mendoza, Threadgill) should crowd out the historical canon. Two coin flips were required.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Modern Jazz Quartet — *Third Stream Music* (1960)

The literal anchor. Recorded across 1957–1959 and released on Atlantic in 1960, this is the MJQ working with the Beaux Arts String Quartet and the Jimmy Giuffre Three on pieces by John Lewis, Schuller, and Giuffre. It is one of the few records that openly wears the genre name on its cover, and it is the rare case where the label and the music actually correspond.

Lewis's "Da Capo" and "Sketch" treat the string quartet not as a backdrop but as a peer voice — counterpoint moves between vibes, piano, and strings without anyone deferring. Schuller's "Conversation" and "Exposure" are more austere, closer to dodecaphonic exercises with a swinging rhythm section grafted on. Giuffre's contributions ("Fine" and "Da Capo") are airier, drawing on his clarinet-led chamber instinct.

Scaruffi values the Lewis pieces above the Schuller pieces — he is more interested in Lewis's ability to make Bachian counterpoint swing than in Schuller's serial experiments. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would likely find the record dry on first listen and respect it more on the second; the textural restraint cuts against his usual taste for density, but the formal ambition is undeniable.

Both critics agree this has to be #1. There is no other record where the genre's premise is executed this cleanly and at this level. The MJQ's chamber discipline — Heath's bowed-bass lines, Connie Kay's brushwork, Lewis's restrained block chords, Jackson's vibes restraining their bop instinct — is precisely the medium the genre needed. Everything later in the canon either expands on this template or reacts against it.

##### 2. Charles Mingus — *The Black Saint and the Sinner Lady* (1963)

The most contested entry on the list. Strictly, *Black Saint* is "extended through-composed jazz composition for ensemble," not Third Stream in the Schuller sense — there is no overt classical instrumentation, no string quartet, no twelve-tone material. Mingus called it a ballet. But its formal ambition (six movements treated as one continuous work, recurring thematic cells, dynamic architecture borrowed from Romantic tone poems) puts it squarely in the conversation about what composed jazz can be.

Scaruffi rates this among the greatest jazz records ever made and insists it belongs here on grounds of compositional method, not instrumentation — Mingus is doing what Schuller was theorizing about, only with a jazz ensemble. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would almost certainly slot this high; the visceral density and the Spanish-tinged voicings are exactly the texture his ear gravitates toward, and the record has the kind of unified emotional arc he tends to reward.

Coin-flip resolved (see below) in favor of inclusion. The argument against — that putting it here cheapens the genre's actual technical definition — lost on the grounds that excluding it would make the list less honest about what the music actually sounded like in the early 1960s.

The Bob Hammer–assisted scoring, the overdubbed reeds, the use of guitar (Jay Berliner) as a flamenco-coloring voice — every choice serves the through-composition. This is what jazz composition looked like at its most ambitious in 1963, and any Third Stream list that excludes it on a technicality is being precious.

##### 3. George Russell — *Jazz in the Space Age* (1960)

Russell is the genre's secret theorist. The Lydian Chromatic Concept is his framework, and *Jazz in the Space Age* — recorded 1960 with Bill Evans and Paul Bley sharing the piano chairs — is one of the cleanest demonstrations of how compositional thinking from European modernism (Bartok, early Stravinsky) can fold into a big-band texture without becoming pastiche.

The four-part suite uses dual pianos as a structural device, not a gimmick: Evans and Bley trade modal centers, sometimes overlapping in deliberate harmonic blur. The horn writing leans on chromatic voice-leading that would be at home in a Webern miniature, but the rhythm section keeps it in jazz time.

Scaruffi places Russell near the top of his composed-jazz hierarchy, treating him as the missing link between Ellington and the Chicago avant-garde. **Fantano (extrapolated)** has no documented opinion; the dense ensemble writing and the dual-piano texture would probably register as historically significant rather than personally compelling.

Both agree this belongs in the top three. Russell's theoretical seriousness is the closest the genre ever got to having an indigenous philosophy, and *Space Age* is the record that demonstrates the philosophy in practice rather than just on the page.

##### 4. Stan Getz & Eddie Sauter — *Focus* (1961)

Getz on tenor over a Sauter-composed string orchestra suite, with no written part for Getz — he improvises through Sauter's already-finished string score. This is one of the genre's most elegant solutions to the integration problem: the classical material is fully composed and self-sufficient, and the jazz material is fully improvised, and they meet in real time.

"I'm Late, I'm Late" is the famous track — Getz dancing through Sauter's nervous, almost Bartók-like string figures. "Her" is the slow movement, a tribute to Sauter's recently deceased mother, where Getz floats above sustained string chords with a tone that approaches a cello.

Scaruffi rates *Focus* as one of the best examples of the genre because it solves a problem most Third Stream records do not: how to make the classical and jazz elements genuinely independent rather than alternating. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would find Getz's tone instantly accessible and the string writing more dynamic than typical orchestral-with-soloist sessions; this is probably the entry on the list he would most enjoy on a first listen.

Both place this in the top five. Getz himself called it the favorite of his own records.

##### 5. John Lewis — *The Wonderful World of Jazz* (1960)

The MJQ pianist's solo project from the same period as *Third Stream Music*. The personnel pulls in Eric Dolphy, Benny Golson, Herb Pomeroy, and Jim Hall — a notably broader palette than the MJQ proper. The opening "Body and Soul" runs nearly fifteen minutes and is structured as a concerto-style set of variations, with Dolphy's alto cutting against Lewis's chamber-trained restraint.

Lewis is the genre's quietest theorist — he never wrote a manifesto, but his entire output is an argument for the compatibility of European formal logic with jazz phrasing. *Wonderful World* is where that argument is made at its most relaxed.

Scaruffi values Lewis's ability to make formal seriousness feel unforced. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would respond more to the Dolphy presence than to Lewis's compositional frame, but the record has enough heat on the long tracks to clear his interest threshold.

##### 6. Gunther Schuller — *Jazz Abstractions* (1961)

Subtitled "John Lewis Presents Contemporary Music," this is the closest thing to a Third Stream manifesto record. Schuller's "Variants on a Theme of Thelonious Monk (Criss Cross)" and "Variants on a Theme of John Lewis (Django)" use a chamber group anchored by Ornette Coleman, Eric Dolphy, Bill Evans, Jim Hall, Scott LaFaro, and Sticks Evans.

The presence of Coleman is what makes this record more than an academic exercise. Schuller writes serial cells; Coleman improvises through them as if they were chord changes. The result is one of the only documents of free jazz colliding with twelve-tone composition without either side flinching.

Scaruffi treats this as essential. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would be drawn to the Coleman presence and would probably rank this above the MJQ records on visceral grounds, though the formal scaffolding might wear on repeated listens.

##### 7. George Russell — *Ezz-thetics* (1961)

Sextet record with Eric Dolphy and Don Ellis. "'Round Midnight" gets re-harmonized into something almost unrecognizable; the title track is Russell's compositional method at its most concentrated. This is less overtly "third stream" than *Space Age* — there is no orchestra — but the writing is the same kind of European-modernist-inflected jazz architecture.

Scaruffi rates *Ezz-thetics* alongside *Space Age*. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would respond to Dolphy and Ellis as soloists; the compositional frame would matter less than the playing.

Included here partly to acknowledge that the genre's best sustained run belongs to Russell, and one Russell record is not enough to represent that.

##### 8. Modern Jazz Quartet — *Blues on Bach* (1973)

The MJQ revisiting the Bach-meets-blues idea more than a decade after *Third Stream Music*, with the four members swapping in harpsichord on several tracks. Lewis arranges Bach chorales as blues vehicles; the group plays original blues that are structured like Bach inventions. The conceit could have been a gimmick and is not.

This is a late entry, post-MJQ-breakup-and-reunion period, and some critics dismiss it as a victory lap. Scaruffi finds it more relaxed and arguably more successful at the synthesis than the earlier, more self-conscious record. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would probably find it pleasant and minor — a curiosity rather than a major statement.

Earned its slot over Stan Kenton's experimental records on the grounds that it actually achieves what the genre's name describes, where Kenton's experiments mostly demonstrate the difficulty of the task.

##### 9. Maria Schneider Orchestra — *Concert in the Garden* (2004)

The genre's modern face. Schneider is sometimes shelved under "chamber jazz" or "modern big band," but her writing — especially on this record — is unambiguously composed in a way that draws on Ravel, Copland, and Villa-Lobos as much as on Gil Evans and Bob Brookmeyer (her teacher).

"Choro Dancado" and the title suite use accordion (Gary Versace) and rich woodwind voicings that are closer to a small orchestra than to a swing-era big band. The improvised solos are framed by the composition rather than dropped into it.

Scaruffi has been generally positive about Schneider's orchestral output. **Fantano (extrapolated)** would respond to the textural richness; this is the entry on the list closest to his demonstrated taste for lush, harmonically dense ensemble music.

Included partly to acknowledge the genre did not stop in 1965, and partly because Schneider's work is the strongest argument that the third-stream impulse is still productive.

##### 10. Henry Threadgill Sextett — *You Know the Number* (1986)

A border case. Threadgill's "sextett" (his spelling, two drummers) is a chamber jazz unit working with through-composed material that draws on march, ragtime, and twentieth-century chamber composition (Ives, Stravinsky). It is rarely shelved as Third Stream, but it inherits the central problem the genre tried to solve: how to make jazz improvisation function inside formally composed material without either being reduced to decoration.

Scaruffi rates Threadgill very high in his composed-jazz hierarchy and would defend his inclusion here as a continuation of the Russell–Lewis line by other means. **Fantano (extrapolated)** has no specific documented Threadgill commentary but would likely respond to the rhythmic asymmetry and the unconventional ensemble voicings.

Coin-flip-resolved against Stan Kenton's *City of Glass* (Robert Graettinger's writing for Kenton, 1948–1951) — the argument for Kenton was historical primacy; the argument for Threadgill was that his record is more listenable and more representative of where the genre's energy actually lives in the post-1970s period. Threadgill won.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Two genuinely irreconcilable disagreements were settled by coin flip after debate exhausted itself.

**Coin Flip 1: *Black Saint and the Sinner Lady* — include or exclude?**

The argument for exclusion: it is not technically Third Stream by Schuller's definition. It uses a jazz ensemble, no classical instrumentation, no twelve-tone material. Putting it on the list dilutes the genre's already-soft boundaries.

The argument for inclusion: any list of through-composed jazz that excludes it is performing a category trick. Mingus is doing the work the genre wanted done; the absence of a string quartet is a surface feature.

Coin flip: **include**. Landed on the side of honesty over technicality.

**Coin Flip 2: Threadgill's *You Know the Number* vs Stan Kenton's *City of Glass* for slot #10.**

The argument for Kenton/Graettinger: historical priority. Graettinger was writing pieces that were structurally closer to mid-century concert music than to dance-band jazz before Schuller had even named the category. This is proto-Third Stream, and the genre owes it something.

The argument for Threadgill: *City of Glass* is admired more than it is listened to. As a record it is a curiosity; as music it is uneven. Threadgill demonstrates that the genre's central problem is still being worked on, which is more useful than canonizing an early attempt.

Coin flip: **Threadgill**. Kenton/Graettinger moves to honorable mentions.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **Stan Kenton — *City of Glass* (1951, Robert Graettinger arrangements).** Proto-Third Stream by historical priority. More important than enjoyable.
- **Modern Jazz Quartet — *European Concert* (1962).** Live document of the MJQ at peak; less explicitly Third Stream than the studio records but a key reference for how the chamber-jazz aesthetic worked in performance.
- **John Lewis — *The Comedy* (1962).** MJQ-plus-vocals project drawing on commedia dell'arte; one of Lewis's more theatrical extensions of the third-stream idea.
- **Don Ellis Orchestra — *Electric Bath* (1967).** Asymmetric meters, Indian and Bulgarian rhythmic borrowings, brass writing that draws on twentieth-century concert music. Borderline; probably more "experimental big band" than Third Stream proper, but in the conversation.
- **Anthony Davis — *X: The Life and Times of Malcolm X* (1992 recording of the 1986 opera).** Davis is one of the few composers writing jazz-classical hybrid opera; *X* is the major statement.
