Excluded from the main ten because it is more "American opera with jazz inflection" than Third Stream balanced from both sides.
- **David Amram — *Triple Concerto for Woodwind, Brass, and Jazz Quintets and Orchestra* (recorded 1971).** Amram is one of the genre's most consistent practitioners; this concerto is one of his clearest realizations.
- **Bob Brookmeyer — *Electricity* (1991, Mel Lewis Orchestra).** Brookmeyer's late composing for big band sits very close to the Schneider position; the writing is symphonically ambitious in ways that prefigure her output.
- **Vince Mendoza — *Epiphany* (1996).** Orchestral arrangements with jazz soloists; a contemporary continuation of the Sauter-Getz approach.
- **Charles Mingus — *Mingus Ah Um* (1959).** Cited in the brief; does not belong in a Third Stream top ten — it is a hard bop masterpiece. Listed here for completeness because it appears in some loose Third Stream discussions.

**Scope honesty:** This is a small genre and the top ten necessarily includes records that stretch the definition (Mingus, Threadgill, Schneider). A strict-construction list would have maybe six titles in it: the two MJQ records, the two Russell records, *Focus*, *Jazz Abstractions*. Everything else is interpretation.

---

### ECM / Chamber Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

ECM is a critic's label by design. Manfred Eicher founded it in Munich in 1969 with a thesis — that jazz could absorb European art-music textures, Nordic open space, and post-classical reverence for silence — and built a recorded sound (the famously dry-yet-cavernous "ECM reverb") that made every release sound like a single ongoing conversation between musicians and weather. Picking ten "best" ECM albums is therefore less a ranking than a map of the label's competing impulses: Jarrett's solo Romanticism, Garbarek's glacial folk-jazz, Towner's chamber strings, Metheny's Midwestern lyricism, Rypdal's electric-cathedral guitar, the Eastern European modernism of Stańko and Brahem, and the late-career sacred turn that culminates in Pärt and *Officium*.

Scaruffi engaged with ECM seriously, especially the 1970s output. He treats *The Köln Concert* as a flawed phenomenon (he prefers Jarrett's quartet records and his Charles Lloyd-era playing), elevates Towner and Metheny, and respects Garbarek's collaboration with the Hilliard Ensemble. His instinct is to push past the obvious: he is wary of canonical Jarrett worship and skeptical of the prettier post-1985 ECM, where he hears mannerism replacing risk.

Fantano is the harder voice to pin down here. He has essentially no public commentary on ECM as a label or on most of its core 1970s catalogue. **Extrapolation flag: every Fantano position in this debate is inferred** from his stated tastes — he favors maximalism over minimalism, rewards strong compositional architecture, prizes contemporary relevance, and tends to undervalue records whose virtues are textural rather than structural. Where Scaruffi reaches back to 1971, Fantano (b. 1985) is more likely to find the modern ECM artists — Tord Gustavsen, Anouar Brahem, Stańko's late period — accessible entry points, while suspecting the 70s catalogue of being a shibboleth.

The debate ran six rounds. Round one nominated the obvious giants. Round two argued Jarrett's solo vs trio output. Round three was Metheny — how much of the early ECM run counts as chamber jazz, and how much is already drifting toward fusion? Round four handled the Nordic axis (Garbarek, Rypdal, Weber). Round five surfaced the European modernists (Stańko, Brahem, Gismonti). Round six resolved sacred-crossover work (Pärt, *Officium*). Three irreconcilables went to coin flip.

A note on what ECM is and isn't, because the genre boundary matters for the list. ECM's "chamber jazz" is not a marketing genre invented by the label — Eicher rejects the term — but it is the most useful descriptor for what the label's first two decades actually produced: small-group jazz played with the dynamics, rehearsal discipline, and notation density of European chamber music, recorded in resonant rooms with audiophile clarity, and released with cover art that looked like Bauhaus catalogues. The label also released straight free jazz (the Art Ensemble of Chicago records, Sam Rivers), straight post-bop (Dave Holland's quintet), and contemporary classical (the New Series imprint). Those records are not on this list because they belong to other genres. What follows is the chamber-jazz core — the records that built and sustained the aesthetic for which ECM became a one-word shorthand.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Keith Jarrett — *The Köln Concert* (1975)

Both critics concede this album is unavoidable, even if neither loves the discourse around it. Recorded January 24, 1975, at the Cologne Opera House on a too-small Bösendorfer that was nearly swapped out before the show, *The Köln Concert* is Jarrett improvising for sixty-six minutes on an instrument he openly considered inadequate. The constraint is the record's secret architecture: the upper register is brittle, the bass is muddy, so Jarrett stays in the middle of the keyboard and works ostinati — those rolling left-hand vamps that became, fairly or not, the template for thirty years of new-age piano.

Scaruffi's read is mixed-positive: he hears genuine improvisational courage, a melodic instinct that occasionally collapses into sentimentality, and a sound-world that helped define ECM's house aesthetic. He prefers Jarrett's American Quartet (Dewey Redman, Charlie Haden, Paul Motian) for sustained risk, but acknowledges *Köln*'s historical weight as the best-selling solo piano record in jazz history.

Fantano (extrapolated, flag) would likely rate this lower than Scaruffi does — the formal looseness, the audience-pleasing arpeggios, and the lack of structural development are not his modes — but he would have to concede it as one of the most listened-to jazz records of its decade and a piece of cultural infrastructure. The grunting, the audience coughs, the way the second half resolves into something almost gospel-folk: these are documentary qualities that survive any aesthetic objection. *Köln* is on this list because to omit it would be to pretend the genre we are mapping does not have a center of gravity. It does. This is it. Every solo piano improvisation released in its wake — Brad Mehldau's solo concerts, Bill Evans's late solo work, Brad's own *Live in Tokyo*, every Tord Gustavsen record — operates either with or against this album's gravitational field.

##### 2. Keith Jarrett — *Belonging* (1974)

If *Köln* is the popular front of Jarrett's ECM work, *Belonging* is the album the musicians' musicians actually return to. Recorded April 1974 in Oslo with the European Quartet — Jan Garbarek (saxophones), Palle Danielsson (bass), Jon Christensen (drums) — *Belonging* is the moment ECM's house sound hardened into a coherent grammar. The opener "Spiral Dance" sets a pattern: simple folk-melodic head, harmonic openness, drummer playing color rather than time, saxophone entering as if from a distance.

Scaruffi rates this very high. He prefers *Belonging* and *My Song* to *Köln* precisely because the quartet format pushes Jarrett away from his sentimental impulses — Garbarek's tone is too austere to allow saccharine gestures, and Christensen's drumming insists on space rather than drive. The ballad "My Song" appears here in embryo via "Solstice" and "Long as You Know You're Living Yours," whose riff Steely Dan would later be accused of borrowing for "Gaucho."

Fantano (extrapolated) would respond to the compositional clarity here — *Belonging* has actual songs, with structures, hooks, and resolution — more than to *Köln*'s open-form improvisation. The European Quartet is also where ECM's claim to be doing something distinct from American post-bop becomes audible: the harmony is still jazz, but the melodic vocabulary is drawn from Norwegian folk, Lutheran hymnody, and the ambient logic of Nordic light. This is the album that made the rest of the 1970s ECM catalogue possible.

##### 3. Pat Metheny — *Bright Size Life* (1976)

Metheny was twenty-one when he recorded his debut for ECM in December 1975, with Jaco Pastorius on bass and Bob Moses on drums. The trio is improbable — Pastorius was already a fusion virtuoso, Metheny was a kid from Lee's Summit, Missouri — but the chemistry is unmistakable. The title track opens with a melody so plainspoken it sounds like a hymn from a denomination that doesn't exist yet, then dissolves into the harmonic openness ECM was inventing in real time.

Scaruffi rates this album highly and treats Metheny's first three or four ECM records as one of the strongest debut runs in jazz. He hears the Ornette Coleman influence (the album closes with a medley of Coleman's "Round Trip / Broadway Blues") as proof that Metheny was thinking about the jazz tradition seriously, not just chasing tone. The Pastorius bass solo on "Sirabhorn" is one of the great early Jaco performances, recorded six months before his solo debut.

Fantano (extrapolated, flag) is harder to predict here, but the album's clean architecture, melodic generosity, and short runtime (under thirty-eight minutes) match his stated preferences. *Bright Size Life* is also one of the most influential guitar records of its era — every Midwestern indie-rock guitarist working in open tunings owes it something, whether they know it or not. The album's Americana-inflected lyricism is what separates Metheny from his European labelmates and gives ECM its unexpected second center, in a Missouri-shaped accent.

##### 4. Jan Garbarek / The Hilliard Ensemble — *Officium* (1994)

*Officium* is the album that broke ECM out of jazz entirely. Recorded September 1993 in the monastery of Propstei St. Gerold in Austria, it pairs Garbarek's saxophone with the Hilliard Ensemble — four male voices performing medieval and Renaissance polyphony (Pérotin, Morales, Dufay, anonymous chant). Garbarek improvises around and through the vocal lines, treating Gregorian and early-Renaissance source material as a living harmonic field rather than a museum piece.

Scaruffi engaged with this record seriously. He treats it as one of the rare successful jazz-classical crossovers — most fail because the jazz musician imposes themselves on the classical material; here, Garbarek's restraint and the Hilliard Ensemble's tonal purity create genuine dialogue. The album sold over 1.5 million copies, which is staggering for a record whose oldest piece is from the 12th century.

Fantano (extrapolated) would likely respect *Officium* even outside his usual zones because of its compositional rigor — the Hilliard Ensemble are not background, they are co-composers — and its singular conceptual identity. The album's success was not a marketing accident; the reverberant acoustics of St. Gerold and Garbarek's willingness to disappear into the texture create a piece of music that genuinely earns its solemnity. *Officium* also represents ECM's New Series imprint at full strength, the label's classical wing colliding productively with its jazz mission. It belongs in this top ten.

##### 5. Ralph Towner — *Solstice* (1975)

Towner's *Solstice*, recorded December 1974 with Garbarek (saxes/flute), Eberhard Weber (bass), and Christensen (drums), is the chamber-jazz record that most clearly states the genre's thesis: classical guitar (12-string and nylon) treated as a lead voice, no piano, no chord instrument forcing harmonic resolution, four players each contributing a distinct timbre. The opening track "Oceanus" is a study in how much harmonic information can be carried by a single bass line and a flute melody.

Scaruffi places Towner among the most important ECM artists of the 1970s. He values *Solstice* for what it isn't — it isn't fusion, it isn't post-bop, it isn't third-stream classical compromise — and for what it is: a serious attempt to build a new jazz chamber idiom from European folk, classical guitar tradition, and the open-form rhythmic language Jarrett's quartet was developing in parallel.

Fantano (extrapolated, flag) has no on-record opinion. The album's commitment to texture over groove is not in his usual zone. But the writing is strong (Towner is a real composer, not just a guitarist), the four-way interplay is tight, and "Drifting Petals" is the kind of pure melodic event that survives genre prejudice. *Solstice* is also the album that proved ECM could make a record without piano or electric instrumentation that still felt modern. It cleared the path for a great deal of what followed on the label, including Towner's continued work with Oregon.

##### 6. Charlie Haden — *Closeness* (1976)

*Closeness* is technically on Horizon (an A&M imprint), not ECM proper, but it is the chamber-jazz duet record that defines what duo improvisation became in this aesthetic, and Haden was one of the central figures on ECM in the late 70s through Old and New Dreams. The album is four duos: Haden with Keith Jarrett, with Ornette Coleman, with Alice Coltrane, and with Paul Motian. Each track is a portrait, and each one is structured around silence.

Scaruffi values Haden's bass playing throughout the post-1975 period, and *Closeness* sits in his canon as one of the great duet records in jazz history. The Ornette duet "O.C." is particularly important — two players who had spent fifteen years together by that point, treating the bass-saxophone pairing as a fully self-sufficient ensemble.

Fantano (extrapolated) would likely respond to the directness here — duets are structurally legible, no one is hiding — and to the emotional weight of the Alice Coltrane track, "For a Free Portugal," recorded in 1976, two years after John's death. The chamber-jazz aesthetic is sometimes accused of being decorative; *Closeness* is the counterexample. It is intimate, formally lean, politically engaged (the Portugal track, the Ornette friendship), and it documents Haden's belief that a duet is enough — you don't need a quintet to say something serious. The album is on this list as the case for ECM-adjacent chamber jazz outside the label proper.

##### 7. Eberhard Weber — *The Colours of Chloë* (1974)

Weber's debut as leader, recorded December 1973, is one of the foundational ECM solo records. Weber plays his self-built electric upright bass — five strings, fretted, run through a chorus pedal that gives it a synthesizer-like sustain — and writes for cello ensemble, piano (Rainer Brüninghaus), and drums. The result is a chamber-jazz record with no horns, organized around bass melodies that float above the harmonic motion rather than anchoring it.

Scaruffi engages with Weber as one of the architects of ECM's distinct bass aesthetic. The opening track "More Colours" introduces the cello ensemble as a genuine compositional voice, not a string-section sweetener. Weber's tone — that signature high, singing electric upright — became one of the most recognizable instrumental signatures on the label.

Fantano (extrapolated, flag) has no commentary. The record's reliance on composed material over improvisation might read to him as soft, but the writing is genuinely modern — closer to Steve Reich's approach to additive structure than to jazz fusion's blues-rock chassis — and the production (Eicher and Martin Wieland at Tonstudio Bauer) is one of the great early ECM recording achievements. Weber would go on to play with Jarrett's European Quartet briefly, then with Garbarek for over thirty years. *Colours of Chloë* is where his compositional voice arrives whole, and it is one of the best arguments for chamber jazz as a real genre rather than a marketing description.

##### 8. Pat Metheny / Lyle Mays — *As Falls Wichita, So Falls Wichita Falls* (1981)

The collaboration between Metheny and his longtime keyboardist Lyle Mays, recorded September 1980 with Nana Vasconcelos on percussion, is the most ambitious composed work of Metheny's ECM tenure. The twenty-minute title suite moves through ambient drift, programmatic storm imagery, and the long-form melodic development that would define Metheny's later Geffen years.

Scaruffi rates Metheny's early ECM run as a coherent body of work and treats *Wichita Falls* as the moment Metheny stopped being a guitar prodigy and became a bandleader-composer. The Mays partnership is the key — Mays brings the harmonic sophistication that Metheny's lyricism needs to avoid sentimentality.

Fantano (extrapolated) would likely respond to the structural ambition here more than to the trio records. *Wichita Falls* is not just a collection of tunes; it is a piece of music with movements, dynamics, and a real arc. The "Estupenda Graça" closer, with Vasconcelos's berimbau and wordless vocals, also pushes Metheny into Brazilian and South American territory that would mark his work for the next decade. This album is the bridge between ECM's contemplative early-70s aesthetic and the brighter, more programmatic 1980s Pat Metheny Group sound. It deserves the slot ahead of *Watercolors* (1977) because the writing is more ambitious and the production has aged better.

##### 9. Tomasz Stańko — *Litania: Music of Krzysztof Komeda* (1997)

Stańko's tribute to the Polish composer Krzysztof Komeda — best known for scoring Roman Polanski's films, including *Rosemary's Baby*, before his accidental death in 1969 — is one of the great ECM records of the 1990s. Recorded September 1996 with a stacked band (Bobo Stenson on piano, Tony Oxley on drums, Bernt Rosengren and Joakim Milder on saxes, Palle Danielsson on bass, Terje Rypdal on guitar), *Litania* reframes Komeda's compositions as living material rather than memorial.

Scaruffi rates Stańko among the most important European jazz figures of the post-1990 period and engages with *Litania* as one of his strongest records. The album is also the moment Polish jazz history — long suppressed under the Communist period and largely invisible to American listeners — became audible inside the ECM canon.

Fantano (extrapolated, flag) would likely find Stańko more accessible than the 1970s ECM artists because the writing is structurally clear, the band swings (in a loose, late-night way), and Komeda's melodies are hummable in the way film themes are. Stańko's trumpet tone — cracked, breathy, deliberately imperfect — also pushes against ECM's reputation for cool perfection. *Litania* is on this list as the strongest representative of ECM's Eastern European axis, and as the album that made the case for ECM's continued relevance after the early-70s founders had become heritage acts.

##### 10. Anouar Brahem — *The Astounding Eyes of Rita* (2009)

Brahem's quartet record with Klaus Gesing (bass clarinet), Björn Meyer (bass), and Khaled Yassine (darbuka, bendir) is the strongest argument that ECM's chamber-jazz aesthetic remained generative into the 21st century. Brahem plays oud — a fretless Arabic lute — and writes through-composed pieces that blend North African modal traditions, jazz harmony, and ECM's signature approach to space.

Scaruffi engaged with Brahem's earlier ECM records (*Thimar*, 1998) and would likely accept *Astounding Eyes* as the mature statement. The album avoids the world-music trap — it is not Brahem playing oud over jazz changes, and it is not jazz musicians playing fake Arabic music — by using the bass clarinet as a melodic peer to the oud and letting the two voices weave actual counterpoint.

Fantano (extrapolated) is the strongest case for this album as a modern entry point. The dedication to Mahmoud Darwish (the Palestinian poet), the conceptual unity, and the relative directness of the compositions all match his stated preferences. *Astounding Eyes* also demonstrates ECM's continued willingness to fund records that don't sound like anything else — Brahem's oud is the lead voice on a major label release in 2009, and the band is built around him rather than the reverse. The album is on this list because the genre needs a 21st-century representative, and this is the strongest one. It is also one of the best-selling ECM records of the past twenty years.

The Brahem inclusion also resolves a quiet argument in the debate about whether ECM's chamber-jazz aesthetic is portable across cultural traditions. The 1970s ECM was overwhelmingly Northern European and white; by the 2000s the label had built a serious catalogue of records by Tunisian, Brazilian, Lebanese, and Israeli musicians who used the chamber-jazz frame to do work that wouldn't fit anywhere else. *Astounding Eyes* is the strongest argument that this expansion was substantive rather than cosmetic.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

Three slots reached genuine impasse and were resolved by coin flip rather than further argument.

**Coin flip 1: Pat Metheny *Bright Size Life* vs Pat Metheny Group *Pat Metheny Group* (1978).** Scaruffi argued for *Bright Size Life* on debut-grounds and Pastorius collaboration; the extrapolated Fantano position favored the self-titled, which is more melodically polished and where the Lyle Mays partnership cohered. Coin flip went to *Bright Size Life*. The 1978 self-titled moves to honorable mention.

**Coin flip 2: Garbarek/Hilliard *Officium* vs Garbarek *Dis* (1977).** Scaruffi has serious affection for *Dis* — Ralph Towner's 12-string against Garbarek's saxophone, recorded in a Norwegian sound chamber that captures wind sounds — and argued it is the more original ECM Garbarek statement. The extrapolated Fantano position favored *Officium* on cultural-impact and conceptual-clarity grounds. Coin flip went to *Officium*. *Dis* moves to honorable mention.

**Coin flip 3: Stańko *Litania* vs Stańko *Soul of Things* (2002).** Both critics had cases. *Soul of Things* is the cleaner trio statement (Marcin Wasilewski on piano), *Litania* is the larger-scope tribute. Coin flip went to *Litania*. *Soul of Things* moves to honorable mention.

A meta-note on the coin flips: none of these three pairings have a wrong answer. The Metheny debut vs the PMG self-titled is a genuine fork in his career — one record points toward post-bop, the other toward the programmatic group sound that defined his next twenty years. The Garbarek pairing is more about which collaboration matters more historically: Towner vs the Hilliard Ensemble, the chamber-jazz idea vs the sacred-crossover idea. The Stańko pairing is about scope vs intimacy. In each case, the loser is a top-tier ECM record, and a different reasonable critic would seat the list differently. The honorable mentions section is therefore not a consolation list — it is a parallel top ten, almost.

#### Honorable Mentions

These records were in serious contention and survived to round five or six but did not reach the final ten.

- **Keith Jarrett — *My Song* (1978).** The European Quartet's second masterpiece. Scaruffi prefers *Belonging*; Fantano (extrapolated) might prefer *My Song* for the title-track melody alone. Either choice is defensible; *Belonging* won on chronological priority.
- **Keith Jarrett Trio — *Standards, Vol. 1* (1983).** The Standards Trio with Gary Peacock and Jack DeJohnette is one of the great long-running jazz ensembles, but the chamber-jazz frame fits the European Quartet better.
- **Pat Metheny — *Watercolors* (1977).** The bridge between *Bright Size Life* and the PMG records. Lyle Mays's first appearance with Metheny.
- **Pat Metheny Group — *Pat Metheny Group* (1978).** Lost the coin flip to *Bright Size Life*.
- **Pat Metheny — *80/81* (1980).** Two-disc summit with Dewey Redman, Mike Brecker, Charlie Haden, Jack DeJohnette — closer to post-bop than chamber jazz, which is why it didn't make the cut.
- **Jan Garbarek — *Dis* (1977).** Lost the coin flip to *Officium*.
- **Oregon — *Oregon* (1983).** Towner's group with Paul McCandless, Glen Moore, Collin Walcott. The self-titled ECM debut after the Vanguard years.
- **Terje Rypdal — *Odyssey* (1975).** Norwegian electric guitar at full cathedral volume. Rypdal is the loudest argument against the "ECM is quiet" stereotype.
- **Old and New Dreams — *Old and New Dreams* (1979).** Don Cherry, Dewey Redman, Charlie Haden, Ed Blackwell — the Ornette alumni reunion. ECM's clearest connection to free jazz.
- **Egberto Gismonti — *Sol do Meio Dia* (1978).** Brazilian guitar/piano polymath, one of ECM's most distinctive solo voices.
- **Bill Frisell — *In Line* (1983).** Frisell's ECM debut, solo and duo guitar with Arild Andersen. The blueprint for everything Frisell did later.
- **Steve Tibbetts — *Safe Journey* (1984).** Minnesota guitarist, ECM's most underground rock-adjacent voice.
- **Bobo Stenson Trio — *War Orphans* (1997).** Swedish piano trio at its most lyrical.
- **Tord Gustavsen Trio — *Changing Places* (2003).** The clearest 21st-century ECM piano-trio statement.
- **Manu Katché — *Neighbourhood* (2005).** French drummer's ECM debut with Stańko and Garbarek.
- **Anouar Brahem — *Thimar* (1998).** With John Surman and Dave Holland; the earlier statement of Brahem's ECM voice.
- **Bill Frisell — *Lookout for Hope* (1988).** Frisell's group record, leaning more toward Americana than chamber jazz.
- **Arvo Pärt — *Tabula Rasa* (1984).** ECM New Series classical, not jazz, but the recording aesthetic and the Pärt-ECM relationship are inseparable from the *Officium* lineage.
- **Charlie Haden / Jan Garbarek / Egberto Gismonti — *Magico* (1980).** The trio summit. Almost made the top ten.
- **Keith Jarrett — *Solo Concerts: Bremen / Lausanne* (1973).** The pre-*Köln* solo recordings. Scaruffi often cites these as superior to *Köln* on improvisational grounds.

The shape of the list reflects ECM's actual center of gravity: a 1970s European quartet aesthetic, a Midwestern lyrical guitar tradition that runs through Metheny, a Nordic axis (Garbarek, Weber, Rypdal), an Eastern European modernist axis (Stańko), and a Mediterranean / North African extension (Brahem). The label's continued relevance after Eicher's eventual retirement is an open question, but the body of work assembled between 1969 and 2010 is one of the most coherent in any genre. Whatever the ranking arguments, the case for ECM as a single ongoing artistic statement — rather than a collection of records that happened to share a logo — survives the debate intact.

What does not survive intact is the easy critical framing that pits Scaruffi against Fantano on every record. The honest report from this debate is that on roughly half the catalogue Fantano simply has no public position, and the extrapolations made above are working from his stated preferences in adjacent genres (post-rock, contemporary classical, ambient, modern jazz reissues) rather than from anything he has said about ECM. A debate with a real Fantano archive on this material would look different. The Scaruffi side, by contrast, is reconstructed from explicit written positions in his jazz history and individual artist pages, and is therefore more reliable. Readers should weight the two voices accordingly: Scaruffi as a documented critic engaging with material he genuinely cares about; Fantano as a synthetic projection that approximates how a structurally-minded contemporary critic might respond if pressed. The list is stronger for the projection, but the projection is a tool, not a claim.

---

### Smooth Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*

#### Debate Summary

This is the most contested list in Volume 2 — not because the critics disagree about the records but because both critics, in different registers, view the entire sub-genre with suspicion. Smooth jazz is the genre that jazz purists love to hate: the sound of dental waiting rooms and Lite-FM drive time, the commercial endgame of the fusion that Miles Davis kicked off in 1969 sanded down for adult-contemporary radio. Scaruffi, who reserves real enthusiasm for the avant-garde and the loft scene, has historically treated smooth jazz as a category-error — pleasant background music that borrowed jazz's instrumentation and discarded its risk. Fantano (b. 1985) has no significant direct smooth-jazz commentary on record; the entire premise of his criticism — sincerity of vision, willingness to alienate, formal ambition — runs against what smooth jazz is engineered to do. **All Fantano positions in this list are extrapolations and should be read as such.**

That said, the assignment forces ten records. The honest path is to surface the albums whose musicianship, songwriting, or production are strong enough to survive the critical contempt the genre as a whole earns. The two critics converged faster than expected on the top tier — *Breezin'*, *Winelight*, *Mister Magic*, the late Wes Montgomery CTI material, and a couple of Pat Metheny Group records sit comfortably above the noise — but the back half of the list became a four-round argument about whether to include Kenny G at all (no, says everyone), whether Sade counts as smooth jazz (Scaruffi: no, she's quiet storm soul; Fantano-extrapolated: probably also no but she's more defensible than half the actual smooth-jazz canon), and whether Chuck Mangione's *Feels So Good* is a guilty pleasure or a small masterpiece of melodic writing.

Two records went to coin flip: Bob James / David Sanborn *Double Vision* vs. Joe Sample *Carmel*, and Earl Klugh *Finger Paintings* vs. Lee Ritenour *Rio*. The Rippingtons, Kenny G, Dave Koz, Boney James, Najee, and Norman Brown were all rejected without serious dispute — both critics agreed these represent the genre's commercial nadir rather than its defensible center. Sade's *Diamond Life* was placed in honorable mentions because she is more accurately a soul/quiet-storm artist whose production aesthetic overlaps with smooth jazz, not a smooth-jazz musician.

The list that follows leans toward the genre's earlier, jazzier, more harmonically ambitious end (mid-70s CTI sessions, Crusaders-era Joe Sample, Pat Metheny's ECM-adjacent crossover) and away from the late-80s/90s sax-led radio era that defined the term in popular usage. Both critics agreed this is the only honest way to assemble a top-10 from this material.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. George Benson — *Breezin'* (1976)

The canonical anchor and the only smooth jazz record that both critics accept without qualification. *Breezin'* is the album that defined the template — Tommy LiPuma's gleaming production, Phil Upchurch's rhythm guitar locked to a quiet-storm pocket, Benson's single-note runs treated like vocal hooks rather than improvisations. The title track, a Bobby Womack composition, is the genre's foundational statement: a melody so memorable it functions as a pop song, surrounded by playing fluid enough to satisfy a jazz audience. "This Masquerade," with Benson's vocal-and-guitar unison technique borrowed from Slam Stewart and George Shearing, became the first record in any genre to top the pop, R&B, and jazz charts simultaneously and won the Grammy for Record of the Year.

Scaruffi grants Benson the unusual concession of real respect — he treats Benson's playing as the legitimate continuation of the Charlie Christian / Wes Montgomery line rather than a betrayal of it, and notes that *Breezin'* is the rare commercial jazz crossover that doesn't require apology for its musicianship. The Coltrane / Wes Montgomery / Grant Green DNA is audible underneath the gloss. Fantano-extrapolated would likely note that this is one of the few records in the genre that survives precisely because Benson refused to dumb down his actual playing for the format — the harmonic content is denser than the surface implies.

Where *Breezin'* matters historically: it proved that polished jazz-funk could move millions of units without sacrificing technical credibility, which both opened the door for the genre's better records (Washington's *Winelight*, Metheny's crossover work) and gave the green light to the sax-led 1990s commercial wave that the same critics dismiss. Benson is the genre's best-case-scenario musician and *Breezin'* is the best-case-scenario record. Everything that came after had to measure itself against this and almost nothing did.

##### 2. Grover Washington Jr. — *Winelight* (1980)

The other anchor. *Winelight* is the smooth jazz record that proves the genre at its peak could produce something genuinely beautiful rather than merely pleasant. Washington's tone on soprano and alto — broad, breathy, melodically generous — sits inside arrangements that respect both the song and the player. The title track is one of the most harmonically sophisticated pieces ever to soundtrack a wine commercial. "Just the Two of Us," the Bill Withers vocal feature, became inescapable but the album cuts — "Let It Flow (For Dr. J)," "In the Name of Love," "Make Me a Memory" — are where the record actually lives.

Both critics accept this without much argument. Scaruffi treats *Winelight* as the rare smooth jazz album where the writing and the playing are strong enough to survive the production aesthetic — Washington plays like he means it, the rhythm section (Marcus Miller on bass, Steve Gadd on drums on several cuts) is too good to coast, and the harmonic vocabulary is closer to the jazz-funk lineage of Eddie Harris and Stanley Turrentine than to the radio-targeted material that would follow. Fantano-extrapolated would probably acknowledge that Washington is doing real compositional work here even if he'd find the production dated.

The four Grammys *Winelight* won, including Best Jazz Fusion Performance, mark the moment smooth jazz became commercially unstoppable. The historical irony is that the genre's commercial peak is also one of its few critical defensible monuments. Washington died in 1999 after collapsing during a CBS taping; he was 56. *Winelight* is the record by which his legacy is measured and rightly so — it is the smooth jazz album that has aged best, the one that still rewards close listening rather than ambient absorption. The bass lines alone (Miller is operating at full strength) put it in a different category from the soprano-sax wallpaper that would dominate the genre a decade later.

##### 3. Grover Washington Jr. — *Mister Magic* (1975)

The proto-text. *Mister Magic* predates the smooth jazz designation by roughly a decade and is more accurately filed as jazz-funk — but its DNA runs through everything the genre would later become. Bob James produced and arranged; Eric Gale and Gary King hold down the rhythm section; Harvey Mason plays drums on the title track. The four extended cuts — "Earth Tones," "Mister Magic," "Black Frost," "Passion Flower" — are built on long, hypnotic grooves that Washington plays over with the kind of post-Coltrane phrasing that the smooth-jazz format would eventually sand off entirely.

This is the record that earns Washington his place at the top of any smooth-jazz canon. It went to #10 on the Billboard 200 and spent weeks at #1 on the jazz chart at a moment when "jazz chart" still meant something. Scaruffi has historically been more sympathetic to *Mister Magic* than to *Winelight* precisely because the jazz-funk aesthetic — heavier on the groove, lighter on the strings — sits closer to the Headhunters / Donald Byrd / Roy Ayers lineage he respects. The title track's bass line is one of the most sampled in hip-hop history (A Tribe Called Quest, De La Soul, Kid Cudi), which both critics would likely cite as evidence that the record's foundational rhythmic content was strong enough to survive translation into entirely different musical contexts.

The honest place to put *Mister Magic* in this list is as the bridge between jazz-funk and what smooth jazz would become — it's the moment the genre's commercial logic was being assembled but before the format hardened. Hearing it now, you can identify every element that would later be optimized into wallpaper: the long melodic lines, the locked-in rhythm section, the polished but jazz-literate production. The difference is that on *Mister Magic* nothing is yet predictable.

##### 4. Pat Metheny Group — *Pat Metheny Group* (1978)

The self-titled ECM debut is where Metheny's crossover aesthetic crystallized. With Lyle Mays on piano and synth, Mark Egan on bass, and Dan Gottlieb on drums, the record sits at the intersection of ECM chamber jazz, jazz-fusion, and what would later be classified as smooth jazz adjacent. "San Lorenzo," "Phase Dance," "April Joy," "Lone Jack" — these are compositions, not riffs, with the kind of melodic memorability that crossed Metheny over to audiences who would never have bought a Keith Jarrett or Jan Garbarek record.

Both critics treat Metheny as the genre's escape hatch — the artist who proves that accessibility and harmonic ambition aren't mutually exclusive. Scaruffi's general respect for the Pat Metheny Group catalogue is qualified but real; he locates Metheny in the pastoral-jazz lineage rather than in smooth jazz proper, but acknowledges that the group's commercial success and the radio-friendly nature of the writing put them in conversation with the genre. Fantano-extrapolated would almost certainly prefer Metheny over any of the genre's pure-form practitioners on the grounds that there is actual compositional intent here.

The argument for including this record in a smooth jazz list rather than a fusion or ECM list is that *Pat Metheny Group* was the album that defined the "Windham Hill / Hearts of Space / smooth jazz" college-radio Venn diagram of the early 1980s — the place where adult listeners who wanted instrumental music with melody but without aggression went to find it. Metheny was that audience's first stop and frequently their last. The record's emotional vocabulary — wistful, open, harmonically luminous — established a template that the better corners of smooth jazz spent the next decade trying and failing to replicate.

##### 5. Wes Montgomery — *A Day in the Life* (1967)

The CTI / Creed Taylor commercial period that the jazz purist tradition has spent fifty years apologizing for and that Scaruffi, against expectation, has always treated with more sympathy than the orthodoxy allows. *A Day in the Life* — Don Sebesky arrangements, Herbie Hancock and Ron Carter in the rhythm section, Montgomery playing pop melodies (the Beatles title track, "Eleanor Rigby," "California Dreamin'") with his thumb-octave technique — is the record that essentially invented the smooth-jazz template a decade before the term existed.

The standard line is that Verve and CTI ruined Montgomery by making him play pop tunes over orchestral arrangements instead of letting him blow over standards. The revisionist line, which both Scaruffi and the more thoughtful contemporary critics now hold, is that Montgomery's playing on these records is no less brilliant for the context — the octave runs, the melodic phrasing, the rhythmic pocket are all intact, and the records sold in numbers that funded an entire generation of jazz musicians' subsequent work at CTI. Montgomery died in 1968, less than a year after this record's release, of a heart attack at 45. The commercial era turned out to be the end of his career rather than a phase he could later recover from.

Including this in a smooth jazz top 10 rather than a hard bop or guitar jazz list is the right call because *A Day in the Life* is the actual ur-text of the genre — the proof of concept that a serious jazz musician could play pop material over polished arrangements without it being artistically nothing. Everyone from Benson to Klugh to Ritenour was working from this template. The record is critically contested in a way that the rest of Montgomery's catalogue is not, but the playing is undeniable and the historical importance for this specific genre is total.

##### 6. Bob James — *Three* (1976)

Bob James's CTI tenure produced a string of records — *One*, *Two*, *Three*, *Four* — that functioned as both commercial smooth-jazz product and as the source material for an entire wave of hip-hop sampling. *Three* is the strongest of them: "Westchester Lady" is the kind of polished mid-70s instrumental funk that is genuinely composed rather than merely arranged, and "Nautilus" — the closer — is one of the most sampled jazz recordings of all time, the rhythmic and harmonic foundation underneath Slick Rick's "Children's Story," Ghostface Killah's "Daytona 500," Run-DMC, Jeru the Damaja, Eric B. & Rakim, and dozens more.

The hip-hop sampling history is the honest critical defense of Bob James. Scaruffi would likely note that the records' compositional strength is more easily heard now, in the wake of the sampling, than it was at the time when smooth-jazz purists were treating these as background music. Fantano-extrapolated would probably hold that any record whose bass-line and drum break have generated this much downstream cultural production has earned at least conditional respect — the raw material was strong enough to survive recontextualization, which is more than can be said for most of the genre's actual canon.

*Three* belongs on this list as the representative of the Bob James CTI era — a producer-arranger-pianist who, more than any single other figure, engineered the production aesthetic that defined smooth jazz's first commercial wave. The records are slick by design but the harmonic language underneath the gloss is closer to Herbie Hancock's *Maiden Voyage*-era post-bop than to anything that would later carry the smooth-jazz label. James understood that the hook was the song; the genre took that lesson and forgot every other one James knew.

##### 7. Pat Metheny Group — *Offramp* (1982)

The second Pat Metheny Group entry, included over the protest of both critics that two PMG records is a lot for a top-10 list. The argument that wins is that *Offramp* — the record that introduced Metheny's Synclavier guitar-synth on "Are You Going with Me?" and that won the first of the group's many Grammys — is a different kind of statement than the 1978 self-titled debut. Where the debut was pastoral, *Offramp* is electric, percussive, and in places almost minimalist; "Au Lait" is one of the most emotionally direct pieces of music in the entire ECM catalogue; "James" (the Lyle Mays-co-written tribute to James Taylor) is the genre's purest ballad.

Scaruffi prefers *Offramp* among the early-80s Metheny records on the grounds that the guitar-synth experiments push the music into territory that smooth jazz proper would never go — the textures are stranger, the rhythmic ideas more committed. Fantano-extrapolated would likely prefer this one too because it does the most genuinely formal work of any record on the list. Both critics agreed that Metheny's two-album presence here is unusual but defensible: he is the genre's escape valve, the proof that the format wasn't entirely artistically dead in the 1980s.

The case against *Offramp* on a smooth-jazz list is that it isn't really smooth jazz — it's a fusion record with crossover sensibility. The case for is that the same college-radio audience that was buying David Sanborn and Spyro Gyra was also buying this, that "Are You Going with Me?" got smooth-jazz radio play, and that the Pat Metheny Group's commercial trajectory belongs to the genre's history whether the critics filed in adjacent bins or not. The record won the Grammy for Best Jazz Fusion Performance — the same category *Winelight* won — which is genre-confused enough to confirm the placement.

##### 8. The Crusaders — *Street Life* (1979)

The Crusaders — Joe Sample on keys, Wilton Felder on tenor, Stix Hooper on drums (until shortly after this record), with Larry Carlton frequently on guitar — were the jazz-funk supergroup whose late-70s commercial peak put them squarely inside the smooth-jazz genealogy even as their musicianship pulled them toward harder territory. *Street Life* is the record where the balance worked: Randy Crawford's vocal on the eleven-minute title track is one of the great soul-jazz performances of the era, the band's playing underneath is locked in to a degree that the genre's later records would lose entirely.

Scaruffi has historically been kinder to the Crusaders than to most smooth-jazz acts on the grounds that the band came up through legitimate hard bop and post-bop (as the Jazz Crusaders in the 1960s) and brought that rhythmic and harmonic literacy with them into the commercial era. Joe Sample's Fender Rhodes work and Wilton Felder's tenor sit closer to Cannonball Adderley's late-60s soul-jazz than to anything you'd hear on a 1990s Wave radio playlist. Fantano-extrapolated would likely respect the band's willingness to commit to long-form arrangements — "Street Life" runs eleven minutes in its full version, which is not a smooth-jazz move.

*Street Life* is the smooth-jazz crossover album that has the strongest claim to being a soul record at the same time. Crawford's vocal is the star, but the instrumental cuts ("My Lady," "Carnival of the Night," "Rodeo Drive") sit comfortably with the rest of the band's late-70s output — confident, rhythmically alive, harmonically generous. The Crusaders are the bridge between soul-jazz and smooth-jazz proper, and *Street Life* is the bridge song.

##### 9. Chuck Mangione — *Feels So Good* (1977)

The contested entry. Both critics expressed reservations about including *Feels So Good* — the title track is one of the most overplayed instrumental hits of the 1970s, and the flugelhorn-led easy-listening aesthetic represents exactly the kind of soft-jazz crossover that gives the genre its bad reputation. The argument for inclusion is that Mangione is a serious musician (he was a sideman with Art Blakey's Jazz Messengers in the mid-60s) whose compositional skill carries the record beyond its commercial-jingle reputation. The title track is genuinely well-written; the long version, at nine minutes, has actual jazz content.

Scaruffi's position was effectively "fine, include it, but understand what you're including" — the album is the genre's middle-of-the-road peak rather than its artistic peak, and *Feels So Good* belongs on the list as historical evidence rather than as critical recommendation. Fantano-extrapolated would probably be skeptical of any record this thoroughly soundtracked into the cultural background, but might grant Mangione's musicianship more credit than the surface implies. The record sold more than two million copies; the title track was nominated for Record of the Year at the Grammys; and Mangione spent the late 1970s as one of the most visible jazz musicians in America despite the genre's general commercial decline.

The case for placement at #9 rather than higher is honest: *Feels So Good* is the smooth-jazz record that most people who don't listen to smooth jazz can hum from memory, which is both the source of its historical importance and the reason serious listeners tend to discount it. The other tracks on the album — "Hide & Seek," "Maui-Waui," "The XIth Commandment" — are stronger than the title-track reputation suggests. The record represents the genre's commercial high-water mark before the 1990s sax-led era flattened everything.

##### 10. Bob James / David Sanborn — *Double Vision* (1986)

The last slot, won by coin flip over Joe Sample's *Carmel* (see Coin-Flip Resolutions). *Double Vision* is the Bob James / David Sanborn collaboration that won the Grammy for Best Jazz Fusion Performance and that, in retrospect, marks the moment the smooth-jazz era reached its mid-80s production-aesthetic peak: Marcus Miller on bass, Al Jarreau on the title-track vocal, Sanborn's signature alto cutting through James's polished arrangements with the kind of bluesy R&B-derived tone that defined his career.

Both critics agreed this was the most defensible 1980s pure-smooth-jazz record on the table. Scaruffi treated it with the standard skepticism — too polished, too radio-targeted, too obviously engineered for the format — but conceded that Sanborn's playing is recognizably his own and that Marcus Miller's bass work is too good to dismiss. Fantano-extrapolated would likely note that *Double Vision* is at least committed to its production aesthetic in a way that the genre's later records would not be — the slap bass, the gated reverb on the drums, the synthetic textures are all working together rather than functioning as default settings.

Sanborn's tone is the thing that justifies the placement. He is one of the few alto players whose phrasing is identifiable in three notes — bluesy, slightly cracked, vocal in inflection. Even on the most polished material his playing has friction. The record's hits — "Maputo," "Moon Tan," the title track — are all built around that contrast: gleaming production, rough-edged sax. This is the smooth-jazz aesthetic working at its highest level of execution. Everything later in the genre would be a thinner version of this.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

**Bob James / David Sanborn *Double Vision* vs. Joe Sample *Carmel* (1979)** — Sample's solo record is genuinely strong (the title track is gorgeous, "Sunrise" and "Cannery Row" sit comfortably with his Crusaders work) and arguably represents the more "pure" smooth-jazz aesthetic. *Double Vision* won on the Sanborn factor — his alto presence is more historically representative of the genre's mid-80s peak than Sample's Rhodes-led California sound. Coin flip, *Double Vision* wins. Sample's *Carmel* moves to honorable mentions.

**Earl Klugh *Finger Paintings* (1977) vs. Lee Ritenour *Rio* (1979)** — Both records belong in a comprehensive smooth-jazz survey. Klugh's nylon-string approach is one of the genre's most distinctive sounds; Ritenour's Brazilian-fusion crossover on *Rio* is musically richer. The list as constructed already had two guitarists (Benson, Metheny x2 if you count the band as Metheny-led) so a third was hard to justify. Coin flip went to neither — both moved to honorable mentions in favor of holding the Pat Metheny Group second slot for *Offramp*. Acknowledged that this is the most arguable cut on the list.

**Pat Metheny Group second album decision** — The non-coin-flip but contested decision was whether to include *Offramp* or to use the second slot for *Still Life (Talking)* (1987) or *American Garage* (1979). Scaruffi pushed for the earlier ECM aesthetic; Fantano-extrapolated had no preference. *Offramp* won on the basis that its Grammy and crossover impact place it most cleanly in the smooth-jazz historical conversation, even though *Still Life (Talking)* arguably has the stronger compositions.

#### Honorable Mentions

**Joe Sample — *Carmel* (1979).** Lost the coin flip with *Double Vision*. The title track is one of the most beautiful Rhodes melodies of the era and the band — Larry Carlton on guitar, Wilton Felder on bass — is essentially a Crusaders side-project with Sample writing all the material. Should be in any expanded list.

**Earl Klugh — *Finger Paintings* (1977).** The nylon-string acoustic guitar approach is the most distinctive non-Benson voice in smooth-jazz guitar. Klugh's tone and phrasing borrow from Chet Atkins as much as from any jazz lineage. *Finger Paintings* is the canonical entry point and the record where his crossover sensibility found its template.

**Lee Ritenour — *Rio* (1979).** The Brazilian-fusion record that introduced Djavan to American audiences and that pushed Ritenour's playing closer to Pat Metheny's harmonic territory than to the studio-session guitarist reputation he had earned in the prior decade. Underrated in the genre's historical accounting.

**Sade — *Diamond Life* (1984).** Filed here rather than in the top 10 because Sade is more accurately a quiet-storm soul artist than a smooth-jazz musician — Stuart Matthewman's saxophone and the band's harmonic vocabulary overlap with the smooth-jazz aesthetic but the songwriting and vocal tradition belong to a different lineage. Both critics agreed she should be acknowledged but not classified as smooth jazz proper. The record is, however, better than most of what's on the actual list.

**Larry Carlton — *Larry Carlton* (1978).** The Steely Dan / Crusaders session guitarist's solo debut with "Room 335" — the title-as-room-number references his home studio — which is one of the most influential guitar instrumentals of the era. The fusion content is heavier than most smooth-jazz records but the production aesthetic and crossover audience are continuous.

**The Yellowjackets — *Mirage à Trois* (1983).** Russell Ferrante's compositions and the band's tight ensemble playing make *Mirage à Trois* the strongest of the early Yellowjackets records. They sit closer to Weather Report-derived fusion than to smooth jazz proper but the genre overlap is real.

**Spyro Gyra — *Morning Dance* (1979).** The title track is the most famous smooth-jazz instrumental to come out of the late-70s commercial wave. Critically contested — both critics noted it as evidence of the genre's middle-of-the-road tendencies — but historically inescapable.

**Al Jarreau — *Breakin' Away* (1981).** The vocal-jazz / R&B crossover record that won Jarreau two Grammys. Sits at the smooth-jazz / adult-contemporary border. Belongs in any expanded smooth-jazz survey but is more accurately a vocal-jazz record (and Jarreau will appear in the vocal-jazz file).

**Acoustic Alchemy — *Red Dust & Spanish Lace* (1987).** The Greg Carmichael / Nick Webb acoustic-guitar duo record that established their aesthetic. Closer to "new acoustic music" than to smooth jazz proper but the radio audience overlap was total.

**David Sanborn — *Voyeur* (1981).** Sanborn's solo Grammy-winner. Included in the top 10 via *Double Vision* but *Voyeur* deserves the standalone acknowledgment — "All I Need Is You" and "Wake Me When It's Over" are among his strongest solo performances.

**Wes Montgomery — *Down Here on the Ground* (1968).** The other late-period CTI / Verve record alongside *A Day in the Life*. The title track (the Lalo Schifrin composition from *Cool Hand Luke*) is one of Montgomery's most beautiful late performances. Lost to *A Day in the Life* on the grounds that the latter is more historically representative but both records are strong.

**Gato Barbieri — *Caliente!* (1976).** The Argentine tenor's Herb Alpert-produced commercial-fusion period. "Europa" — the Carlos Santana cover that became Barbieri's signature — is the kind of record that Scaruffi would normally dismiss but that has too much tonal personality to ignore. Sits at the Latin-jazz / smooth-jazz border.

**Bob James — *One* (1974).** The CTI debut where the template was first assembled. *Three* won the top-10 slot on the strength of "Nautilus" and the broader hip-hop sampling history but *One* (with "Feel Like Making Love" and "Nautilus"-precursor "In the Garden") is the foundational document.

**Notably absent.** Kenny G, Dave Koz, Najee, Boney James, Norman Brown, Russ Freeman & The Rippingtons, Peter White, Walter Beasley, and the rest of the late-80s / 90s commercial smooth-jazz wave. Both critics agreed that none of these records belong in any defensible top-10 list. The genre's commercial endpoint is not the place to look for its critical merit. The honest position is that the smooth-jazz canon peaks in the 1970s and early 1980s and declines thereafter into format-radio anonymity — what survives critical scrutiny is the material that retained jazz literacy underneath the gloss, not the material that perfected the gloss at jazz literacy's expense.

---

### Nu Jazz / Future Jazz
*Top 10 Albums of All Time — Scaruffi vs Fantano*
*Cross-listed with Volume 10 (Electronic); this is the anchor list.*

#### Debate Summary

Few sub-genres force these two critics into more uncomfortable proximity than Nu Jazz. Scaruffi's framework was largely set by 2000: he hears Cinematic Orchestra and Nils Petter Molvær as legitimate extensions of the ECM tradition through the dub-and-glitch era, treats St. Germain's *Tourist* as a clever lounge artifact rather than a milestone, and has limited patience for the post-2015 London revival, which he tends to view as competent retro-eclecticism rather than structural innovation. Fantano arrives from the opposite direction: he was eight years old when *Khmer* came out, weaned on Madlib, J Dilla, and Flying Lotus, and reads the entire field through the lens of jazz-as-rhythm-section-language meeting hip-hop and electronic production. He champions Robert Glasper, Makaya McCraven, Sons of Kemet, *Promises*, and the broader London/LA continuum with consistent enthusiasm.

The five-round debate produced sharper consensus than expected. Both sides agreed *Khmer*, *Every Day*, *Promises*, and *Black Radio* belong on any honest list — those four were locked by round two. Both sides also agreed Jaga Jazzist's *A Livingroom Hush* was a structural achievement on the Norwegian side that no later record has matched. From there the fights began. Scaruffi pushed hard for Bugge Wesseltoft's *New Conception of Jazz* as the foundational Norwegian text and for 4hero's *Two Pages* as the broken-beat masterpiece that most subsequent London work descends from. Fantano insisted that ignoring Makaya McCraven's *Universal Beings* and Sons of Kemet's *Your Queen Is a Reptile* would falsify the present tense of the genre, and that *We Out Here* (Shabaka-curated) deserves consideration as a scene document.

Three real fights: St. Germain's *Tourist* vs *Boulevard* (Scaruffi prefers the rougher 1995 record, Fantano the polished 2000); whether Kamasi Washington's *The Epic* belongs here or solely in spiritual jazz (Fantano: both; Scaruffi: neither, file under maximalist post-Coltrane); and whether GoGo Penguin or Floating Points & Pharoah Sanders better represents the British piano-trio-meets-electronics axis. Coin flips resolved two of these. The result is a list weighted slightly toward the modern London/LA axis (Fantano's home turf) but anchored by the late-90s European originators Scaruffi has tracked since they emerged.

#### The Top 10

##### 1. Nils Petter Molvær — *Khmer* (1997)

The record both critics named first, and the only one neither tried to dislodge in five rounds. *Khmer* is a Norwegian trumpeter's collision with Bristol-school dub, ambient electronics, and ECM's negative space — released on ECM itself, which gave the experiment institutional credibility it might otherwise have lacked. Molvær plays sparse, processed, breath-forward lines over programmed beats by Rune Arnesen and electronics by Reidar Skår and Ulf W. Ø. Holand, with Eivind Aarset on guitar and Roger Ludvigsen contributing additional textures. (Jan Bang, often associated with the broader Norwegian electronic-jazz scene and with Molvær's later live performances, is not on the original 1997 album.) The opening title track does in seven minutes what most of the genre spent the next decade trying to recover: it establishes that a horn player can occupy a downtempo electronic landscape without becoming smooth jazz, and that the ECM tradition of patient timbral attention can absorb hip-hop production logic without dilution.

Scaruffi has long treated *Khmer* as one of the few late-90s jazz fusions worth defending — he reads it as a continuation of the ambient-jazz line running through Jon Hassell rather than as a novelty. Fantano, who came to it later, hears it as the ur-text for everything from Bonobo's jazzier moments to Floating Points's collaboration with Pharoah Sanders. The album's structural insight — that programmed rhythm and acoustic improvisation can share a single timbral plane rather than alternating like a remix — became the technical premise of the entire genre. *Solid Ether* (2000) refines the formula but *Khmer* is where the door opens.

The record sold unexpectedly well in Europe, won the Norwegian Spellemannprisen, and made Molvær briefly the face of an international "future jazz" scene that ECM had not exactly intended to launch. Twenty-eight years later it remains the one undisputed canon entry, the album every subsequent attempt at this fusion has had to either build on or pretend not to have heard.

##### 2. The Cinematic Orchestra — *Every Day* (2002)

Jason Swinscoe's group consolidated everything implicit on *Motion* (1999) into a single-statement record. *Every Day* uses a small live ensemble (Tom Chant on saxes, Phil France on bass, Luke Flowers on drums, Patrick Carpenter on turntables) plus Swinscoe's production, and brings in vocalists Roots Manuva and Fontella Bass for two of the most arresting individual tracks of the decade. "All Things to All Men" with Roots Manuva is a hip-hop track that thinks like a Mingus arrangement; "All That You Give" with Bass — the Chess Records voice of "Rescue Me" — is a slow build that earns its eight minutes of accumulation.

Scaruffi rates Cinematic Orchestra higher than almost any of their British peers and treats *Every Day* as the genre's most successful single-album integration of jazz composition with electronic and hip-hop production: not a beats-with-horns project but actually composed music with through-arrangements, recapitulations, and weight. Fantano agrees the record is excellent and acknowledges its influence on the entire London scene that emerged a decade later, though he prefers *Ma Fleur* (2007) as an emotional experience.

The debate over which Cinematic Orchestra album to canonize was the cleanest in the entire process. *Every Day* won on structural ambition; *Ma Fleur* sits in honorable mentions. The record's instrumental tracks — "Burn Out," "Evolution," the title piece — work as compositions even without the guest vocalists, which is the test most beats-and-horns records fail. Two decades on, it is the British template against which every subsequent Brownswood and Gondwana record gets measured, usually unfavorably.

##### 3. Floating Points, Pharoah Sanders & the London Symphony Orchestra — *Promises* (2021)

A nine-movement, single-composition record made by an electronic producer (Sam Shepherd, the Floating Points project), an 80-year-old spiritual jazz patriarch (Sanders, in what proved to be his final major recording), and a string section. The piece is built on a seven-note motif Shepherd repeats throughout, with Sanders entering and departing across the movements and the LSO providing harmonic gradient. It is patient, devotional music — closer to Górecki or late Coltrane than to anything the Floating Points dance records would suggest.

Both critics rate *Promises* among the finest records of the 2020s in any genre. Scaruffi treats it as a successful late-style work for Sanders and a serious composition by Shepherd; Fantano gave it 9/10 — one of his higher 2021 scores — and has continued to cite it as the model for how electronic-jazz collaboration should work — a single sustained idea rather than a beats-and-features compilation. There was no debate about its inclusion.

What makes *Promises* belong to nu jazz rather than purely to spiritual jazz or contemporary classical is the composition's origin in Shepherd's electronic practice and the way the synthesizers and strings function as a single textural body. Sanders does not solo in the bebop sense; he places single tones inside a slowly evolving environment built by a producer raised on Detroit techno. The cross-generational, cross-discipline collaboration is the genre's founding premise executed at the highest level it has yet reached. Sanders died eighteen months after release, which has only deepened the record's standing.

##### 4. Robert Glasper Experiment — *Black Radio* (2012)

The record that broke Glasper out of the Blue Note straight-ahead lane and reframed the entire question of what a working jazz pianist's group could sound like in the 2010s. *Black Radio* is a hip-hop and R&B record built on jazz harmonic language and live rhythm-section interplay, with a guest list (Erykah Badu, Mos Def, Bilal, Lalah Hathaway, Ledisi, Lupe Fiasco, Common, Meshell Ndegeocello) that established Glasper as the connective tissue between late-Soulquarian neo-soul and the post-Dilla beat scene. Casey Benjamin's vocoder, Derrick Hodge's bass, and Chris Dave's drums are the engine; Glasper's chord voicings — those famous Herbie-Hancock-via-Dilla suspensions — are the harmonic signature.

**[FLAGGED: Fantano position extrapolated — no Needle Drop review of *Black Radio* located.]** What is on the record is consistent with Fantano's stated affinities — a working pianist's group reorganizing itself around hip-hop and neo-soul rhythm, with Chris Dave's drumming as the technical anchor — and the record has been a fixture in 2010s jazz-pivot conversations he participates in adjacent to. Scaruffi is more skeptical of the R&B framing and treats it as a smart crossover rather than a major statement, but conceded in round three that the record's influence on everything from Glasper's own *In My Element* descendants through the entire LA Brainfeeder scene is undeniable, and that Chris Dave's drumming is a genuine contribution to the language of jazz time.

The record won the 2013 Grammy for Best R&B Album — not Best Jazz Album — which Glasper has joked about and which neatly captures why it belongs here: it sits at the precise membrane this sub-genre is named for. The Lauryn Hill-ish "Cherish the Day" cover and the *Smells Like Teen Spirit* arrangement with Benjamin's vocoder are the obvious entry points; the instrumental "Black Radio" outro is the structural one.

##### 5. Makaya McCraven — *Universal Beings* (2018)

McCraven's method is the genre's most genuinely new idea since *Khmer*: record live improvising sessions in four cities (New York, Chicago, London, Los Angeles) with rotating local musicians, then take the multitrack tapes home and edit them as a hip-hop producer would, splicing, looping, re-pitching, and re-sequencing. *Universal Beings* is the first record where this post-production-as-composition approach delivers a complete artistic statement rather than a method demonstration.

The London side features Shabaka Hutchings, Nubya Garcia, Ashley Henry, and Tom Skinner — a snapshot of the British scene at the exact moment it was breaking internationally. The LA side has Jeff Parker, Miguel Atwood-Ferguson, and Carlos Niño. Each side has its own personality but McCraven's edit binds them into a single record. He calls himself a "beat scientist" rather than a drummer, which understates his actual playing — the source improvisations are excellent — and overstates the manipulation.

**[FLAGGED: Fantano position extrapolated — no Needle Drop review of *Universal Beings* located.]** The case for Fantano-favorable framing rests on his consistent enthusiasm for producer-as-author records and for the broader London/LA scene McCraven operates inside; the specific numeric grade is not on record. Scaruffi was initially skeptical of the producer-as-author framing but conceded in round four that the editing is genuinely compositional and that the cross-city structure is more than a marketing conceit. The record's place in the genre's top tier was settled in round four after Fantano argued — correctly — that no list of post-2015 nu jazz can credibly omit McCraven's central methodological innovation.

##### 6. Jaga Jazzist — *A Livingroom Hush* (2001)

The Norwegian collective's second album and the one both critics named when asked for the genre's most ambitious through-composed statement. Jaga is ten musicians: horns, vibes, guitars, electronics, two drummers. *A Livingroom Hush* is post-rock construction logic applied to jazz instrumentation, with electronics integrated at the arrangement level rather than overdubbed on top. Lars Horntveth, the band's main composer, was twenty when he wrote most of it, which is the kind of fact that ages well.

Scaruffi has consistently rated Jaga as the most genuinely compositional act in the European nu-jazz field and treats *A Livingroom Hush* as a small classic of post-Tortoise instrumental composition. Fantano agrees the record is excellent though he generally finds Jaga's later more-progressive direction (*One-Armed Bandit*, *Starfire*) less interesting than this one. The compromise was easy: this album, top six.

What it accomplishes that few peers do is make the electronics feel structural rather than decorative — the programmed elements are part of the harmonic and rhythmic material, not a coat of varnish on acoustic playing. The track "Animal Chin" is the obvious showcase but the record's deeper achievement is its sequencing as a single suite. Twenty-four years later it remains the European answer to *Every Day* — a different solution to the same problem of how a band can play this music live without it collapsing into either fusion-rock or chill-out.

##### 7. Sons of Kemet — *Your Queen Is a Reptile* (2018)

Shabaka Hutchings's tenor-saxophone-tuba-two-drummers quartet at peak focus, with each track named for a Black woman the album proposes as an alternative to the British monarchy (Harriet Tubman, Angela Davis, Doreen Lawrence, Yaa Asantewaa, Ada Eastman — Shabaka's great-grandmother — and others). Theon Cross's tuba functions as the bass, Tom Skinner and Eddie Hick share drum kit duties, Hutchings's tenor leads. The music draws on Caribbean carnival traditions, Afrobeat, dub, free jazz, and the Sun Ra/Pharoah Sanders strain of spiritual jazz.

Fantano reviewed *Your Queen Is a Reptile* favorably — a "light to decent 8" in his vocabulary, roughly an 80, one of his higher jazz scores of 2018 — and has cited it consistently as the breakthrough document of the London scene. Scaruffi is more reserved — he reads it as accomplished political jazz with strong rhythmic ideas rather than a structural innovation — but agreed in round three that any honest top ten of the last decade has to include either this or *We Out Here*, and this is the better record.

The Mercury Prize nomination, the international touring, and the subsequent dissolution of the band in 2022 all give *Your Queen Is a Reptile* the feel of a scene-defining peak. Hutchings's parallel projects (The Comet Is Coming, Shabaka and the Ancestors) are excellent in their own ways but this is the one where the political content, the rhythmic invention, and the recording quality align. Cross's tuba lines — particularly on "My Queen Is Harriet Tubman" and "My Queen Is Ada Eastman" — are the sort of bass playing that rewires what you think the instrument can do.

##### 8. St. Germain — *Tourist* (2000)

Ludovic Navarre's second album as St. Germain, released on Blue Note (the only French house producer on the label at the time), and the record that took the deep-house-meets-jazz formula of *Boulevard* (1995) and refined it into something that could play on the radio without losing its head. Live musicians — including Pascal Ohsé on trumpet, Edouard Labor on saxophones, and Idrissa Diop on percussion — improvise over Navarre's house and Latin-house production. Edmundo Ros's "Cuban Soul," John Lee Hooker's "Harlem Blues," and Marlena Shaw's "Woman of the Ghetto" all get sampled or quoted.

This was the genre's biggest commercial success — over four million copies sold worldwide — and the reason "nu jazz" briefly meant something to a general audience. Scaruffi prefers the rougher, less polished *Boulevard* and considers *Tourist* slick; Fantano was a child when it came out and has limited public commentary, but accepts the historical case. The coin flip in round four came down on *Tourist* over *Boulevard* on commercial-and-cultural impact grounds, though both editors register the result with mild regret about the rougher record's exclusion.

What the album does well is sustain a single mood — late-evening, urban, slightly nostalgic — across nine tracks without becoming background music. The live solos are short and well-placed. "Rose Rouge" and "Sure Thing" are the canonical singles. Twenty-five years later it sounds dated in obvious ways (the house production is very 2000) and modern in others (the live-band-over-loops template is now everywhere). It belongs on the list as the genre's commercial peak and as the record most non-jazz listeners would name first.

##### 9. 4hero — *Two Pages* (1998)

Marc Mac and Dego McFarlane's London production duo, originally drum-and-bass pioneers (their *Parallel Universe* from 1994 is a foundational jungle record), released *Two Pages* in 1998 as a double album that broke open the entire broken-beat-meets-orchestral-soul axis that subsequent London jazz drew on. The record uses live strings (arranged by Marc Mac), Brazilian percussion, vocal features (Ursula Rucker, Carol Crosby, Carina Andersson), and the duo's drum programming, organized as a song-based first disc and a more abstract instrumental second.

Scaruffi rates *Two Pages* very highly and treated it as the album most directly ancestral to the post-2015 London scene. He pushed in round two for it to sit in the top five; Fantano agreed it belongs but argued for the modern records to take the higher slots, since Glasper, McCraven, and Sons of Kemet have done more to extend the language than any subsequent 4hero record has. The compromise was top ten with high standing.

The record sits at the intersection of broken beat (which 4hero, Bugz in the Attic, and the West London scene around the Co-Op club essentially invented) and orchestral soul (which *Two Pages* arguably reintroduced into electronic music). The track "Star Chasers" is the obvious entry; "Loveless" with Carol Crosby and the strings on "Spirits in Transit" are the deeper case. Twenty-seven years later it remains the genre's most underrated structural document — a record that quietly shaped what London would sound like a decade and a half after it came out.

##### 10. Bugge Wesseltoft — *New Conception of Jazz* (1996)

The Norwegian pianist's debut as a leader and the record that, alongside *Khmer*, established that there was a Scandinavian school doing something distinct with jazz and electronics in the second half of the 1990s. Wesseltoft was already an experienced sideman (with Jan Garbarek among others) when he made this; the album's premise is straightforward — acoustic piano and Fender Rhodes over programmed beats and electronics — but the execution is more careful and harmonically rich than the genre's later commercial wave would suggest.

Scaruffi pushed hard for this album as the genre's foundational European text and rates it above several records that finished higher. Fantano has limited public material on Wesseltoft and accepted the case based on Scaruffi's argument and his own listen-through during the debate. The coin flip in round five was between *New Conception of Jazz* and Erik Truffaz's *Bending New Corners* (1999) for the final European slot; Wesseltoft won.

What the album does is establish a quieter, more harmonically sophisticated alternative to the dance-floor-leaning records that followed. It is closer in spirit to ECM than to St. Germain. The Jazzland label Wesseltoft founded to release it became one of the genre's central European homes, putting out subsequent records by Eivind Aarset, Sidsel Endresen, and Wesseltoft's own series. Top-ten standing on historical-foundational grounds; Scaruffi argued, persuasively, that excluding it would make the list unintelligible as a history of the genre.

#### Coin-Flip Resolutions

**St. Germain — *Tourist* (2000) vs *Boulevard* (1995).** Scaruffi argued the rougher 1995 record is more honest deep-house-meets-jazz; Fantano had no strong preference. Coin flip went to *Tourist* on cultural-impact grounds. *Boulevard* lands in honorable mentions.

**Bugge Wesseltoft — *New Conception of Jazz* (1996) vs Erik Truffaz — *Bending New Corners* (1999) for the final European slot.** Both have advocates (Scaruffi for both). Coin flip went to Wesseltoft on foundational-text grounds. Truffaz to honorable mentions.

**Kamasi Washington — *The Epic* (2015) was contested for inclusion at all.** Fantano wanted it in the top ten; Scaruffi argued for spiritual jazz only. Resolution: cross-list in spiritual jazz (Volume 2) and place in honorable mentions here, since the structural innovations of the LA Brainfeeder-adjacent scene are better represented by Glasper and McCraven on this list.

#### Honorable Mentions

- **The Cinematic Orchestra — *Ma Fleur* (2007):** the more emotional, song-based companion to *Every Day*. Patrick Watson's vocals on "To Build a Home" gave the band their best-known track. Lost the head-to-head to *Every Day* on structural grounds.
- **St. Germain — *Boulevard* (1995):** the rougher original, lost a coin flip to *Tourist*. Closer to deep house than its successor.
- **Erik Truffaz — *Bending New Corners* (1999):** the French trumpeter's hip-hop-jazz crossover with Nya on vocals. Lost a coin flip to Wesseltoft.
- **Kamasi Washington — *The Epic* (2015):** triple-album spiritual-jazz statement, primary slot in Volume 2's spiritual jazz list.
- **Nils Petter Molvær — *Solid Ether* (2000):** the polished follow-up to *Khmer*; some listeners prefer it.
- **GoGo Penguin — *v2.0* (2014):** the Manchester piano trio's Mercury-nominated breakthrough; closer to electronica-influenced acoustic jazz than to programmed nu jazz.
- **Shabaka and the Ancestors — *Wisdom of Elders* (2016):** Hutchings with a South African ensemble; cross-list candidate with spiritual jazz.
- **The Comet Is Coming — *Trust in the Lifeforce of the Deep Mystery* (2019):** Hutchings's electronic-leaning trio; Mercury nominee.
- **Yussef Kamaal — *Black Focus* (2016):** Yussef Dayes and Kamaal Williams's only album as a duo; the foundational document of the modern London piano-and-drums lineage.
- **Nubya Garcia — *Source* (2020):** the London tenor saxophonist's debut album; reggae, cumbia, and jazz in conversation.
- **Ezra Collective — *Where I'm Meant to Be* (2022):** Mercury Prize winner 2023; Afrobeat-leaning London quintet.
- **Christian Scott aTunde Adjuah — *Stretch Music* (2015):** the trumpeter's manifesto record for his post-genre concept.
- **Bonobo — *Black Sands* (2010):** the jazz-leaning peak of Simon Green's electronic project.
- **Quantic — *The 5th Exotic* (2001):** Will Holland's Tru Thoughts debut; downtempo with strong jazz and Latin samples.
- **Wax Tailor — *Tales of the Forgotten Melodies* (2005):** French abstract hip-hop with heavy jazz sampling.
- **Mark de Clive-Lowe — *Heritage* (2019):** the broken-beat veteran's Japanese-jazz exploration.
- **Soulive — *Doin' Something* (2001):** the Hammond-organ-trio update of soul jazz with hip-hop sensibilities.
- **Lettuce — *Mt. Crushmore* (2016):** New York funk-jazz collective, cousin to Soulive.
- **Floating Points — *Crush* (2019):** Shepherd's pre-*Promises* electronic record with strong jazz harmonic content.
- **Sons of Kemet — *Black to the Future* (2021):** the political-statement follow-up to *Your Queen Is a Reptile*.
- **Makaya McCraven — *In These Times* (2022):** McCraven's most through-composed record, with extended-form pieces.
- **Robert Glasper — *Black Radio 2* (2013) and *Black Radio III* (2022):** the series continued with diminishing-returns critical reception but remained influential.
- **Various Artists — *We Out Here* (2018):** Brownswood compilation curated by Shabaka Hutchings; the document of the London scene at its breakthrough moment, more sampler than album.

---

## Closing — From Jazz Forward

Seventeen sub-genre files, three parts, roughly one hundred and seventy album-length entries, a hundred years of recorded jazz from the Original Dixieland Jass Band's 1917 sides through the most recent London-revival and Chicago / International Anthem releases.
