The volume is more uneven than Volume 1 was, in ways the introduction tried to be honest about, and the closing should be honest about as well.

**Coin-flip totals.** Across the seventeen files, the debate process produced approximately fifty coin-flip resolutions — averaging two to three per file, with the bulk concentrated in the contested-canon files. The coin-flip-heavy files are Hard Bop (four flips, including the Wayne Shorter-anchor-album dispute, the Lee Morgan / Joe Henderson placement question, the Jackie McLean-into-modal boundary, and the *Speak No Evil* versus *JuJu* ordering), Free Jazz (four flips, mostly around the Coltrane late-period material that overlaps with Spiritual Jazz and the question of how much Cecil Taylor versus how much Ornette versus how much Ayler the file should weight), Modal Jazz (three flips, including the *Kind of Blue* placement debate and the Coltrane modal versus spiritual boundary), Fusion (three flips, mostly around the Miles electric-period internal hierarchy and the Weather Report album-of-record question), and Nu Jazz / Future Jazz (three flips, all in the contemporary tail where neither critic has had time to settle into stable positions). The coin-flip-light files are Dixieland (two flips, on a canon stable enough that the placements are largely settled before the debate begins), Smooth Jazz (one flip — the genre's canon is small enough and contested little enough by serious listeners that there is not much to flip on), and Third Stream (one flip — the smallest sub-genre in the volume by canonical density, with most placements arrived at by consensus).

**Where Scaruffi and Fantano were most honestly aligned.** Three places. (1) The avant-garde / free / spiritual-jazz wing. Both critics treat post-1965 Coltrane, Ornette, Cecil Taylor, Albert Ayler, Pharoah Sanders, Alice Coltrane, and the AACM-and-loft generation as one of the great twentieth-century artistic traditions. Scaruffi's coverage is more granular and his rankings are more developed, but Fantano's stated criteria — sonic singularity, harmonic adventurousness, raw emotional content, willingness to go long and to go strange — produce placements that map onto Scaruffi's almost cleanly. The Free Jazz and Spiritual Jazz files are the volume's most consensus-aligned major sub-genres. (2) The Coltrane lineage broadly — *A Love Supreme*, the late modal quartet records, the post-*Ascension* spiritual-and-free synthesis — is the place where Fantano's direct critical work goes deepest into pre-1990 jazz and where his and Scaruffi's positions are most stably aligned. The contemporary spiritual-jazz revival (Kamasi, Floating Points / Pharoah, the London scene) is the bridge that lets Fantano's modern coverage connect back into the older canon, and the file works that bridge explicitly. (3) The contemporary nu-jazz / London / Chicago revival. Both critics have direct, recent positions on this material; Scaruffi's recent updates to his jazz pages have covered the Shabaka Hutchings and Makaya McCraven catalogs in detail, and Fantano's reviews of the same material are some of his strongest jazz writing. The Nu Jazz / Future Jazz file is the volume's most contemporaneously-arrived-at and the placements there have the quality of working-critic agreement rather than reconstructed canon.

**Where Scaruffi's voice dominated by default.** The pre-bop sub-genres — Dixieland / New Orleans Jazz, Swing / Big Band, and to a lesser extent the early portions of Bebop — are the volume's most Scaruffi-weighted files. Fantano has essentially no on-record coverage of the 1917-1945 period. The Vocal Jazz / Standards file is similarly Scaruffi-dominant — Fantano's vocal-jazz reviews are concentrated on contemporary singer-songwriter-adjacent material (Cécile McLorin Salvant gets coverage, the Norah Jones / Diana Krall axis does not) and the canonical Ella / Holiday / Sinatra / Vaughan placements are constructed largely from Scaruffi's positions and from extrapolation of what Fantano's stated criteria would produce. The Smooth Jazz file is the volume's most editor-driven; both critics are structurally unsympathetic to the genre, and the file's argument for the form's artistic peaks is mostly the editor's against both critics' default positions, with the disagreement flagged. Reading those four files (Dixieland, Swing, Vocal Jazz, Smooth Jazz) with the appropriate weighting — heavier on Scaruffi's and the editor's voice, lighter on the extrapolated Fantano positions — is the right approach.

**Where the volume strains.** Three places, partly the same as Volume 1 strained and partly different. (1) The pre-1965 Fantano extrapolations, more acutely than the equivalent extrapolations in Volume 1. The roots traditions had the virtue that Fantano's contemporary coverage included a lot of records that drew on the older traditions (contemporary blues records, contemporary folk records, contemporary gospel records), which gave the extrapolations a contemporary anchor. Jazz has less of that — the contemporary revival is real but the contemporary records do not always read directly back onto the canonical pre-1965 material in the way the contemporary blues and folk material did. The Bebop, Cool Jazz, and Hard Bop files in particular have to lean on extrapolation more heavily than any single Volume 1 file did. (2) The cross-listing density. Modal jazz overlaps with hard bop, free jazz overlaps with spiritual jazz, soul jazz overlaps with hard bop and with smooth jazz on the late tail, fusion overlaps with funk and with rock, ECM overlaps with chamber and with European third-stream material, nu jazz overlaps with electronic music and with the spiritual-jazz revival. The cross-listing convention works but it is heavily exercised, and a different editor would have made some of the anchor decisions differently. (3) The contemporary tail. The most recent five years of jazz releases — the post-2020 London scene, the most recent Makaya McCraven and Jeff Parker, the Cécile McLorin Salvant late catalog, the most recent Shabaka Hutchings solo work — has not had time to settle into stable canonical positions, and the placements in those slots are the most provisional in the volume. The lists reserve the right to be revised.

**What's coming.** Volume 3 covers the parallel Black American tradition that overlaps with jazz at every era — Soul, R&B, and Funk. The overlap is structural, not coincidental. Hard bop and the early Stax / Hi soul records were emerging simultaneously in the mid-to-late 1950s, with shared sidemen and shared production personnel (Booker T. and the MGs were a Stax house band whose members had jazz backgrounds; the early Aretha Atlantic sessions used jazz musicians; the entire Memphis horn vocabulary that defines Stax is a hard-bop-derived horn vocabulary). Soul jazz literally fuses the two traditions — the Jimmy Smith / Cannonball Adderley / Lee Morgan groove records read as soul jazz to jazz audiences and as instrumental R&B to soul audiences, and they were charted on both formats. The 1970s jazz-funk wing (Donald Byrd's *Black Byrd*, Herbie Hancock's *Headhunters*, the entire CTI label's late catalog, the Roy Ayers Ubiquity records) is jazz musicians playing funk and funk musicians playing jazz, with the boundary increasingly negotiable. And the hip-hop sampling era from roughly 1989 forward (A Tribe Called Quest's heavy use of Ron Carter and the broader CTI-era sample base, the Pete Rock production catalog, the Madlib / J Dilla / Black Star generation's deep-jazz sample obsession) brings the jazz canon back into popular music's present through the sampling apparatus, in a way that complicates the question of what counts as a "jazz record" in the post-sample era. Volume 3 will work the boundary explicitly, with cross-listings into Volume 2 wherever the records anchor primarily as jazz but draw heavily on R&B / soul / funk vocabulary.

After Volume 3, the project continues outward through the genre map: Volume 4 covers Rock and the Punk and Metal subdivisions, Volume 5 covers Hip-Hop, Volume 6 covers Electronic and the Experimental / Avant-garde axis, Volume 7 covers Reggae and the Caribbean traditions, Volume 8 covers Classical, Volume 9 covers Pop and the lighter contemporary mainstream, Volume 10 covers Latin and World traditions, and Volume 11 closes with Soundtrack / Score and the Disco question. Some of those will end up combined or re-divided. The structure remains provisional past Volume 3.

**Why jazz is uniquely demanding to canon-build.** The form has seventeen distinct sub-traditions in this volume's draft and could plausibly support more (the editor considered separate files for jazz-funk, for the Brazilian wing, for the 1980s Marsalis-and-around neo-traditionalists, and for the Scandinavian post-jazz wing, and folded each into adjacent files for length reasons rather than because they were not distinct enough to deserve separate treatment). Each of the seventeen has its own internal canon — the bebop canon is not the cool canon is not the hard-bop canon is not the modal canon — and each is defended by serious listeners with serious investment in the form's distinctions. The ECM-jazz partisans and the AACM-loft-jazz partisans are arguing about which kind of post-free-jazz tradition matters more; the hard-bop traditionalists and the fusion traditionalists are arguing about whether the electric turn was a rupture or a betrayal; the smooth-jazz defenders (a small but real constituency) and the smooth-jazz dismissers (the consensus jazz-press position) are arguing about whether commercial accessibility is compatible with artistic seriousness. None of those arguments are settled. None of them are likely to settle.

That is, ultimately, why jazz needed its own volume rather than a section in an omnibus. Seventeen sub-traditions, each with its own defensible canon, each contested by listeners who have done the work — that is more taxonomic complexity than any other tradition this project will cover, by some margin. Rock will be larger by record count but more linear in its sub-genre relations. Hip-hop will be more contested by recent records but less internally subdivided. Classical music has the longest history but a more settled canonical apparatus. Jazz is the form whose sub-traditions are most numerous, most internally rigorous, and most actively defended at the same time, and a canon project that does it justice has to take all seventeen seriously rather than collapsing them into a smaller number of more manageable categories. The volume that results is uneven in places, Scaruffi-weighted in places, extrapolated in places, and (the editor hopes) honest about all three. Read it as such. Argue with it. The lists are an opening offer, the same as Volume 1's were, and they remain open to it.

The volume next door is Soul, R&B, and Funk. The traditions touch jazz at every era. The cross-references will run dense in both directions. Read forward.
